Allah's Perfection and Pre-existence
Allah's perfection, eternal attributes, and pre-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Allah is perfect in His essence, eternal in His attributes, and existed before space and time.

Core Idea
Allah's perfection and pre-existence are fundamental to Islamic theology.

This passage from Imam Nasafi's Bahr al-Kalam describes the absolute perfection and pre-eternity of Allah. It affirms that Allah is perfect in His essence, eternal in His attributes, and existed before all creation, including space and time. This establishes the foundation for understanding Allah's transcendence and independence from His creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and English translation to get the overall meaning.
2
Identify the key attributes
Notice the attributes: perfect in essence, eternal in attributes, transcendent above deficiency, Knower of the unseen, Victorious, Knower without confusion.
These attributes highlight Allah's uniqueness.
3
Understand the pre-existence statement
Focus on 'lam yazal kan' – He has never ceased to be before creating space and time.
This affirms Allah's priority over creation.
4
Reflect on the significance
Why are these attributes important? They distinguish Allah from creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو الكامل في ذاته، الأزلي بصفاته المنزَّه عن النقصان، العالم الغالب بلا نسيان، العالم بلا اشتباه، لم يزل كان قبل أن يخلقَ المكان وقبل أن يخلق الوقت والزمان.
And He is perfect in His essence, eternal in His attributes, transcendent above deficiency, the Knower of the unseen, the Victorious, without forgetfulness, the Knower without confusion. He has never ceased to be existing before He created space and before He created time and duration.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage continues the description of Allah. It states that Allah is perfect in His essence (kamil fi dhatihi) and eternal in His attributes (azali bi sifatihi), meaning His attributes are not acquired but intrinsic and eternal. He is transcendent above all deficiency (nughsan). He is 'alim al-ghalib' — the Knower of the unseen and the Victorious — without forgetfulness (nisyan) and without confusion (ishtibah). This emphasizes the absolute perfection of His knowledge. Finally, it affirms that Allah existed before the creation of space (makan) and time (waqt and zaman), establishing His pre-eternity and independence from creation.

Key Terms
كامل
أزلي
صفات
العالم الغالب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The phrase 'al-ghalib' means Allah is victorious in a human sense, implying occasional failure.
Correction: In this context, 'al-ghalib' refers to Allah's overwhelming knowledge of the unseen, not a transient victory.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, when did Allah exist?
A) After the creation of time
B) Before the creation of space and time
C) At the same time as the creation of the throne
D) Only after the creation of the universe

Key Takeaway
Allah is perfect and eternal, existing before all creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah is perfect in His essence and transcends all deficiency, why do you still allow doubts about His justice to creep into your heart when you face trials? What concrete step can you take this week to replace that doubt with conviction?
?
The passage says Allah existed before time and space. Does your daily schedule reflect that you are aware of His eternal presence, or do you live as if He is confined to your moments of worship? How will you change that starting today?
?
You claim Allah knows without confusion and forgetfulness. Yet, when you sin, do you truly believe He knows your struggle and still forgives? Or do you feel He overlooks you? Write one specific way you will trust His perfect knowledge in your repentance tonight.

Allah's Comprehensive Knowledge and Predestination
Imam Nasafi affirms Allah's comprehensive knowledge and absolute decree.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Islamic belief that Allah's knowledge encompasses all things—past, future, and counterfactuals—and that every event occurs solely by His will, decree, and predestination, while accepting His attributes as described in the Qur'an without modality.

Core Idea
Allah knows everything that happens and everything that could happen, and nothing occurs without His will and decree.

This passage from Imam Nasafi's Bahr al-Kalam lays the foundation for understanding divine predestination (qadar) in Sunni Islam. It affirms that Allah's knowledge is eternal and comprehensive, covering not only actual events but also hypothetical scenarios. Moreover, it declares that every single thing in creation happens through Allah's knowledge, will, desire, decree, and predestination. Finally, it reminds us to accept Allah's self-description without attempting to imagine a form or likeness.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once to get a feel for its rhythm. Even if you do not understand every word, notice the repeated emphasis on divine attributes.
The passage is short but packed with theological weight.
2
Understand the translation
Read the English translation carefully. Pay special attention to the counterfactual statement: 'what will not be, if it were to be, how it would be.' This is a distinct aspect of divine knowledge.
3
Analyze the attributes listed
Identify the five attributes mentioned: knowledge ('ilm), will (irada), desire (mashi'a), decree (taqdir), and predestination (qada'). Reflect on how each one contributes to Allah's absolute control over creation.
These are not redundant; each emphasizes a different facet of divine authority.
4
Grasp the final point on self-description
Notice the concluding phrase: 'without form' (min ghayri sura). This teaches that we affirm Allah's attributes exactly as He described Himself, without comparing Him to creation or asking 'how.'
This is the principle of tafwid—consigning the modality to Allah.
5
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to see if you can identify what Allah's knowledge includes according to the passage.
The answer is C, which includes both actual and counterfactual knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عَلِمَ ما يكون قبل أن يكون، وما لا يكون أن لو كان كيف يكون. قد سبق علمه في الأشياء قبل كونها، ولا يكون في ملكه شيء إلا هو بعلمه وإرادته ومشيئته وتقديره وقضائه، وهو كما وصف نفسه في كتابه من غير صورة، وكما عرف نفسه.
He knew what will be before it exists, and what will not be, if it were to be, how it would be. His knowledge preceded things before their existence, and there is nothing in His dominion except that it is by His knowledge, His will, His desire, His decree, and His predestination. And He is as He described Himself in His Book without form, and as He defined Himself.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage discusses Allah's knowledge and decree. It first affirms that Allah knows all future events before they occur, and also knows counterfactuals (what would happen if a certain non-occurring event were to occur). His knowledge predates all things. Then it states that nothing occurs in His dominion except by His knowledge ('ilm), will (irada), desire (mashi'a), decree (taqdir), and predestination (qada'). This is a strong affirmation of divine predestination. Finally, it concludes by stating that Allah is exactly as He described Himself in the Qur'an without form (min ghayri sura) and as He defined Himself — meaning we accept His attributes without delving into how (bila kayf) and without tashbih or ta'til.

Key Terms
علم
إرادة
مشيئة
تقدير
قضاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the strong predestinarian language negates human responsibility entirely.
Correction: This passage does not address human acquisition (kasb); it only affirms Allah's ultimate control. Sunni theology holds that humans have real choice and are accountable, while Allah's knowledge and decree encompass all things.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what does Allah's knowledge include?
A) Only past events
B) Only future events that will happen
C) Both what will happen and what would happen if something did not occur
D) Only general principles, not specifics

Key Takeaway
Allah's knowledge covers everything—actual and possible—and all events occur only by His will, decree, and predestination, while we accept His description without asking 'how'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah knows every possible outcome of your decisions—including the ones you never make—does that change how you approach your choices today? Will you consciously rely on His will in your next difficult decision, or do you still act as if the outcome depends entirely on you?
?
This passage states that nothing in Allah's dominion occurs except by His will, desire, decree, and predestination. Think of one specific event in your life that you attribute to your own effort. Can you honestly now attribute it to Allah's decree? What would change in your heart if you truly believed that?
?
You have just read that Allah is as He described Himself without form. Tomorrow, when you pray, will you let this truth reshape how you imagine Allah in your heart, or will you allow your mind to form a picture despite this warning? What step will you take to avoid that?

God's Oneness and Perfection in Nasafi's Essence
Nasafi affirms God's oneness, pre-eternality, and negation of imperfections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi affirms God's absolute oneness, perfection, and transcendence over space and time through a series of negations and positive attributes.

Core Idea
God is One, perfect, and existed before all creation—no opposite, no peer, no dependence on space or time.

This passage from Imam Nasafi's Bahr al-Kalam lays the foundation for Islamic theology by affirming God's unique oneness and perfection. It uses a series of negations and affirmations to clarify that God is unlike any created being. As you read, notice how each phrase builds a complete picture of divine transcendence and eternality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its English translation once to get the overall flow. Pay attention to both the negations (لَا, وَلَا) and affirmations (هُوَ, لَمْ يَزَلْ).
The passage is dense but every phrase carries theological weight.
2
Identify the negations
Find the phrases that deny anything comparable to God: 'لَا ضِدَّ لَهُ' (no opposite) and 'وَلَا نِدَّ لَهُ' (no peer). Understand that these negate any equal or rival in essence or attributes.
These establish God's absolute uniqueness (tawḥīd).
3
List the key affirmative attributes
Note the series of adjectives describing God: أَحَدًا (One), صَمَدًا (Self-Sufficient), فَرْدًا (Unique), وَتْرًا (Alone). Also note the phrases 'الكامل في ذاته' (perfect in essence) and 'الأزلي بصفاته' (eternal in attributes).
These affirm God's perfection and self-sufficiency.
4
Notice the negation of imperfection
See how the text says 'المنزَّه عن النقصان' (transcendent above imperfection) and gives examples: 'بلا نسيان' (without forgetfulness) and 'بلا اشتباه' (without confusion).
This underscores that God's knowledge is absolute.
5
Reflect on God's priority over creation
The final sentence asserts that God existed before creating place (المكان) and time (الوقت والزمان). This shows that God is not conditioned by space or time.
This refutes any notion that God is subject to created dimensions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
له، ولا ضِدَّ له، ولا ندَّ له، لم يزل أحدًا صمدًا، فردّا، وترا، ولا يزال كذلك أبدًا، وهو الكامل في ذاته، الأزلي بصفاته المنزَّه عن النقصان، العالم الغالب بلا نسيان، العالم بلا اشتباه، لم يزل كان قبل أن يخلقَ المكان وقبل أن يخلق الوقت والزمان.
He has no opposite and no peer. He has always been One, Self-Sufficient, Unique, Alone, and He will always be so forever. He is perfect in His essence, eternal in His attributes, transcendent above imperfection, the All-Knowing, the Dominant without forgetfulness, the All-Knowing without confusion. He has always been before He created place and before He created time and duration.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage is a definition and affirmation of God's oneness and perfection. It begins by negating any opposite (ḍidd) or peer (nidd) for God, establishing His uniqueness. Then it affirms His eternal attributes: He has always been (lam yazil) 'one' (aḥad), 'self-sufficient' (ṣamad), 'unique' (fard), 'alone' (watr), and will remain so forever. Next, it describes God as perfect in His essence (kāmil fī dhātih), eternal in His attributes (azalī bi-ṣifātih), free from any deficiency (munaẓẓah ‘an al-nuqṣān). Then it mentions two specific attributes: 'All-Knowing, the Dominant' (al-‘ālim al-ghālib) without forgetfulness, and 'All-Knowing' without confusion. Finally, it asserts God's priority over creation: He existed before creating place (makān) and before creating time and duration (al-waqt wa al-zamān), emphasizing that He is not conditioned by space or time.

Key Terms
ضِدّ
نِدّ
صمد
فرد
وتر
كامل
أزلي
منزَّه
العالم الغالب
المكان
الوقت
الزمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that saying 'no opposite' only means God has no rival, not that He has no equal in any quality.
Correction: The negation of opposite (ḍidd) and peer (nidd) goes beyond mere rivalry; it denies any being that could be comparable in any aspect of essence or attributes.

Checkpoint
What does the author affirm about God's existence before creation?
A) God began at the creation of place
B) God existed before place and time
C) God is subject to time
D) God created time first

Key Takeaway
God is one, without any equal or opposite, perfect in His essence and attributes, and existed before all creation, including space and time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'God has no opposite,' does your heart truly feel that nothing—not your fears, desires, or idols—stands as an equal to Him in your life? What specific attachment today acts as a hidden 'opposite' to His sole sovereignty over your decisions?
?
The passage says God is 'perfect in His essence, eternal in His attributes.' In what area of your life are you still expecting perfection from yourself or others, forgetting that only He is perfect? How can you repent from that unrealistic expectation today?
?
God existed before creating place and time. Do you live as though your circumstances or timeline define who you are? Name one worry about the future that reveals you are trusting time more than the Timeless One, and replace it with a specific act of tawakkul right now.

Allah's Foreknowledge and Counterfactual Knowledge
Nasafi affirms God's foreknowledge and knowledge of counterfactuals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Allah's knowledge encompasses both what will occur and what would have occurred under different circumstances.

Core Idea
Allah knows everything — actual and hypothetical — before it happens.

Imam Nasafi begins by affirming the absolute scope of divine knowledge. He not only states that Allah knows everything that will happen before it occurs, but also that He knows the full nature of events that never come to pass. This challenges any limited view of God's omniscience.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the two distinct clauses about knowledge.
2
Identify the two aspects of knowledge
The passage presents two claims: (1) Allah knows future events before they exist, and (2) Allah knows how non-occurring events would have been. Find each in the text.
The second claim is about counterfactuals.
3
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself why counterfactual knowledge matters. Think about how this guards against limiting God's knowledge to what actually happens.
This relates to the misconception that God only knows actual events.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عَلِمَ ما يكون قبل أن يكون، وما لا يكون أن لو كان كيف يكون. قد سبق علمه في الأشياء قبل كونها، ولا يكون في ملكه شيء إلا هو بعلمه وإرادته ومشيئته وتقديره وقضائه.
He knew what will be before it is, and what will not be, how it would be if it were. His knowledge preceded all things before their existence, and nothing occurs in His dominion except by His knowledge, His will, His wish, His decree, and His determination.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage is a proof of God's omniscience and predestination. First, it states that God knows what will happen before it occurs (mā yakūn qabla an yakūn), and also knows what will not happen—but if it were to happen, He knows how it would be (mā lā yakūn an law kāna kayfa yakūn). This addresses hypotheticals, counterfactuals, and conditions. Then it asserts that God's knowledge preceded all things before their coming into existence (sabaqa ‘ilmuhu fī al-ashyā’ qabla kawnihā). Finally, it declares that nothing occurs in God's dominion except by His knowledge, will (irāda), wish (mashī‘a), decree (taqdīr), and determination (qaḍā’). This refutes the Qadariyya (who deny predestination) and affirms Ash'ari/Maturidi doctrine of divine decree.

Key Terms
علم
سبق العلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God only knows actual events, not hypotheticals.
Correction: The text explicitly affirms that God knows how things would be if they occurred, even if they never do.

Checkpoint
According to the text, what does God know about things that never happen?
A) He does not know them because they never occur
B) He knows how they would be if they occurred
C) He knows them only after they could have happened
D) He has no knowledge of unrealized events

Key Takeaway
Allah's knowledge includes all actual events and all possible outcomes of unrealized scenarios.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah already knows every sin you will commit before you commit it, how does that affect the urgency and sincerity of your repentance? Will you truly turn back to Him today, knowing He saw your failure before it happened?
?
Think of a decision you regret—a path not taken. Allah knows exactly how that life would have been. Does this knowledge humble you or comfort you? Can you accept that His knowledge is better than your fantasy, and let go of that regret?
?
Before you were born, Allah's knowledge encompassed every detail of your life. Does that make you feel watched or cared for? Write down one area where you need to live more consciously of His all-encompassing knowledge, and commit to a specific change today.

Allah's Decree: All Things Occur by His Will
Nasafi enumerates the divine attributes involved in every occurrence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that nothing occurs in Allah's dominion except by His knowledge, will, wish, decree, and determination.

Core Idea
Every event is under Allah's comprehensive will and decree.

Having established the breadth of divine knowledge, Nasafi now connects that knowledge to divine action. He asserts that every occurrence in creation is directly tied to Allah's will and determination. This teaching refutes any belief in independent causality or events outside God's control.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic sentence and its translation. It is a single comprehensive statement listing five attributes.
The sentence begins with 'wa lā yakūnu' (and nothing occurs).
2
Note each attribute listed
Identify the five terms: knowledge, will, wish, decree, determination. Understand that they are all involved in every event.
The order is: 'ilm, irāda, mashī'a, taqdīr, qaḍā'.
3
Understand the combined implication
Ask yourself why all five are mentioned together. This emphasizes that nothing happens outside God's full control and plan.
This refutes the Qadariyya who claimed human independence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عَلِمَ ما يكون قبل أن يكون، وما لا يكون أن لو كان كيف يكون. قد سبق علمه في الأشياء قبل كونها، ولا يكون في ملكه شيء إلا هو بعلمه وإرادته ومشيئته وتقديره وقضائه.
He knew what will be before it is, and what will not be, how it would be if it were. His knowledge preceded all things before their existence, and nothing occurs in His dominion except by His knowledge, His will, His wish, His decree, and His determination.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage is a proof of God's omniscience and predestination. First, it states that God knows what will happen before it occurs (mā yakūn qabla an yakūn), and also knows what will not happen—but if it were to happen, He knows how it would be (mā lā yakūn an law kāna kayfa yakūn). This addresses hypotheticals, counterfactuals, and conditions. Then it asserts that God's knowledge preceded all things before their coming into existence (sabaqa ‘ilmuhu fī al-ashyā’ qabla kawnihā). Finally, it declares that nothing occurs in God's dominion except by His knowledge, will (irāda), wish (mashī‘a), decree (taqdīr), and determination (qaḍā’). This refutes the Qadariyya (who deny predestination) and affirms Ash'ari/Maturidi doctrine of divine decree.

Key Terms
قضاء
قدر
إرادة
مشيئة
تقدير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that human actions are independent of God's will.
Correction: The text asserts that every event, including human actions, occurs only by God's will and decree.

Checkpoint
According to the text, through how many attributes does every occurrence happen?
A) three
B) four
C) five
D) six

Key Takeaway
Every event in creation is a direct result of Allah's knowledge, will, wish, decree, and determination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that every single event in your life occurs only by Allah's knowledge, will, wish, determination, and decree, how does that change the way you view your biggest regret? Will you now see it as part of a divine plan rather than a mistake?
?
When you wake up tomorrow and make a choice, do you feel you are acting independently? How can you remind yourself, in that moment, that your decision is also under Allah's will? What practical step will you take to embed this belief into your daily consciousness?
?
If nothing happens outside of Allah's decree, then your failures and trials are also from Him. Will you now accept them with patience and gratitude, knowing they are part of His perfect plan? What can you do today to cultivate that acceptance?

The First Two Refutations: Huwa and Ahad
Nasafi explains the refutational force of 'Huwa' and 'Ahad' against four deviant groups.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the words 'Huwa' and 'Ahad' in Surah al-Ikhlas refute those who deny God's existence or attributes and those who associate partners with Him.

Core Idea
The first two divine names in the surah dismantle atheism and polytheism in one breath.

Nasafi opens his tafsir by showing that Surah al-Ikhlas is a comprehensive refutation of false beliefs. He explains that 'Huwa' points to the existent God, refuting those who deny His attributes (Mu'aṭṭila) and those who claim esoteric knowledge (Batiniyya). Then 'Ahad' affirms absolute oneness, refuting polytheists and dualists. This lesson focuses on these first two refutations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation, noting how each word is linked to a specific group.
2
Identify the two words and their refutations
'Huwa' refutes Mu'aṭṭila and Batiniyya; 'Ahad' refutes polytheists and dualists. Understand what each group believes.
Mu'aṭṭila deny attributes; Batiniyya claim hidden meanings; polytheists worship others; dualists believe in two gods.
3
Reflect on the significance
Why does Nasafi emphasize that the surah is not just a statement but a naqḍ (refutation)?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو كما وصف نفسه في كتابه من غير صورة، وكما عرف نفسه، من غير رؤية وإحاطة، فقال جل جلاله لرسوله : قُلْ هُوَ اللَّهُ أَحَدٌ ﴾ إلي آخر السورة. و هُوَ ) إشارة إلى الموجود، ونقضّ على المعطلة والباطنية، (أحدٌ ) إثبات وحدته، ونقضّ على المشركين والثنوية، اللهُ الصَّمَدُ ) [الاخلاص : ٢] نقض على المشبهة لَمْ يَلِدْ وَلَمْ يُولَدٌ ﴾ [الإخلاص ۳] نقض علي اليهود والنصارى وَلَمْ يَكُن لَّهُ : كُفُوًا أَحَدٌ ﴾ نقضّ على المجوس بقولهم : يَزْدَان) وأَهْرَمَن» كما قال الله تعالي: لَيْسَ كَمِثْلِهِ شَيْءٌ وَهُوَ السَّمِيعُ الْبَصِيرُ ﴾ [الشورى: ١١].
He is as He described Himself in His Book without form, and as He defined Himself without vision or comprehension. So He, majestic is His glory, said to His Messenger: 'Say: He is Allah, the One' to the end of the surah. 'Huwa' is a pointer to the Existent, and a refutation of the Mu'aṭṭila and Batiniyya. 'Ahad' affirms His unity and refutes the polytheists and dualists. 'Allah al-Ṣamad' refutes the Mushabbiha. 'Lam yalid wa lam yūlad' refutes the Jews and Christians. 'Wa lam yakun lahu kufuwan ahad' refutes the Magians with their saying Yazdan and Ahriman. As Allah said: 'There is nothing like Him, and He is the All-Hearing, the All-Seeing.'
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory tafsir of Surah al-Ikhlas, showing how each verse serves as a refutation (naqḍ) of a specific deviant group. First, it sets the context: God is as He described Himself without form (ṣūra) and without being seen or fully comprehended (rū’ya wa iḥāṭa). Then it cites the surah and breaks it down. 'Huwa' points to the existent (al-mawjūd) and refutes the Mu'aṭṭila (those who deny God's attributes) and Batiniyya (esotericists). 'Ahad' affirms God's unity and refutes polytheists (mushrikūn) and dualists (thanawiyya). 'Allah al-Ṣamad' refutes the Mushabbiha (anthropomorphists). 'Lam yalid wa lam yūlad' refutes the Jews (who claim Ezra is son of God) and Christians (who believe in Jesus as son). 'Wa lam yakun lahu kufuwan ahad' refutes the Magians (Zoroastrians) who believe in two principles: Yazdan (good) and Ahriman (evil). The author concludes with a supporting verse from Quran 42:11: 'There is nothing like Him...'

Key Terms
هو
أحد
نقض
معطلة
باطنية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Surah al-Ikhlas only declares monotheism in a general sense.
Correction: Nasafi shows that each verse refutes specific historical and theological errors, making it a precise polemical tool.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, which two groups does the word 'Huwa' refute?
A) Mushabbiha and Jews
B) Mu'aṭṭila and Batiniyya
C) Polytheists and Dualists
D) Magians and Christians

Key Takeaway
The first two words of Surah al-Ikhlas, 'Huwa' and 'Ahad', refute four major deviant groups: those who deny God's attributes, esotericists, polytheists, and dualists.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'Qul Huwa Allahu Ahad', do you consciously realize that the first word 'Huwa' is a direct refutation of those who deny God's existence or His attributes? How can you use this knowledge to strengthen your own conviction against doubts? Commit to reciting this surah with this awareness for the next week.
?
The Batiniyya claimed hidden meanings that nullified the apparent message. Are there areas in your life where you twist clear teachings to suit your desires, rather than accepting them as they are? What is one teaching you have been avoiding obeying literally?
?
The polytheists and dualists deny God's exclusive oneness. In what subtle ways do you associate partners with God, such as seeking approval from people or relying on your own strength? How will you combat that this week?

Refuting Anthropomorphism and Divine Sonship: Al-Samad and Lam Yalid
Nasafi explains the refutation of anthropomorphists and those who assert divine sonship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the verses 'Allah al-Samad' and 'Lam yalid wa lam yūlad' refute anthropomorphists and those who attribute offspring to God.

Core Idea
God's self-sufficiency and not begetting or being begotten refute both anthropomorphism and the idea of a divine son.

In the second part of his tafsir, Nasafi explains how the next two verses of Surah al-Ikhlas refute two additional groups: the Mushabbiha who liken God to creation, and the Jews and Christians who claim God has offspring. The verse 'Allah al-Samad' affirms God's eternal self-sufficiency, while 'Lam yalid wa lam yūlad' denies any begetting.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for this lesson
Read the Arabic and translation, focusing on 'Allah al-Samad' and 'Lam yalid wa lam yūlad'.
2
Understand 'al-Samad'
The word 'al-Samad' means the eternal, self-sufficient being, which refutes the Mushabbiha who compare God to His creation.
3
Understand 'lam yalid wa lam yūlad'
This verse denies that God begets or is begotten, refuting the Jewish and Christian claims of divine offspring.
Jews claimed Ezra is son of God; Christians claimed Jesus is son.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو كما وصف نفسه في كتابه من غير صورة، وكما عرف نفسه، من غير رؤية وإحاطة، فقال جل جلاله لرسوله : قُلْ هُوَ اللَّهُ أَحَدٌ ﴾ إلي آخر السورة. و هُوَ ) إشارة إلى الموجود، ونقضّ على المعطلة والباطنية، (أحدٌ ) إثبات وحدته، ونقضّ على المشركين والثنوية، اللهُ الصَّمَدُ ) [الاخلاص : ٢] نقض على المشبهة لَمْ يَلِدْ وَلَمْ يُولَدٌ ﴾ [الإخلاص ۳] نقض علي اليهود والنصارى وَلَمْ يَكُن لَّهُ : كُفُوًا أَحَدٌ ﴾ نقضّ على المجوس بقولهم : يَزْدَان) وأَهْرَمَن» كما قال الله تعالي: لَيْسَ كَمِثْلِهِ شَيْءٌ وَهُوَ السَّمِيعُ الْبَصِيرُ ﴾ [الشورى: ١١].
He is as He described Himself in His Book without form, and as He defined Himself without vision or comprehension. So He, majestic is His glory, said to His Messenger: 'Say: He is Allah, the One' to the end of the surah. 'Huwa' is a pointer to the Existent, and a refutation of the Mu'aṭṭila and Batiniyya. 'Ahad' affirms His unity and refutes the polytheists and dualists. 'Allah al-Ṣamad' refutes the Mushabbiha. 'Lam yalid wa lam yūlad' refutes the Jews and Christians. 'Wa lam yakun lahu kufuwan ahad' refutes the Magians with their saying Yazdan and Ahriman. As Allah said: 'There is nothing like Him, and He is the All-Hearing, the All-Seeing.'
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory tafsir of Surah al-Ikhlas, showing how each verse serves as a refutation (naqḍ) of a specific deviant group. First, it sets the context: God is as He described Himself without form (ṣūra) and without being seen or fully comprehended (rū’ya wa iḥāṭa). Then it cites the surah and breaks it down. 'Huwa' points to the existent (al-mawjūd) and refutes the Mu'aṭṭila (those who deny God's attributes) and Batiniyya (esotericists). 'Ahad' affirms God's unity and refutes polytheists (mushrikūn) and dualists (thanawiyya). 'Allah al-Ṣamad' refutes the Mushabbiha (anthropomorphists). 'Lam yalid wa lam yūlad' refutes the Jews (who claim Ezra is son of God) and Christians (who believe in Jesus as son). 'Wa lam yakun lahu kufuwan ahad' refutes the Magians (Zoroastrians) who believe in two principles: Yazdan (good) and Ahriman (evil). The author concludes with a supporting verse from Quran 42:11: 'There is nothing like Him...'

لم يلد
لم يولد
مشبهة
يهود
نصارى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'al-Samad' only means 'the eternal' without realizing its refutational context.
Correction: Nasafi explicitly states that it is a refutation of the Mushabbiha, who liken God to creation.

Checkpoint
Which group does 'Allah al-Samad' refute according to Nasafi?
A) Mu'aṭṭila
B) Mushabbiha
C) Polytheists
D) Magians

Key Takeaway
The verses 'Allah al-Samad' and 'Lam yalid wa lam yūlad' refute anthropomorphism and the belief that God has offspring.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to believe that Allah is al-Samad, self-sufficient and independent. In what area of your life do you still seek fulfillment from people or possessions rather than from Him alone? What concrete step will you take today to redirect that need to Allah?
?
The verse 'lam yalid' refutes the idea that God has a son. Yet do you ever, in your thoughts or speech, indirectly associate anything with Allah as though it shares His uniqueness? How will you guard your heart and tongue against subtle shirk from today onward?
?
Knowing that al-Samad means Allah is in no need of you, while you are in absolute need of Him, how does this truth reshape your prayer life starting this very night? Will you stand before Him with greater humility and hope?

Refuting Dualism: The Final Verse Against the Magians
Nasafi refutes Zoroastrian dualism using the last verse and cites a supporting Quranic verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the last verse of Surah al-Ikhlas refutes the dualist belief in two divine principles and how it connects to the Quranic principle of incomparability.

Core Idea
The verse 'Wa lam yakun lahu kufuwan ahad' denies any equal or opposite to God, refuting Zoroastrian dualism.

Nasafi concludes his tafsir of Surah al-Ikhlas by explaining how the final verse refutes the Magians (Zoroastrians) who believe in two gods: Yazdan (good) and Ahriman (evil). The verse denies that God has any equal or peer. He then supports this with Quran 42:11, which affirms that nothing is like Him.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the last verse and the concluding quote.
2
Understand the Magian belief
The Magians (majus) believed in two eternal principles: Yazdan (light/good) and Ahriman (darkness/evil).
3
Connect the refutation
The verse 'wa lam yakun lahu kufuwan ahad' denies that God has any equal or counterpart, thus refuting dualism.
The word kufuwan means equal or peer.
4
The concluding verse
Nasafi cites 'Laysa kamithlihi shay'' to reinforce that God is incomparable.

Source Text

?

?

?
Read the verse again: 'There is nothing like Him, and He is the All-Hearing, the All-Seeing.' Does your heart accept that no person, force, or circumstance can ever rival His authority? Repent for the moments you have given that authority to something else.

The Sunni Definition of Knowledge
Definition of knowledge by Ahl al-Sunna with distinction between human and divine.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni definition of knowledge and how it differs for created beings and Allah.

Core Idea
Human knowledge is cognition; divine knowledge is encompassing and eternal.

This passage begins with a question about the definition of knowledge. Ahl al-Sunna provides a precise answer that distinguishes between human and divine knowledge, grounding it in a Quranic verse.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic passage where Ahl al-Sunna defines knowledge as 'cognition of the known as it is' for created beings, and as 'encompassing and acquaintance' for Allah.
Notice the Arabic terms used: معرفة for human, إحاطة and خبر for divine.
2
Understand the distinction
Reflect on why Allah is not described as having 'ma'rifa' (cognition). The reason given is that He has always been knowing, so His knowledge is not acquired.
This distinction prevents attributing change or acquisition to Allah.
3
Connect to the Quranic evidence
The author cites verse 18:91 as evidence for divine encompassing knowledge. Read the translation and see how it supports the definition.
The verse uses the same root (أحطنا) as the term 'ihata'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : ما حد العلم؟ قال أهل السنة والجماعة: معرفة المعلوم على ما هو به، وهو علم المخلوقين. وعلم الله تعالى الإحاطة والخبر على ما هو به، لأنه لا يوصف بالمعرفة، لأنه لم يزل عالماً لما بيناً. قال الله تعالى: ﴿وَقَدْ أَحَطَنَا بِمَا لَدَيْهِ خُبْرًا ﴾ [الكهف: ٩١]. وقالت المعتزلة : حدَّ العلم معرفة الشيء على ما هو به. وهذا باطل، لأنَّ المعدوم ليس بشيء، ولا يقع عليه اسم الشيء، لأنَّ الله تعالى خلق الأشياء لا من شيء، وقالت المعتزلة: خلق الأشياء بقوله: كُن فَيَكُونُ ﴾ [يس: ۸۲]. وعندنا بالصنع لا بالقول، فلو قلنا: إنه معرفة الشيء على ما هو به، يؤدي إلى قِدَم الأعيان مع الله تعالى، وذلك مذهبُ الدَّهرية الكفرة الفَجَرة؛ لأن عندهم العالم قديم، والله تعالى عالم بعلمه، والعلم من صفاته الأزلية، بخلاف ما قالت المعتزلة: إنَّ ذاته علمه، والله تعالى عالم بذاته على ما ذكرنا . وعندنا هو عالم بعلمه، والعِلْمُ من صفاته الأزلية، علم ما يكون قبل أن يكون، وما لا يكون أن لو كان كيف يكون قد سبق علمه الأشياء قبل كونها قال الله تعالى: ﴿قل لا يَعْلَمُ مَن فِي السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ الْغَيْبَ إِلَّا اللهُ ﴾ [النمل: ٦٥]. وقالت المعتزلة والرافضة والقدرية : إنه لا يعلم الشيء ما لم يخلقه ويوجده.
If it is said: What is the definition of knowledge? Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a said: Knowledge is the cognition of the known as it is, and that is the knowledge of created beings. And knowledge of Allah Almighty is encompassing and familiarity of what is as it is, because He is not described by cognition, for He has always been knowing through what we have clarified. Allah Almighty said: 'We have encompassed all that he had with knowledge' [18:91]. And the Mu'tazila said: The definition of knowledge is knowing a thing as it is. This is false, because non-existence is not a thing, and the name 'thing' does not apply to it, because Allah Almighty created things from nothing, and the Mu'tazila said: He created things by His word 'Be, and it is' [36:82]. According to us, it is by act, not by word. For if we said that it is knowing a thing as it is, it would lead to the eternity of entities with Allah Almighty, and that is the doctrine of the Dahriyya, the disbelieving sinners, because according to them the world is eternal, and Allah Almighty is Knowing by His knowledge, and knowledge is among His eternal attributes, contrary to what the Mu'tazila said: that His essence is His knowledge, and Allah Almighty is Knowing by His essence according to what we mentioned. And according to us, He is Knowing by His knowledge, and knowledge is among His eternal attributes. He knows what will be before it is, and what would not be if it were to be, how it would be; His knowledge preceded all things before their existence. Allah Almighty said: 'Say: None in the heavens and the earth knows the unseen except Allah' [27:65]. And the Mu'tazila, the Rafida, and the Qadariyya said: He does not know a thing until He creates it and brings it into existence.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage is a definition and refutation. It begins with the question: 'What is the definition of knowledge?' The author gives the Sunni definition: knowledge is cognition (ma'rifa) of the known as it is, which applies to created beings. For Allah, knowledge is encompassing (ihata) and acquaintance (khabar) of things as they are, because He is not described by the term 'cognition' (ma'rifa) since He has always been knowing. A Quranic verse is cited. Then the Mu'tazila definition is given: knowledge is knowing a thing as it is. The author refutes it by arguing that non-existents are not things, and if knowledge were simply knowing a thing as it is, it would imply that non-existents have some reality, leading to the eternity of entities alongside God—the doctrine of the Dahriyya materialists. The author then contrasts the Sunni view: God knows by His knowledge, which is an eternal attribute, not by His essence as the Mu'tazila claim. This knowledge encompasses all things before they exist, supported by another verse. Finally, the author mentions the false view of the Mu'tazila, Rawafid, and Qadariyya that God does not know a thing until He creates it, which is refuted by the preceding affirmation of eternal knowledge.

Key Terms
العلم
المعرفة
المعلوم
الإحاطة
الخبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is Allah not described as having 'ma'rifa'?
A) Because He does not know things as they are
B) Because He has always been knowing, so His knowledge is not acquired
C) Because His knowledge is created
D) Because He only knows generalities

Key Takeaway
Ahl al-Sunna defines human knowledge as cognition of the known as it is, and divine knowledge as eternal encompassing and acquaintance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own knowledge, do you ever feel proud of what you have acquired? But this lesson says human knowledge is merely cognition — finite and dependent. How does that humble you right now, and what can you do to keep that humility alive in your daily life?
?
You claim to believe that Allah's knowledge encompasses everything, even your hidden thoughts and future actions. Yet, do you live as if that is true? When you sin in secret, do you forget that His knowledge is an encompassing presence? What one step will you take today to act with the awareness that Allah already knows?
?
The passage says Allah is not described with 'ma'rifa' because He has always been knowing. This means nothing escapes His eternal gaze. Is there any area of your life where you are trying to hide from that reality? What would it mean for you to stop hiding and instead surrender to the One whose knowledge never began and never ends?

Refutation of the Mu'tazila on Knowledge and Affirmation of Divine Foreknowledge
Refutation of Mu'tazila definition and affirmation of eternal divine knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazila definition of knowledge is rejected and how the Sunni position affirms God's eternal knowledge of all things.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila definition implies non-existents are real things, leading to the heresy of eternal entities.

The author now refutes the Mu'tazila definition of knowledge by exposing its dangerous implication: that non-existents would be considered 'things', leading to the belief in eternal entities alongside Allah. He then affirms the Sunni doctrine of eternal divine knowledge and refutes those who deny pre-knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila definition and its refutation
Read how the Mu'tazila define knowledge as 'knowing a thing as it is'. Then see the refutation: non-existents are not things, so this definition falsely implies they are.
The key point is that 'thing' (شيء) only applies to existent entities.
2
Understand the heretical consequence
The author argues that if knowledge is knowing a thing as it is, then non-existent things would have a reality, leading to the eternity of entities alongside God—the belief of the Dahriyya.
The Dahriyya are materialists who believe the world is eternal.
3
Absorb the Sunni position on divine knowledge
The author contrasts the Sunni view: Allah is Knowing by His knowledge, which is an eternal attribute, not by His essence as the Mu'tazila claim. This knowledge encompasses all things before they exist.
This affirms God's foreknowledge of all things, including what will be and what would have been.
4
Note the sects that deny pre-knowledge
The passage concludes by naming the Mu'tazila, Rafida, and Qadariyya as those who say God does not know a thing until He creates it—a view refuted by the Quranic verse.
This verse (27:65) affirms that none knows the unseen except Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : ما حد العلم؟ قال أهل السنة والجماعة: معرفة المعلوم على ما هو به، وهو علم المخلوقين. وعلم الله تعالى الإحاطة والخبر على ما هو به، لأنه لا يوصف بالمعرفة، لأنه لم يزل عالماً لما بيناً. قال الله تعالى: ﴿وَقَدْ أَحَطَنَا بِمَا لَدَيْهِ خُبْرًا ﴾ [الكهف: ٩١]. وقالت المعتزلة : حدَّ العلم معرفة الشيء على ما هو به. وهذا باطل، لأنَّ المعدوم ليس بشيء، ولا يقع عليه اسم الشيء، لأنَّ الله تعالى خلق الأشياء لا من شيء، وقالت المعتزلة: خلق الأشياء بقوله: كُن فَيَكُونُ ﴾ [يس: ۸۲]. وعندنا بالصنع لا بالقول، فلو قلنا: إنه معرفة الشيء على ما هو به، يؤدي إلى قِدَم الأعيان مع الله تعالى، وذلك مذهبُ الدَّهرية الكفرة الفَجَرة؛ لأن عندهم العالم قديم، والله تعالى عالم بعلمه، والعلم من صفاته الأزلية، بخلاف ما قالت المعتزلة: إنَّ ذاته علمه، والله تعالى عالم بذاته على ما ذكرنا . وعندنا هو عالم بعلمه، والعِلْمُ من صفاته الأزلية، علم ما يكون قبل أن يكون، وما لا يكون أن لو كان كيف يكون قد سبق علمه الأشياء قبل كونها قال الله تعالى: ﴿قل لا يَعْلَمُ مَن فِي السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ الْغَيْبَ إِلَّا اللهُ ﴾ [النمل: ٦٥]. وقالت المعتزلة والرافضة والقدرية : إنه لا يعلم الشيء ما لم يخلقه ويوجده.
If it is said: What is the definition of knowledge? Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a said: Knowledge is the cognition of the known as it is, and that is the knowledge of created beings. And knowledge of Allah Almighty is encompassing and familiarity of what is as it is, because He is not described by cognition, for He has always been knowing through what we have clarified. Allah Almighty said: 'We have encompassed all that he had with knowledge' [18:91]. And the Mu'tazila said: The definition of knowledge is knowing a thing as it is. This is false, because non-existence is not a thing, and the name 'thing' does not apply to it, because Allah Almighty created things from nothing, and the Mu'tazila said: He created things by His word 'Be, and it is' [36:82]. According to us, it is by act, not by word. For if we said that it is knowing a thing as it is, it would lead to the eternity of entities with Allah Almighty, and that is the doctrine of the Dahriyya, the disbelieving sinners, because according to them the world is eternal, and Allah Almighty is Knowing by His knowledge, and knowledge is among His eternal attributes, contrary to what the Mu'tazila said: that His essence is His knowledge, and Allah Almighty is Knowing by His essence according to what we mentioned. And according to us, He is Knowing by His knowledge, and knowledge is among His eternal attributes. He knows what will be before it is, and what would not be if it were to be, how it would be; His knowledge preceded all things before their existence. Allah Almighty said: 'Say: None in the heavens and the earth knows the unseen except Allah' [27:65]. And the Mu'tazila, the Rafida, and the Qadariyya said: He does not know a thing until He creates it and brings it into existence.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage is a definition and refutation. It begins with the question: 'What is the definition of knowledge?' The author gives the Sunni definition: knowledge is cognition (ma'rifa) of the known as it is, which applies to created beings. For Allah, knowledge is encompassing (ihata) and acquaintance (khabar) of things as they are, because He is not described by the term 'cognition' (ma'rifa) since He has always been knowing. A Quranic verse is cited. Then the Mu'tazila definition is given: knowledge is knowing a thing as it is. The author refutes it by arguing that non-existents are not things, and if knowledge were simply knowing a thing as it is, it would imply that non-existents have some reality, leading to the eternity of entities alongside God—the doctrine of the Dahriyya materialists. The author then contrasts the Sunni view: God knows by His knowledge, which is an eternal attribute, not by His essence as the Mu'tazila claim. This knowledge encompasses all things before they exist, supported by another verse. Finally, the author mentions the false view of the Mu'tazila, Rawafid, and Qadariyya that God does not know a thing until He creates it, which is refuted by the preceding affirmation of eternal knowledge.

Key Terms
العلم
المعرفة
المعدوم
الشيء
الدهرية
القدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that non-existent things are 'things' in a real sense, and that God's knowledge is dependent on the existence of things.
Correction: The passage teaches that non-existents are not things, and God's knowledge is eternal and precedes all creation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the Mu'tazila definition lead to the doctrine of the Dahriyya?
A) Because it denies that God has knowledge
B) Because it implies non-existents are things, making them eternal
C) Because it claims the world is created
D) Because it equates God's knowledge with His essence

Key Takeaway
The Sunni view: God's knowledge is an eternal attribute that encompasses all things before they exist, unlike the Mu'tazila definition that implies the eternity of non-existent entities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God's knowledge encompasses every detail of your life—including the sins you will commit—before you were born? How does that awareness change the way you approach your private thoughts and actions today?
?
The Mu'tazila denied that God knows things before they exist. In your own life, do you ever act as if God is unaware of your intentions or future choices? What would it mean for you to live with the conviction that His knowledge precedes every moment of your existence?
?
The author warns that a seemingly small definitional error can lead to heresy. Are there any beliefs you hold that, upon honest examination, might logically undermine God's perfection? Will you take the time to uncover and correct them, even if it is uncomfortable?

Creation is by Divine Act, Not by Command
Imam Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazila view that creation is by command and warns of the consequence of eternal entities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazila view that creation occurs through the command 'Be' leads to the problematic conclusion of eternal entities alongside Allah, and why Ahl al-Sunna affirm creation by divine making.

Core Idea
Creation is by divine act, not by speech.

This lesson addresses a critical difference between Ahl al-Sunna and the Mu'tazila on how Allah creates. The Mu'tazila claim that creation occurs through the command 'Be,' but Imam Nasafi refutes this by showing that it implies the non-existent is a thing and that created entities would be eternal. Instead, he affirms that creation is by divine act (ṣunʿ) from nothing.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazila claim
Read the passage and identify the Mu'tazila position that Allah creates by His command 'Be' (kūn fayakūn).
Notice that the Mu'tazila consider the command itself as the creative act.
2
Identify the author's refutation
The author begins by stating that the Mu'tazila view is false. He argues that the non-existent (al-ma'dūm) is not a thing, and Allah created things from nothing.
This refutes the idea that the command brings things into existence from pre-existing non-existence.
3
See the consequence of the Mu'tazila view
The author warns that if creation were by command and the command is equated with Allah's knowledge, then the known entities would be eternal with Allah, leading to the doctrine of the Dahriyya who believe in the eternity of the world.
This shows the high stakes of this theological disagreement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا باطل، لأنَّ المعدوم ليس بشيء، ولا يقع عليه اسم الشيء، لأنَّ الله تعالى خلق الأشياء لا من شيء، [وقالت المعتزلة: خلق الأشياء] بقوله: كُن فَيَكُونُ وعندنا بالصنع لا بالقول، فلو قلنا: إنه معرفة الشيء على ما هو به، يؤدي إلى قِدَم الأعيان مع الله تعالى، وذلك مذهبُ الدَّهرية الكفرة الفَجَرة؛ لأن عندهم العالم قديم، والله تعالى عالم بعلمه، والعلم من صفاته الأزلية، بخلاف ما قالت المعتزلة: إنَّ ذاته علمه، والله تعالى عالم بذاته على ما ذكرنا.
And this is false, because the non-existent is not a thing, and the name 'thing' does not apply to it, because Allah Most High created things from nothing. [And the Mu'tazila said: He created things] by His word: 'Be, and it is.' And with us it is by making (al-ṣunʿ) not by speech (al-qawl). For if we said that it (the command) is knowledge of the thing as it is, it would lead to the eternity of entities (al-aʿyān) with Allah Most High. And that is the doctrine of the Dahriyya, the disbelieving, wicked ones; because with them the world is eternal, and Allah Most High knows by His knowledge, and knowledge is among His eternal attributes, contrary to what the Mu'tazila said: that His essence is His knowledge, and Allah Most High knows by His essence, as we have mentioned.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila view on creation. The author begins by stating that the Mu'tazila position is false. He argues that the non-existent (al-ma'dūm) is not a thing and does not merit the name 'thing', because Allah created things from nothing. The Mu'tazila claim that Allah creates by His command 'Be'. In contrast, the Ahl al-Sunna (here identified as 'with us' meaning the Maturidi school) hold that creation is by divine making (al-ṣunʿ), not by speech. The author then shows the dangerous implication of the Mu'tazila view: if creation were by command, and if the command is taken as knowledge of the thing as it is, then the objects of knowledge would be eternal along with Allah, because Allah's knowledge is eternal. This leads to the doctrine of the Dahriyya (materialists/eternalists) who assert the eternity of the world. The author then distinguishes the correct view: Allah knows by His eternal knowledge, which is among His eternal attributes, contrary to the Mu'tazila who hold that His essence is His knowledge.

Key Terms
المعدوم (al-ma'dūm) - the non-existent
الصنع (al-ṣunʿ) - making/creation by act
القول (al-qawl) - speech/command
الدَّهرية (al-dahriyya) - eternalists/materialists

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Mu'tazila view is a minor difference, but the real issue is that it implies eternal creation alongside God.
Correction: The Mu'tazila view leads to the eternity of entities (qidam al-a'yān) alongside Allah, which is the heresy of the Dahriyya.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Nasafi, what would be the consequence if we said that creation is by command and the command is knowledge of the thing?
A) It would contradict revelation
B) It would imply that entities are eternal with Allah
C) It would make Allah subject to time
D) It would negate Allah's power

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila view of creation by command leads to the heresy of eternal entities, whereas Ahl al-Sunna affirm creation by divine act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the Mu'tazila view that creation is by command, do you ever feel a sense that God's actions are distant or merely verbal? In what ways does that affect your awe of His active power in your life? Will you today consciously acknowledge that every moment of your existence is sustained by His direct act (ṣunʿ), not just a passive command?
?
The passage warns that a wrong view of creation leads to the heresy of eternal entities. Reflect on any area in your life where you treat something as if it were eternal alongside God—perhaps a habit, a possession, or a relationship. What would it mean for you to truly see it as created by His act from nothing, and thus contingent?
?
Imam Nasafi calls the Dahriyya 'disbelieving, wicked ones.' How does your understanding of God's active creation shape your gratitude for your own existence? Are you living as if your existence is a direct, ongoing act of God, or as if it were a fixed, independent reality? Challenge yourself to spend five minutes today in reflection on your absolute dependence on His making.

God's Knowledge is Eternal and Precedes All Things
Affirmation of God's eternal knowledge and refutation of deniers of pre-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ahl al-Sunna affirm that Allah's knowledge is eternal and precedes all things, and how this differs from the views of the Mu'tazila, Rawāfiḍ, and Qadariyya.

Core Idea
Allah's knowledge is eternal and encompasses all things before they exist.

This lesson presents a foundational creedal point from Imam Nasafi's Bahr al-Kalam. The author states the Ahl al-Sunna position that Allah knows by His eternal knowledge, which precedes all creation. He then contrasts this with the opposing views of three sects: the Mu'tazila, Rawāfiḍ, and Qadariyya. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping the Islamic doctrine of divine omniscience.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic text and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation to grasp the overall meaning.
Pay attention to the key terms like 'al-ʿilm' and 'al-ṣifāt al-azaliyya'.
2
Identify the two main parts
Notice that the passage first affirms Allah's eternal knowledge and then refutes the opposing sects.
The refutation is a key polemical element common in kalam texts.
3
Reflect on the Qur'anic evidence
Consider how the verse 'None knows the unseen except Allah' supports the claim that His knowledge encompasses all things before they occur.
This verse is a direct proof against those who limit God's knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعندنا هو عالم بعلمه، والعِلْمُ من صفاته الأزلية، علم ما يكون قبل أن يكون، وما لا يكون أن لو كان كيف يكون قد سبق علمه الأشياء قبل كونها قال الله تعالى: ﴿قل لا يَعْلَمُ مَن فِي السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ الْغَيْبَ إِلَّا اللهُ﴾ وقالت [المعتزلة و] الرافضة والقدرية: إنه لا يعلم الشيء ما لم يخلقه ويُوجده،
And with us, He is Knowing by His knowledge, and knowledge is among His eternal attributes. He knows what will be before it is, and what would not be if it were to be, how it would be. His knowledge preceded things before their coming into existence. Allah Most High said: 'Say: None in the heavens and the earth knows the unseen except Allah.' And the Mu'tazila, Rawāfiḍ, and Qadariyya said: He does not know a thing until He creates it and brings it into existence.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage states the Ahl al-Sunna view on divine knowledge. The author affirms that Allah knows by His knowledge, and that knowledge is an eternal attribute. This means that Allah's knowledge is not identical to His essence (as the Mu'tazila claim) but is an eternal attribute subsisting in Him. He knows all things before they occur, including what will happen and even hypotheticals (what would not be, if it were, how it would be). The author supports this with a Qur'anic verse: 'Say: None in the heavens and the earth knows the unseen except Allah.' Then he contrasts this with the views of the Mu'tazila, Rawāfiḍ (Shi'a), and Qadariyya, who claim that Allah does not know a thing until He creates it and brings it into existence. This refutation upholds the pre-existence and comprehensiveness of divine knowledge.

Key Terms
العلم (al-ʿilm) - knowledge
الصفات الأزلية (al-ṣifāt al-azaliyya) - eternal attributes
المعتزلة (al-mu'tazila)
الرافضة (al-rawāfiḍ)
القدرية (al-qadariyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that God's knowledge is acquired over time or that He does not know things until they happen.
Correction: This passage asserts that Allah's knowledge is eternal and precedes all things, including hypothetical scenarios.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, how do the Mu'tazila, Rawāfiḍ, and Qadariyya view God's knowledge?
A) They say He knows by an eternal knowledge
B) They say He knows only after creating things
C) They say He does not know at all
D) They say He knows by His essence

Key Takeaway
Allah's knowledge is eternal and encompasses all things before their existence, contrary to the views of the Mu'tazila, Rawāfiḍ, and Qadariyya.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah's knowledge encompasses every detail of your life—including every thought, intention, and action—how does that change the way you think about your private moments when no one else is watching? What one thing will you stop doing because you now know Allah has always known it?
?
When you face a difficult decision, do you truly believe that Allah already knows the outcome and has known it from eternity? How does that realization affect your reliance on His guidance rather than your own planning? Commit to seeking His guidance first in your next decision.
?
The passage says Allah even knows what would never happen. Reflect on a time you were saved from a sin you might have committed. How does knowing that Allah was fully aware of that hypothetical situation deepen your gratitude? Write down one specific way you will thank Him today for that unseen protection.

The True Nature of Faith: Between Jabriyya and Qadriyya
Refutes Jabriyya and Qadriyya and states the correct middle position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct middle position on the origin of faith between the Jabriyya and Qadriyya, and how to distinguish divine attributes from human attributes.

Core Idea
Faith is a blend: action from the servant, guidance from God.

This passage tackles a critical debate in Islamic theology: where does faith come from? The author first presents two extreme views—that faith is entirely from God (Jabriyya) or entirely from man (Qadriyya). He then articulates the balanced Ahl al-Sunna position that distinguishes what comes from God and what comes from the servant, providing a clear principle for distinguishing between created and uncreated.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the question posed at the beginning.
The passage is dense with technical terms.
2
Identify the two extremes
Find where the author describes the Jabriyya position (faith entirely from God) and the Qadriyya position (faith entirely from servant). Note the consequences.
The Jabriyya say the servant is compelled; the Qadriyya claim independence from God.
3
Examine the enumeration
The author lists several pairs: what is from God (hidaya, ta'rif, tawfiq, etc.) and what is from the servant (ma'rifa, ihtida', jidd, etc.). Make a mental note of each.
This enumeration is the heart of the correct position.
4
Grasp the principle
The author concludes that whatever is from God is uncreated, and whatever is from the servant is created. Relate this to the distinction between divine and human attributes.
This principle prevents falling into error.
5
Reflect on the warning
The author ends by warning that failing to distinguish God's attributes from the servant's attributes leads to misguidance. Consider why this is important.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو بعضه من الله تعالى إلى العبد وبعضه من العبد إلى الله؟ فإن قال: من الله تعالى إلى العبد، فهذا قوة لمذهب الجبرية، لأنهم قالوا: العبد مجبور على الإيمان والكفر. وإن قال: من العبد إلى الله تعالى، فهذا قوة لمذهب القدرية لأنهم قالوا: العبد مستطیع بگشب نفسه لنفسه قبل الفعل، ولا يحتاج إلى قوة وعَوْنٍ من الله تعالى. والجواب عنه أن نقول : الإيمان فعل العبد بهداية الرَّبِّ جل جلاله، والتعريف الله تعالى، والمعرفة والتعرُّف من العبد والهداية من الله تعالى، والاهتداء والاستهداء من العبد، والتوفيق من الله تعالى، والجد والعَزْمُ والقصد من العبد، والإكرام والعطاء من الله تعالى والقبول من العبد. فما كان من الله تعالى فهو غير مخلوق، وما كان من العبد فهو مخلوق؛ لأن الله تعالى بجميع صفاته غير مخلوق، والعبد بجميع صفاته مخلوق، فكل من لم يُميز صفةَ الله تعالى من صفة العبد فهو ضال مبتدع.
Or is some of it from God Almighty to the servant and some of it from the servant to God? If he says: from God Almighty to the servant, this is support for the doctrine of the Jabriyya, because they said: the servant is compelled (majboor) over faith and disbelief. And if he says: from the servant to God Almighty, this is support for the doctrine of the Qadriyya, because they said: the servant has power (mustatee') by his own acquisition for himself before the act, and does not need strength or help from God Almighty. And the answer to that is that we say: Faith is the action of the servant with the guidance (hidaya) of the Lord, Glorified be His Majesty, and definition (ta'rif) is from God Almighty, and knowledge (ma'rifa) and self-awareness (ta'arruf) are from the servant, and guidance (hidaya) is from God Almighty, and seeking guidance (ihtida') and drawing guidance (istihda') are from the servant, and enabling success (tawfiq) is from God Almighty, and determination (jidd), resolve ('azm), and intention (qasd) are from the servant, and honoring (ikram) and giving ('ata') are from God Almighty, and acceptance (qabul) is from the servant. So what is from God Almighty is uncreated, and what is from the servant is created; because God Almighty with all His attributes is uncreated, and the servant with all his attributes is created. So whoever does not distinguish the attribute of God Almighty from the attribute of the servant is an erring innovator.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of two extremist positions regarding the origin of faith (iman). The author first presents the question: is faith entirely from God, or entirely from the servant, or partly from both? He then explains that if one says it is entirely from God, that supports the Jabriyya (determinists) who hold that the servant is compelled in both faith and disbelief. If one says it is entirely from the servant, that supports the Qadriyya (free-will proponents) who claim the servant has independent power and does not need God's help. The author then gives the correct answer: faith is the servant's action, but it is guided by God's hidaya. He proceeds to enumerate what aspects come from God and what from the servant: ta'rif (making known) from God, while ma'rifa (knowledge) and ta'arruf (self-awareness) from the servant; hidaya from God, while ihtida' and istihda' from the servant; tawfiq from God, while jidd, 'azm, qasd from the servant; ikram and 'ata' from God, while qabul from the servant. Then he states the principle: whatever is from God is uncreated, and whatever is from the servant is created, because God with all His attributes is uncreated, and the servant with all his attributes is created. So failing to distinguish God's attributes from the servant's attributes leads to misguidance and innovation.

Key Terms
إيمان
جبرية
قدرية
هداية
تعريف
معرفة
توفيق
مخلوق
غير مخلوق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think faith is either entirely created (like all human actions) or entirely uncreated (like God's attributes).
Correction: The passage clarifies that faith involves aspects both from God (uncreated) and from the servant (created).

Checkpoint
According to the author, which of the following is from the servant?
A) Guidance (hidaya)
B) Enabling success (tawfiq)
C) Knowledge (ma'rifa)
D) Definition (ta'rif)

Key Takeaway
The correct position is that faith involves both divine guidance and human action, and failing to distinguish God's attributes from human attributes leads to innovation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you secretly lean toward one extreme—either feeling that your faith is entirely God's doing (so you can be passive) or entirely your own achievement (so you feel self-sufficient)? How does this balanced teaching compel you to adjust your view of your own spiritual efforts?
?
When you experience a moment of spiritual success, do you attribute it more to God or to yourself? And when you fail, do you blame God or yourself? This passage insists on a clear distinction: what must you consciously change in your daily attitude to give each its due?
?
You cannot walk away from this lesson without deciding: will you now begin to actively distinguish, in your own mind, between what God provides (hidaya, tawfiq) and what you provide (jidd, qasd) in your journey of faith? What concrete habit can you adopt to keep this distinction alive?

Refuting the Objection of Shared Attribution Using Cause and Effect
Answers objection using cause-effect distinction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the cause-effect distinction resolves the objection that faith cannot be partly from God and partly from the servant.

Core Idea
Cause and effect are distinct; God's causation doesn't imply sharing.

This lesson addresses a subtle theological objection against the position that faith involves both divine and human agency. The objector claims that if faith is partly from God and partly from the servant, it would be a shared entity (mushtarak) between Creator and creature, which is impossible. The author of the text provides a clear answer by distinguishing between the cause (sabab) and the effect (musabbab), using analogies from everyday life.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to understand the objection that if faith is partly from God and partly from the servant, it becomes shared (mushtarak) and thus impermissible.
Notice the phrasing 'لو كان بعضه من الله وبعضه من العبد'.
2
Grasp the cause-effect distinction
The author answers by defining God's ta'rif as a cause (sabab) for the servant's salvation, and the servant as the caused (musabbab). God is the Musabbib (the One who causes). The caused is not the cause.
Key terms: سبب, مسبب, مسبب.
3
Examine the analogies
The author uses two analogies: provision (rizq) as cause for survival, and ablution (wudu) as cause for prayer. In neither case is the cause considered part of the effect.
These analogies clarify that cause and effect remain distinct.
4
Apply to faith and light of knowledge
The author concludes that God's ta'rif is granting light in the believer's heart, which is not shared. The light of knowledge (nur al-ma'rifa) is created, because everything other than God is created.
This reinforces the distinction: divine act (uncreated) vs. human reception (created).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: الإيمان لو كان بعضه من الله وبعضه من العبد يكون مشتركاً بين الرب والعبد، وذلك لا يجوز. الجواب عنه أن نقول: التعريف من الله تعالى سبب لنجاة العبد، والعبد مسبب، والله مسبب، والمسبب غير المسبب، كما أن الرزق سبب لبقاء العبد، وكذلك الوضوء سبب الجواز الصلاة، ولا يقال بأنه من الصلاة، فكذلك التعريف من الله تعالى سبب النجاة، وهو إعطاء نور في قلب المؤمن فلا يكون مشتركاً، ونور المعرفة في قلب المؤمن مخلوق، لأن ما سوى الله تعالى فهو مخلوق.
For if it is said: If faith is partly from God and partly from the servant, it becomes shared between the Lord and the servant, and that is not permissible. The answer to it is that we say: The definition (ta'rif) from God Almighty is a cause (sabab) for the servant's salvation, and the servant is the one caused (musabbab), and God is the cause (musabbib), and the caused is not the cause. Just as provision (rizq) is a cause for the survival of the servant, and likewise ablution (wudu') is a cause for the permissibility of prayer, and it is not said that it is part of prayer, so similarly definition from God Almighty is a cause for salvation, and it is granting (i'ta') of light in the heart of the believer, so it is not shared. And the light of knowledge (nur al-ma'rifa) in the heart of the believer is created, because whatever is besides God Almighty is created.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection against the position that faith is partly from God and partly from the servant. The objector argues that if such is the case, then faith becomes something shared (mushtarak) between God and servant, which is impermissible because it would imply associating creation with the Creator. The author answers by making a distinction between cause (sabab) and effect (musabbab). The ta'rif from God is a cause for the servant's salvation, while the servant's actions (musabbab) are the effect. God is the Musabbib (the One who causes). The caused is not the cause. He gives two analogies: rizq (provision) is a cause for survival, but survival is not the same as provision; wudu' is a cause for prayer, but not part of prayer. Similarly, God's ta'rif is the cause, and the servant's acceptance (qabul) is the effect; they are distinct, not shared. He then clarifies that the ta'rif is God's granting of light in the believer's heart, and that light of knowledge (nur al-ma'rifa) is created, because everything other than God is created. This reinforces that the divine act (ta'rif) is uncreated (since it is God's action), but the resulting knowledge in the servant is created.

Key Terms
مشترك
سبب
مسبب
مسبب
نور المعرفة
مخلوق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If faith is partly from God and partly from the servant, then it becomes a shared essence between them.
Correction: The author clarifies that it is a cause-effect relationship: God's ta'rif is the cause, and the servant's faith is the effect. Cause and effect are distinct, not shared in essence.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the role of God's ta'rif in relation to the servant's salvation?
A) It is an essential part of the servant's faith
B) It is a cause (sabab) for the servant's salvation
C) It is the same as the servant's faith
D) It is created

Key Takeaway
God's act of granting guidance (ta'rif) is a cause for faith, not a part of it, and the resulting knowledge in the heart is created, thus no shared essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you examine your own faith, do you truly see it as entirely a gift from God (the cause) and your own response as the effect? Or do you secretly believe you contributed something of your own essence that makes it 'shared'? How does this affect your gratitude and humility?
?
The passage says the light of knowledge in your heart is created, meaning it is not God Himself. Do you ever mistake your spiritual experiences or insights for God, making them idols? What would it mean to honor the Giver without worshiping His gift?
?
If God's guidance is a cause and your faith is an effect, then your salvation hinges on God's act, not your effort. Does this cause you to rest in His grace, or does it make you uneasy about your own responsibility? Where is your heart leaning right now?

The Act is Distinct from the Effect
Nasafi states a principle with five parallel pairs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam principle that God's acts are distinct from their created effects, and why this distinction matters for proper tawhid.

Core Idea
God's making is not the thing made: the act is never the effect.

This lesson introduces a foundational principle that pervades kalam discussions on divine attributes, particularly the relationship between God's actions and the created world. Imam Nasafi states this principle concisely, and we will unpack its meaning and theological significance.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the English translation. Let the rhythm of the five pairs settle in your mind.
2
Identify the five pairs
Find each of the five pairs (e.g., ja'l vs. maj'ul, tarziq vs. marzuq). Notice how each pair contrasts a divine act with its created effect.
The pairs are parallel in structure.
3
Understand the theological purpose
Ask yourself: why would a theologian insist on this distinction? The commentary explains that it prevents conflating God's attribute with the created thing, preserving tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا يرجع إلى أصل، وهو أنَّ الجعل غير المجعول، والترزيق غير المرزوق، والتخليق غير المخلوق، والتعريف غير المعرفة، والتكوين غير المكوّن.
And this goes back to a principle, which is that the making (ja'l) is not the made (maj'ul), and the providing (tarziq) is not the provided (marzuq), and the creating (takhliq) is not the created (makhluq), and the defining (ta'rif) is not the knowledge (ma'rifa), and the bringing into existence (takwin) is not the thing brought into existence (mukawwan).
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage formulates a fundamental theological principle that distinguishes between God's acts and the effects or results of those acts. Each pair illustrates the distinction: ja'l (an act of making) is distinct from maj'ul (the thing made); tarziq (providing sustenance) is distinct from marzuq (the sustenance itself); takhliq (creating) is distinct from makhluq (the created thing); ta'rif (defining/making known) is distinct from ma'rifa (knowledge); takwin (bringing into existence) is distinct from mukawwan (the existent thing). This principle is used to argue that when we say faith is from God and from the servant, we are not conflating the divine act with the servant's action; rather, God's act (e.g., ta'rif, tawfiq) is uncreated, while the resulting state in the servant (e.g., ma'rifa) is created. This clarifies the distinction and refutes the objection of sharing.

Key Terms
جعل
مجعول
ترزيق
مرزوق
تخليق
مخلوق
تعريف
معرفة
تكوين
مكون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that 'takhliq' (creating) and 'makhluq' (created) are the same entity or that God's act is the created thing itself.
Correction: The principle shows they are distinct: the divine act is uncreated, the effect is created.

Checkpoint
Which of the following pairs illustrates the principle that God's act is distinct from the created effect?
A) Ja'l and maj'ul
B) Ta'rif and ma'rifa
C) Takwin and mukawwan
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
The principle that God's act is distinct from its effect is essential for preserving divine transcendence and avoiding anthropomorphism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a blessing, do you stop to remember that Allah's act of providing (tarziq) is not the same as the provision itself? How does this distinction deepen your gratitude and prevent you from worshipping the gift rather than the Giver?
?
Have you ever mistaken your own knowledge (ma'rifa) for Allah's guidance (ta'rif)? In what areas of your life might you be relying on your understanding as if it were infallible, forgetting that it is a created effect of His uncreated act?
?
The principle teaches that Allah's act of creating (takhliq) is uncreated, while the created thing is temporal. How should this affect your view of your own existence? Do you live as if you are merely a created effect, or do you sometimes act as if your being is self-sufficient?
