The Decree is Allah's Hidden Secret
Nasafi presents a second answer distinguishing hidden decree from manifest command.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second Ash'ari answer regarding divine decree (qada') as a hidden secret, while the manifest command serves as Allah's proof against His servants.

Core Idea
The decree is Allah's secret; only His commands are our proof.

Imam Nasafi now offers a second answer to the question of how divine decree relates to human responsibility. This answer draws a sharp distinction between the hidden reality of qada' and the manifest reality of Allah's commands and prohibitions. The key is that the decree is concealed from us, so it cannot be used as an excuse; the command is public and serves as the basis for accountability.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the surface meaning. Let the author's own words set the tone.
2
Identify the two key concepts
Notice the contrast: al-qaḍā' is a hidden secret (sirr), while al-amr and al-nahy are a manifest proof (ḥujja). These are the two poles of the argument.
The decree is invisible; the command is visible.
3
Understand why the decree is hidden
The text says 'akhfāhu 'an al-khalq' – He hid it from creation. This means humans have no direct knowledge of Allah's decree; it is not accessible for argument.
If the decree were known, it might be misused as an excuse.
4
Grasp the consequence for punishment
The conclusion: when a person leaves the manifest command while having capacity (mustaṭī‘), that act itself deserves punishment. The hidden decree is irrelevant.
Capacity is the condition for accountability.
5
Connect to the broader discussion
Reflect on how this answer resolves the tension between divine decree and human responsibility: it removes the decree from the realm of human argument and focuses on the manifest command.
This is a classic Ash'ari approach.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجواب آخر وهو أن القضاء سر الله تعالى أخفاه عن الخلق والأمر والنهي حجة الله تعالى على خلقه، فإذا ترك أمره الظاهر وهو مستطيع فلذلك المعنى يستحق العقوبة.
And another answer is that the decree (al-qaḍā’) is the secret of Allah the Exalted, which He hid from creation, while the command and prohibition are the proof (ḥujja) of Allah the Exalted against His creation. So when he leaves His manifest command while he is capable (mustaṭī‘), for that meaning he deserves punishment.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage provides a second answer to the problem of qada' and punishment. The author explains that divine decree (al-qaḍā’) is a secret hidden by Allah from creation—humans do not have access to it. Instead, what is manifest is the command (al-amr) and prohibition (al-nahy), which serve as Allah’s proof (al-ḥujja) against His servants. Therefore, when a servant violates the manifest command while having the capacity to obey (mustaṭī‘), he deserves punishment. The decree itself remains hidden and is not a basis for argumentation.

Key Terms

السر (al-sirr)
الأمر (al-amr)
النهي (al-nahy)
الحجة (al-ḥujja)
الاستطاعة (al-istiṭā‘a)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People think they can argue about the hidden decree to excuse their sins, saying 'it was decreed so I had to sin.'
Correction: This passage clarifies that the decree is hidden and not accessible; it is not a valid excuse. The manifest command and prohibition are the proof, and violation with capacity deserves punishment.

Checkpoint

A) Because the decree compels him to sin.
B) Because he leaves Allah's manifest command while having capacity.
C) Because the decree is public and known to all.
D) Because he knows the secret of the decree.

Key Takeaway
The decree is Allah's hidden secret; the manifest command is His proof, and capacity brings accountability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
This lesson says that the decree is hidden and the manifest command is the proof. If you truly believe that, what will change about how you judge your own sins—and how you advise others who make excuses?
?
Are you living as if your accountability is based on what you can do right now, or do you secretly rely on the idea that 'everything is written' to lower your guard? What is one step you will take today to act on what is manifest and within your capacity?

Evil Not Attributed to Allah Despite His Decree
Refutes objection about attributing evil to Allah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why decreeing evil does not attribute evil to Allah, based on the distinction between Allah’s decree and the servant’s act, and the rational proof of the impossibility of the servant’s will overcoming Allah’s.

Core Idea
Allah decrees all things, but evil is attributed to the servant, not to Allah.

This lesson addresses a common objection against the Ash‘ari doctrine of divine decree: if Allah decrees evil, doesn’t that make Him the author of evil? The author of our text refutes this by drawing a clear distinction between Allah’s decree and the servant’s act. He provides both an illustrative example (the instrument of fornication) and a rational proof (the necessity of Allah’s will prevailing over the servant’s will) to defend the orthodox position.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the opening objection carefully: if Allah decrees evil and the servant cannot escape, then evil is attributed to Allah. Note the logical concern.
This objection is raised against the Ash‘ari view that all things are decreed by Allah.
2
Grasp the answer: distinction
The author answers by stating that the servant’s act is distinct from Allah’s decree. Understand the concept of ‘mumayyaz’ (distinct) as a key to resolving the attribution problem.
The example of the instrument of fornication illustrates how the tool is created by Allah but the sin is not.
3
Follow the rational proof
Read the rational argument: if Allah did not will evil and the servant wills it, the servant’s will would overcome Allah’s, implying incapacity in Allah. Realize why this leads to disbelief and why Allah must will everything.
4
Confirm with the Quranic verse
The author cites Quran 81:29 to reinforce that all wills are under Allah’s will. Reflect on how this verse supports the argument.
This verse is a clear proof that human will is subordinate to Allah’s will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: لو قلنا بأن الله تعالى يقضي بالشر، والعبد لا يقدر أن يفر من قضاء الله تعالى فيؤدي ذلك إلى أن ينسب الشر إلى الله تعالى. قلنا: فعل العبد مميز من قضاء الله تعالى، ألا ترى أن الله تعالى خلق آلة الزنا ولا ينسب الزنا إلى الله تعالى. يدل عليه أن الله تعالى خلق الحركة والقوة في نفس العبد، والعبد مستطيع باستطاعة نفسه ومشيئته ولا ينسب الحركة والقوة إلى الله تعالى وإن كانا بقضاء الله تعالى ومشيئته، يدل على صحة ما قلنا أن الله تعالى لو لم يشأ الشر والكفر والمعصية ولا يقضي به والعبد يشاؤه ويفعله، لغلبت مشيئة العبد مشيئة الله تعالى، فيؤدي إلى أن ينسب العجز إلى الله تعالى وهذا كفر، وكل المشيئات تحت مشيئة الله تعالى وإرادته، قال تعالى: ﴿وَمَا تَشَاءُونَ إِلَّا أَن يَشَاءَ اللَّهُ﴾ [التكوير: ٢٩].
If it is said: 'If we say that Allah the Exalted judges with evil, and the servant cannot flee from Allah’s decree, then that leads to attributing evil to Allah the Exalted.' We say: The action of the servant is distinct from the decree of Allah the Exalted. Do you not see that Allah the Exalted created the instrument of fornication, yet fornication is not attributed to Allah? Evidence for it is that Allah created movement and strength in the soul of the servant, and the servant is capable by his own capacity and will, yet movement and strength are not attributed to Allah, even though they are by Allah’s decree and will. This indicates the correctness of what we said: if Allah had not willed evil, disbelief, and disobedience, and did not decree them, while the servant wills and does them, then the servant’s will would overcome Allah’s will, leading to attributing incapacity to Allah—and this is disbelief. And all wills are under Allah’s will and desire. He said the Exalted: 'And you do not will except that Allah wills' [al-Takwir: 29].
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer followed by a proof. The objector argues: if we say Allah decrees evil, and the servant cannot escape that decree, then evil would be attributed to Allah. The author answers by distinguishing: the servant’s action is distinct from Allah’s decree. He gives the example of fornication (zinā): Allah created the organs used in fornication, but the sin of fornication is not attributed to Allah. Then he provides further evidence: Allah creates movement and strength in the servant, but those attributes are not ascribed to Allah, even though they are by His decree and will. He then turns to a rational proof: if Allah did not will evil and the servant wills it, the servant’s will would overpower Allah’s, which would imply incapacity in Allah—that is disbelief. Therefore, Allah must will all things, including evil. He concludes with the Quranic verse 'You do not will unless Allah wills' (81:29), affirming that all wills are subordinate to Allah’s will.

الحركة والقوة
غلبت مشيئة العبد
العجز
المشيئات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If Allah decrees evil, He is the doer of evil and thus evil is attributed to Him.
Correction: The action of the servant is distinct from Allah’s decree; the decree does not make Allah the actor. Evil is attributed to the servant, not to Allah, just as fornication is not attributed to the Creator of the organs.

Checkpoint
What rational proof does the author give to show that Allah must will evil?
A) Without His will, the servant would be compelled, which is injustice.
B) If Allah did not will evil and the servant wills it, the servant’s will would overcome Allah’s, implying incapacity in Allah.
C) The Quran explicitly says that Allah wills evil.
D) The Mu‘tazila agree that Allah wills evil.

Key Takeaway
Allah decrees all things, but evil is attributed to the servant due to the distinction between decree and action, supported by rational proof and Quranic text.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How does the distinction between the decree and your action change how you should view your personal responsibility? Will you now consciously remind yourself that the evil is yours, not His, and seek forgiveness accordingly?
?
Think of a difficult situation where you experienced evil or injustice. Did you at any point question Allah's justice or feel that He was the source of that evil? How would the understanding that the act is distinct from the decree help you preserve your tawhid? What specific step will you take the next time such doubt arises?
?
The author argues that if Allah did not will evil, the servant's will would overcome Allah's, implying incapacity. Do you truly live as if every single one of your desires and actions is under Allah's will? In what area of your life do you still feel self-sufficient? What would change if you fully internalized that your will is entirely subordinate to His?

The distinction between God's eternal knowledge and the written decree
The author distinguishes between God's unchangeable knowledge and the changeable written decree.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the change recorded in the Preserved Tablet does not imply change in God's eternal knowledge.

Core Idea
God's knowledge never changes; only the written decree can be altered.

This passage addresses a common theological puzzle: if God knows everything from eternity, how can a person's recorded destiny change? The author carefully distinguishes between God's unchangeable knowledge and the changeable written decree in the Preserved Tablet, using the Quranic concept of abrogation to clarify.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through.
Notice the distinction between 'علم الله' and 'اللوح المحفوظ'.
2
Identify the two types of 'ajal'
The author mentions 'الأجل المثبت في اللوح المحفوظ' and 'علم الله القديم'. Find these and note their different properties.
The first is changeable, the second is not.
3
Understand the argument for changeability
Why does the author argue that the written decree can change? Because denying it would invalidate the Books and Messengers.
This is a rational necessity.
4
Reflect on the Quranic proof
The verse from Surah Ar-Ra'd is quoted. Notice how it distinguishes between 'يَمْحُوا اللَّهُ مَا يَشَاءُ وَيُثْبِتُ' and 'أُمُّ الْكِتَابِ'.
The 'Mother of the Book' is with Allah, unchangeable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلنا: من كان في سابق علم الله أنه شقي أو سعيد فإنه لا يتغير ولا يتبدل علمه ولكن لو علم أنه يصير سعيدا في بعض عمره وشقيا في بعض عمره يجوز أن يكون اسمه مكتوبًا في اللوح المحفوظ من الأشقياء أو من السعداء ثم تحول ذلك ويكتب من الأشقياء أو من السعداء لأنا لو قلنا بأن الشقي لا يصير سعيدا، والسعيد لا يصير شقيا يودي إلى إبطال الكتب والرسل وهذا لا يجوز. الأجل المثبت في اللوح المحفوظ، وهو ما تطلع عليه الملائكة، وهو قابل للتغير والتبدل، فالتغير هنا لا يقع في علم الله تعالى، وإنما يقع في المكتوب باللوح، وذلك مثل النسخ في القرآن والثاني: هو علم الله القديم الأزلي الذي لا يقع به تغير ولا تبدل، يدل على ذلك أيضًا قوله تعالى: {وَمَا كَانَ لِرَسُولٍ أَن يَأْتِيَ بِآيَةٍ إِلَّا بِإِذْنِ اللَّهِ لِكُلِّ أَجَلٍ كِتَابٌ يَمْحُوا اللَّهُ مَا يَشَاءُ وَيُثْبِتُ وَعِندَهُ أُمُّ الْكِتَابِ} [الرعد: 38-39].
We say: Whoever was in God's prior knowledge that he is wretched or happy, his knowledge does not change nor alter. But if He knows that he will become happy in some of his life and wretched in some of his life, it is permissible that his name be written in the Preserved Tablet among the wretched or among the happy, then that is changed and he is written among the wretched or among the happy. Because if we say that the wretched does not become happy and the happy does not become wretched, it leads to the invalidation of the Books and Messengers, and that is not permissible. The appointed term fixed in the Preserved Tablet, which the angels see, is subject to change and alteration. So change here does not occur in God's knowledge, may He be exalted, but rather occurs in what is written in the Tablet, like abrogation in the Quran. And the second: it is God's eternal, pre-eternal knowledge, with which no change or alteration occurs. That is also indicated by His statement, may He be exalted: {And it is not for any messenger to bring a sign except by permission of Allah. For every term is a decree. Allah erases what He wills and establishes, and with Him is the Mother of the Book} [Ar-Ra'd: 38-39].
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument addressing the apparent conflict between divine foreknowledge and the possibility of a person's destiny changing. The author begins by affirming that God's prior knowledge (ʿilm) of a person being wretched (shaqī) or happy (saʿīd) is immutable and does not change. However, he explains that if God knows that a person will experience happiness in part of his life and wretchedness in another part, it is permissible for that person's name to be initially recorded in the Preserved Tablet (al-lawḥ al-maḥfūẓ) among one group and then changed to the other. He supports this by saying that denying such change would invalidate the sending of Books and Messengers, which is impermissible. The author then distinguishes two meanings of 'term' (ajal): the term recorded in the Preserved Tablet, which angels see and is subject to change (like abrogation in the Quran), and God's eternal, pre-eternal knowledge which never changes. He quotes the Quranic verse (Ar-Ra'd 38-39) to show that Allah erases and establishes what He wills, while the Mother of the Book (Umm al-Kitāb) remains with Him, indicating the distinction between the changeable earthly record and the unchangeable divine knowledge.

Key Terms
علم الله القديم
اللوح المحفوظ
السعادة
الشقاوة
النسخ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Change in the Preserved Tablet implies change in God's eternal knowledge.
Correction: The author clarifies that change only occurs in the written decree, not in God's eternal knowledge, which remains immutable.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the change recorded in the Preserved Tablet (al-lawḥ al-maḥfūẓ) affect?
A) God's eternal, pre-eternal knowledge
B) The written decree that the angels see
C) The happiness or wretchedness of a person in the afterlife
D) The sending of Books and Messengers

Key Takeaway
God's eternal knowledge is unchangeable, but the written decree in the Preserved Tablet can be altered, as indicated by the Quranic verse on erasing and establishing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as if your fate is already fixed, or do you strive to change your state through supplication and good deeds? This lesson teaches that the written decree can change. How will you change your relationship with Allah's mercy today?
?
When you sin, do you feel that you are already written among the wretched and that there is no hope? The author says that the name can be transferred from wretched to happy. What will you do to change your status in the sight of Allah?
?
The author argues that denying change in the decree invalidates the Books and Messengers. How seriously do you take the purpose of prophethood as a means to alter your destiny? Are you truly listening to the words of the Messenger to change your life?

Divine Attributes: Classification and the Middle Path
Nasafi divides attributes into essence and action and states they are neither identical to nor separate from Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two categories of divine attributes and the key principle that they are neither identical to nor separate from Allah.

Core Idea
Allah's attributes are real but not additional entities — neither He nor other than He.

In this passage, Imam Nasafi presents a foundational classification of divine attributes. He divides them into attributes of essence and attributes of action, then clarifies their relationship to Allah's essence using a striking analogy. This lesson unpacks that classification and the crucial theological principle that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Let the author's own words set the stage.
Pay attention to the listing of attributes and the concluding analogy.
2
Identify the two types of attributes
Notice how Nasafi first divides attributes into two categories: attributes of essence (sifat al-dhat) and attributes of action (sifat al-fi‘l). Write down which attributes belong to each category.
Attributes of essence are intrinsic; attributes of action relate to His creative acts.
3
Understand the middle position
Focus on the phrase 'la huwa wa la ghayruhu' (neither He nor other than He). Reflect on the analogy of the unit from ten to see how this avoids both extremes: identity (leading to multiple gods) and separateness (leading to created attributes).
This is a key principle of Sunni creed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في معرفة الصفات الإلهية فنقول: إنما الصفات على وجهين: صفات الذات، وصفات الفعل. أما صفات الذات كالحياة والقدرة، والسمع والبصر، والعلم، والكلام، والمشيئة، والإرادة. وأما صفات الفعل كالتخليق، والترزيق، والإفضال، والإنعام، والإحسان، والرحمة والمغفرة والهداية. فنقول: الله تعالى بجميع صفاته وأسمائه واحد، وبجميع صفاته وأسمائه قديم أزلي من غير تفصيل. فصفات الله تعالى وأسماؤه لا هو ولا غيره، كالواحد من العشرة، ولأنا لو قلنا بأن هذه الصفات هو الله تعالى يؤدي إلى أن يكون إلهين اثنين، والله تعالى واحدٌ لا شريكَ له، ولو قلنا بأن هذه الصفات غير الله تعالى لكانت هذه الصفات محدثة، وهذا لا يجوز.
Chapter on knowledge of divine attributes. We say: Attributes are of two types: attributes of essence and attributes of action. As for attributes of essence, such as life, power, hearing, sight, knowledge, speech, will, and intention. As for attributes of action, such as creating, providing, bestowing, granting favor, doing good, mercy, forgiveness, and guidance. We say: Allah Most High is one with all His attributes and names, and with all His attributes and names He is eternal and pre-eternal without differentiation. So the attributes of Allah Most High and His names are neither He nor other than He, like the unit from ten. For if we said that these attributes are Allah Himself, it would lead to there being two gods, but Allah Most High is one with no partner. And if we said that these attributes are other than Allah, then these attributes would be originated, and that is not permissible.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage is a systematic exposition on the nature of divine attributes (sifat). The author first provides a classification: attributes are of two kinds – those of the essence (sifat al-dhat) and those of action (sifat al-fi'l). The attributes of essence are eternal, intrinsic qualities like life, power, hearing, sight, knowledge, speech, will, and intention. The attributes of action are those that relate to God's creative acts, such as creating, providing, bestowing, etc. The author then affirms that Allah is one with all His attributes and names, and that He is eternal, without any division or composition. The crucial theological point follows: the attributes and names of Allah are 'neither He nor other than He' (la huwa wa la ghayruhu). This is a middle position avoiding two extremes. To explain this, the author uses the analogy of 'the unit from ten': the number one is neither identical to the number ten nor entirely separate from it, for the ten depends on the ones. If the attributes were identical to the essence, then multiple attributes would imply multiple gods (shirk). If they were entirely separate, they would be created entities (muhdath), which is impossible for God. Thus the attributes are not the same as the essence nor are they separate existences; they are real but not additional entities. This preserves both tawhid and the affirmation of attributes.

Key Terms
صفات الذات
صفات الفعل
لا هو ولا غيره
قديم أزلي
الواحد من العشرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that Allah's attributes are either identical to His essence or entirely separate entities.
Correction: Nasafi teaches that they are neither identical nor separate; they are real and eternal but not additional existences.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what is the problem with saying that Allah's attributes are 'other than' Him?
A) It would imply that the attributes are created and temporal
B) It would imply that Allah is composite
C) It would imply that the attributes are the essence itself
D) It would imply that there are multiple gods

Key Takeaway
Allah's attributes are real and eternal, but they are neither identical to nor separate from His essence; they are 'neither He nor other than He'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim that Allah's attributes are real, yet you often act as if His power is dormant when you fear created beings. How does the truth that His power is neither Him nor other than Him confront your misplaced trust?
?
The passage declares that attributes are 'neither He nor other than He.' Do you treat the mercy of Allah as if it were a separate thing to be earned by your deeds, or do you reduce it to a mere concept? How will you realign your understanding to the middle path?
?
If you truly believed that every attribute of Allah is eternal and none can be separated from Him, would you still dare to despair of His mercy or doubt His knowledge? What specific habit of thought or action will you change today because of this lesson?

Two Categories of Divine Attributes
Imam Nasafi classifies and exemplifies divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two primary classifications of divine attributes in Sunni kalam: attributes of essence and attributes of action.

Core Idea
Divine attributes are either essential (eternal) or active (related to creation).

Imam Nasafi begins his chapter on divine attributes by establishing a fundamental distinction that shapes Islamic theology. This passage presents the two categories—essence and action—and gives clear examples. Understanding this classification is key to grasping later discussions on God's attributes and their relation to His essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage aloud if possible, noting the flow from the chapter title to the classification and examples.
Pay attention to the terms صفات الذات and صفات الفعل.
2
Identify the two categories
The passage explicitly states that attributes are of two types. Locate the two terms and note how the author introduces them.
3
Examine the examples
The passage provides lists of attributes for each category. Compare the lists: what kind of attributes are in each?
Notice that attributes of essence are intrinsic qualities like life and power, while attributes of action are acts like creating and providing.
4
Reflect on the significance
Think about why this distinction matters. How does it affect our understanding of God's relationship with the world?
This classification is foundational for later theological discussions.

Attributes of action include creating, providing, bestowing, granting favors, doing good, mercy, forgiveness, and guidance.

The Attributes: Neither God Nor Other
Imam Nasafi states that God's attributes are neither He nor other, with an analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Islamic theology preserves God's unity by affirming that His attributes are neither identical to Him nor separate from Him, using Imam Nasafi's foundational analogy.

Core Idea
God's attributes are neither He nor other: a middle path preserving tawhid.

When we speak of God's attributes—knowledge, power, life—a question arises: are these attributes the same as God or something else? Imam Nasafi presents the orthodox Ash'ari position that they are neither identical nor separate. In this lesson, we examine his concise argument and the famous analogy that helps us grasp this subtle point.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Let Imam Nasafi's own words set the foundation before we interpret.
Notice the opening assertion about God being one and eternal with all attributes.
2
Understand the analogy
Focus on the analogy 'like the one from ten'. The one is neither other than ten nor identical to ten because its existence depends on ten. Similarly, the attributes depend on God's essence.
The analogy is not about parts; it's about conceptual distinction without separate existence.
3
Identify the two false extremes
Nasafi explicitly rejects two wrong beliefs: (1) saying attributes are God leads to multiple gods, (2) saying they are other than God implies they are created. The middle path avoids both.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقول: الله تعالى بجميع صفاته وأسمائه واحد ، وبجميع صفاته وأسمائه قديم أزلي من غير تفصيل. فصفات الله تعالى وأسماؤه لا هو ولا غيره، كالواحد من العشرة، ولأنا لو قلنا بأن هذه الصفات هو الله تعالى يؤدي إلى أن يكون إلهين اثنين، والله تعالى واحدٌ لا شريكَ له، ولو قلنا بأن هذه الصفات غير الله تعالى لكانت هذه الصفات محدثة، وهذا لا يجوز.
So we say: God, exalted, is one with all His attributes and names, and with all His attributes and names He is eternal without differentiation. Thus the attributes and names of God are neither He nor other, like the one from ten. For if we said that these attributes are God Himself, that would lead to there being two gods, and God is one with no partner. And if we said that these attributes are other than God, then these attributes would be originated, and that is not permissible.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage establishes the relationship between God and His attributes. It opens by asserting that God is one and eternal with all His attributes and names, without differentiation. Then it explains that the attributes and names are neither He nor other, using the analogy of the one from ten: the one is not other than ten nor is it identical to ten, because its existence depends on ten. Similarly, the attributes are neither identical to God nor separate existences, otherwise if they were identical it would imply multiple gods, and if they were other they would be created.

Key Terms

كالواحد من العشرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The analogy of 'one from ten' means the attributes are parts of God, like parts of a whole.
Correction: The analogy only illustrates a relationship of non-identity and non-separateness, not composition. Attributes are not parts; they are inseparable from God's essence.

Checkpoint

B) Because that would imply attributes are created
C) Because that would deny God's unity
D) Because that would imply anthropomorphism

Key Takeaway
To preserve God's unity and the eternity of His attributes, we affirm that they are neither He nor other—a middle path that avoids both polytheism and creationism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The analogy of 'the one from ten' shows that attributes have no existence apart from God. When you call upon God by His names, do you truly feel that you are calling upon Him alone, or do you sometimes treat the name as a separate entity? What step will you take to ensure your heart aligns with this tawhid?

Proof of the Eternity of Divine Attributes
Imam Nasafi proves eternity of attributes via reductio ad absurdum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that Allah's attributes of essence are eternal, based on the impossibility of attributing incapability and ignorance to Him.

Core Idea
If Allah lacked power in eternity, He would be incapable — impossible.

Imam Nasafi here presents a concise objection-and-answer argument. The objector asks for the proof that attributes like power, knowledge, life, etc. are eternal. The answer uses a reductio ad absurdum: if these attributes were not eternal, then before they were created Allah would be described with their opposites — incapability and ignorance — which is impossible. Thus the attributes must be eternal.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style: the objection is only one line, followed by a tight argument.
2
Understand the objection
The objector asks: 'What is the proof that these attributes are eternal?' Identify which attributes are being discussed (power, knowledge, life, hearing, sight).
3
Follow the reductio
The answer begins with 'because if God were not powerful in pre-eternity...'. Trace the conditional: if not eternal power, then when He creates power He would have been incapable before. Similarly for knowledge. This leads to describing Allah with incapability and ignorance, which is impossible.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum: assume the opposite, derive a contradiction, so the original must be true.
4
Recognise the conclusion
Since the consequences are impossible (attributing incapability and ignorance to Allah), the original assumption is false. Therefore, the attributes must be eternal.
5
Reflect on the method
Notice that this proof does not rely on revelation alone but on rational argument. It shows that kalam uses logic to defend necessary truths about Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : ما الدليل على أن هذه الصفات قديمات أزليَّات؟
قلنا لهم: لأن الله تعالى لو لم يكن قادرًا في الأزل كيف قدر حين خلق القدرة؟ وكيف قدر حين خلق الحياة والسمع والبصر ؟ وكيف عَلِمَ حين خلق العلم؟ فيؤدي إلى أن يوصف الله تعالى بالعجز قبل ذلك، وبالجهل قبل ذلك، وهذا ممتنع والهادي هو الله.
If it is said: What is the proof that these attributes are eternal? We say to them: Because if God were not powerful in pre-eternity, how could He have power when He created power? And how could He have power when He created life, hearing, and sight? And how would He have knowledge when He created knowledge? That would lead to describing God with incapability before that, and with ignorance before that, and this is impossible. And the Guide is God.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector asks for a proof that these attributes are eternal. The answer uses a reductio: if God were not powerful in pre-eternity, then when He created power He would have been unable before, which would imply incapability and ignorance, impossible. So the attributes must be eternal.

Key Terms
قديم
أزلي
قادر
قدرة
عجز
جهل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The attributes of Allah (like power and knowledge) were created in time after His existence.
Correction: The argument shows they must be eternal, because if they were created, Allah would have been incapable/ignorant before their creation, which is impossible.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if God were not powerful in eternity?
A) He would be able to create power later
B) He would be described as incapable before
C) He would be eternal
D) He would be self-sufficient

Key Takeaway
The attributes of essence must be eternal because otherwise Allah would have been described with their opposites, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah were not eternally powerful, He would have been incapable. But He is never lacking. Does your reliance on Allah's power reflect this eternal constancy, or do you act as if His power fluctuates with your circumstances? Identify one area where you have doubted His power and commit to a specific prayer asking for trust in His unchangeable strength.
?
The proof shows that describing Allah with ignorance or incapability is impossible. Yet in your heart, when you face uncertainty, do you ever unconsciously attribute limitations to Allah's knowledge or power? Write down a scenario where you can actively remind yourself of the eternal, full extent of Allah's attributes today.
?
You now know that Allah's attributes are eternal—never acquired, never changing. Does your worship of Allah treat Him as if He is the same yesterday, today, and forever, or do you sometimes approach Him as if He needs you to inform Him of your needs? Pledge to recite a specific supplication that acknowledges His eternal knowledge and power.

Attributes of Action: The Maturidi Position
Nasafi enumerates attributes of action and asserts their eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi doctrine that attributes of action are eternal and uncreated.

Core Idea
Attributes of action are eternal with Allah's essence.

This passage sets forth the Maturidi view that God's attributes of action, such as creating and providing, are eternal and uncreated, just as His essence is eternal. Before examining the opposing Ash'ari view, we must grasp this foundational claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the first part of the source passage carefully. Notice the list of attributes and the declaration that they are eternal.
The phrase 'لا هو ولا غيره' will be explained.
2
Identify the attributes listed
List the attributes of action mentioned: creating, providing, bestowing, granting favors, doing good, mercy, forgiveness, guidance.
3
Understand 'لا هو ولا غيره'
This phrase means the attributes are neither identical to Allah's essence nor separate from it. They are eternal as He is eternal.
This is a key Maturidi concept.
4
Connect to previous discussion
The phrase 'على ما مر' indicates that this view has been previously established. Reflect on why the eternity of these attributes matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما صفات الفعل كالتخليق، والترزيق، والإفضال، والإنعام، والإحسان، والرحمة والمغفرة، والهداية، فكلها قديمات أزليات لا هو ولا غيره على ما مر. وقالت الأشعرية: إن هذه الصفات كلها مخلوقة. وقالوا إنه لم يكن خالقاً ما لم يخلق الخلق، ولم يكن رازقاً ما لم يرزق الخلق، إلا أنا نقول: يجوز أن يُسمى خالقاً وإن لم يخلق الخلق، ويسمى رازقًا وإن لم يرزق الخلق. الا يُرى أن واحداً منا إذا كان قادراً على الخياطة يسمى خياطاً وإن لم يوجد منه الخياطة، كذلك ههنا الله تعالى لما كان قادراً على التخليق والترزيق يسمى خالقاً ورازقاً، ألا يُرى أن الله تعالى سمّي نفسه مالك يوم الدين وإن لم يخلق يوم الدين، لكن لما كان قادرًا على تخليقه وإيجاده سمَّى نفسه بذلك الاسم، كذا ههنا، إلا أن هذا الجواب ليس بمتين.
As for attributes of action, such as creating, providing, bestowing, granting favors, doing good, mercy, forgiveness, and guidance, all of them are eternal, neither He nor other, as previously mentioned. The Ashʿariyya said: All of these attributes are created. And they said: He was not a creator until He created creation, and He was not a provider until He provided for creation. But we say: It is permissible to call Him creator even if He has not created creation, and to call Him provider even if He has not provided for creation. Do you not see that one of us, when he is capable of sewing, is called a tailor even if he has not performed sewing? Similarly here, God, exalted, because He is capable of creating and providing, is called Creator and Provider. Do you not see that God called Himself Master of the Day of Judgment even though He has not created the Day of Judgment, but because He is capable of creating it and bringing it into existence, He called Himself by that name. The same applies here. However, this answer is not sound.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage first reiterates that attributes of action are eternal, neither He nor other. Then it presents the Ash'ari view that these attributes are created, with the argument that God was not Creator until He created. The author responds with an analogy: a capable tailor is called tailor even before sewing, likewise God is called Creator and Provider due to ability. The author also gives analogy of 'Master of the Day of Judgment'. However, he admits this answer is not strong (ليس بمتين). This shows a critical attitude.

Key Terms
صفات الفعل
قديمات أزليات
لا هو ولا غيره

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) They are identical to Allah's essence.
B) They are separate from Allah's essence.
C) They are neither identical to nor separate from Allah's essence.
D) They are created in time.

Key Takeaway
Attributes of action are eternal and uncreated, being neither identical to Allah's essence nor separate from it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The phrase 'neither He nor other' guards against thinking God's attributes are either identical to His essence (making Him limited) or separate from Him (making Him dependent). Have you ever unknowingly fallen into one of these extremes in your conception of God? How can this lesson recalibrate your worship and reliance on Him?

The Ash'ari Position and Its Refutation
Nasafi reports the Ash'ari view and offers a weak refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari view that attributes of action are created and the Maturidi response with its admitted weakness.

Core Idea
The Ash'ari claim is countered by weak analogies.

Imam Nasafi now presents the Ash'ari position that God's attributes of action are created. He attempts a refutation using analogies of a tailor and of 'Master of the Day of Judgment', but candidly admits that this answer is not strong. This teaches us the importance of sound theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Ash'ari claim
Identify the Ash'ari statement: attributes of action are created; God is not called Creator until He creates.
2
Examine the tailor analogy
The author argues that a capable tailor is called tailor even before sewing. Similarly, God can be called Creator due to His ability.
Consider the strength of this analogy.
3
Analyze the 'Master of the Day' analogy
God called Himself 'Master of the Day of Judgment' before creating that day, based on His power to create it.
4
Note the author's self-criticism
The author concludes 'this answer is not sound'. Reflect on why it might be weak.
This shows intellectual honesty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما صفات الفعل كالتخليق، والترزيق، والإفضال، والإنعام، والإحسان، والرحمة والمغفرة، والهداية، فكلها قديمات أزليات لا هو ولا غيره على ما مر. وقالت الأشعرية: إن هذه الصفات كلها مخلوقة. وقالوا إنه لم يكن خالقاً ما لم يخلق الخلق، ولم يكن رازقاً ما لم يرزق الخلق، إلا أنا نقول: يجوز أن يُسمى خالقاً وإن لم يخلق الخلق، ويسمى رازقًا وإن لم يرزق الخلق. الا يُرى أن واحداً منا إذا كان قادراً على الخياطة يسمى خياطاً وإن لم يوجد منه الخياطة، كذلك ههنا الله تعالى لما كان قادراً على التخليق والترزيق يسمى خالقاً ورازقاً، ألا يُرى أن الله تعالى سمّي نفسه مالك يوم الدين وإن لم يخلق يوم الدين، لكن لما كان قادرًا على تخليقه وإيجاده سمَّى نفسه بذلك الاسم، كذا ههنا، إلا أن هذا الجواب ليس بمتين.
As for attributes of action, such as creating, providing, bestowing, granting favors, doing good, mercy, forgiveness, and guidance, all of them are eternal, neither He nor other, as previously mentioned. The Ashʿariyya said: All of these attributes are created. And they said: He was not a creator until He created creation, and He was not a provider until He provided for creation. But we say: It is permissible to call Him creator even if He has not created creation, and to call Him provider even if He has not provided for creation. Do you not see that one of us, when he is capable of sewing, is called a tailor even if he has not performed sewing? Similarly here, God, exalted, because He is capable of creating and providing, is called Creator and Provider. Do you not see that God called Himself Master of the Day of Judgment even though He has not created the Day of Judgment, but because He is capable of creating it and bringing it into existence, He called Himself by that name. The same applies here. However, this answer is not sound.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage first reiterates that attributes of action are eternal, neither He nor other. Then it presents the Ash'ari view that these attributes are created, with the argument that God was not Creator until He created. The author responds with an analogy: a capable tailor is called tailor even before sewing, likewise God is called Creator and Provider due to ability. The author also gives analogy of 'Master of the Day of Judgment'. However, he admits this answer is not strong (ليس بمتين). This shows a critical attitude.

Proof That Attributes of Essence Are Eternal
Imam Nasafi proves the eternity of attributes of essence via objection and answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Nasafi's proof that the attributes of essence (power, knowledge, life) are eternal and beginningless, not acquired in time.

Core Idea
If God acquired power later, He would have been powerless before—an impossibility.

Imam Nasafi now defends the eternity of God's essential attributes. An objector asks for proof, and the Imam answers by tracing the logical consequences of denying pre-eternal attributes. The argument shows that temporal acquisition of attributes leads to absurd descriptions of God.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the question: 'What is the proof that these attributes are eternal?' Then follow the answer's reasoning.
2
Follow the reductio ad absurdum
Identify how Imam Nasafi uses a chain of 'how could' questions to show that denying pre-eternal power leads to describing God with incapability and ignorance.
The argument assumes that attributes like power and knowledge cannot be created after being absent, because that would imply prior deficiency.
3
Connect to the conclusion
Understand why the conclusion is that these attributes must be eternal (qadīmāt, azaliyyāt) and that any other view is impossible (mumtaniʿ).
4
Reflect on the method
Notice the method: logical demonstration by contradiction. This is a common tool in kalam to establish divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : ما الدليل على أن هذه الصفات قديمات أزليَّات؟ قلنا لهم: لأن الله تعالى لو لم يكن قادرًا في الأزل كيف قدر حين خلق القدرة؟ وكيف قدر حين خلق الحياة والسمع والبصر ؟ وكيف عَلِمَ حين خلق العلم؟ فيؤدي إلى أن يوصف الله تعالى بالعجز قبل ذلك، وبالجهل قبل ذلك، وهذا ممتنع والهادي هو الله.
For it is said: What is the proof that these attributes are eternal (qadīmāt), beginningless (azaliyyāt)? We said to them: Because if God Most High were not powerful (qādir) in pre-eternity (al-azal), how could He have power when He created power? And how could He have power when He created life, hearing, and sight? And how could He know when He created knowledge? This leads to describing God Most High with incapability (ʿajz) before that, and with ignorance (jahl) before that, and this is impossible (mumtaniʿ). And the Guide (al-hādī) is God.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer format. The objector asks for the proof that the attributes (specifically attributes of essence like power, knowledge, life) are eternal and beginningless. The answer employs a reductio ad absurdum: if God were not powerful in pre-eternity (al-azal), then He would have become powerful only after creating power, implying He was powerless before. Similarly, He would have known only after creating knowledge, implying ignorance beforehand. This leads to attributing deficiency to God, which is impossible. Therefore, the attributes must be eternal, subsisting in God's essence from eternity.

Key Terms
صفات
قديمات
أزلي
قادر
عجز
جهل
ممتنع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God's power is acquired after creating power, as if He becomes powerful only when He creates power.
Correction: If that were so, God would have been powerless before creating power, which is impossible. Hence, God must be eternally powerful, and the attribute of power is eternal.

Checkpoint
What would God be described with if He were not powerful in pre-eternity?
A) Power and knowledge
B) Incapability and ignorance
C) Mercy and forgiveness
D) Creation and provision

Key Takeaway
God's essential attributes must be eternal because acquiring them in time would imply prior deficiency, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God has always been perfect in all His attributes, never lacking? How does your daily worship reflect that conviction, or does it reveal a subtle assumption that God needs something from you? Commit to one action today that affirms His eternal perfection.
?
If God's power is eternal and independent of creation, why do you still rely on your own strength instead of His in moments of difficulty? Identify one situation where you acted as if God's power were limited, and write a prayer asking for reliance on Him.
?
The proof shows that attributing deficiency to God is impossible. Where in your heart do you still harbor a thought or feeling that contradicts God's perfection—perhaps in impatience with His decree or doubt in His wisdom? Repent from that thought now and recite an attribute of His that counters it.

Attributes of Action: Eternal and Subsisting in God's Essence
Nasafi refutes Ash'ari view on attributes of action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Maturidi school holds that attributes of action are eternal and subsist in God's essence, and how the correct argument differs from a weaker analogy-based response.

Core Idea
God's attributes of action are eternal because they subsist in His essence, not merely because He has power over them.

Imam al-Nasafi presents a detailed argument about the nature of God's attributes of action. While the Ash'aris claim these attributes are created, the Maturidi position maintains they are eternal. The author first offers a preliminary rebuttal using analogies, but then declares it insufficient and provides the definitive theological proof: these attributes must subsist in God's essence from pre-eternity, otherwise God's essence would be subject to change.

Guided Path
1
Identify the positions on attributes
Read the passage and note the Maturidi claim that attributes of action are eternal and the Ash'ari claim that they are created.
The Ash'ari view is based on the idea that God is not called creator until He creates.
2
Examine the weak answer
Study the analogy of the tailor and the naming of 'Master of the Day of Judgment'. Why does the author say this answer is not solid?
Analogies have limitations in theological reasoning.
3
Understand the correct answer
Focus on the argument that if attributes were not eternal in God's essence, then His essence would be a locus of temporal events. Grasp why this is impossible.
This is a logical proof based on God's immutability.
4
Apply the lesson
Consider how this argument defends the Maturidi position on divine attributes against the Ash'ari objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما صفات الفعل كالتخليق، والترزيق، والإفضال، والإنعام، والإحسان، والرحمة والمغفرة، والهداية، فكلها قديمات أزليات لا هو ولا غيره على ما مر. وقالت الأشعرية: إن هذه الصفات كلها مخلوقة. وقالوا إنه لم يكن خالقاً ما لم يخلق الخلق، ولم يكن رازقاً ما لم يرزق الخلق، إلا أنا نقول: يجوز أن يُسمى خالقاً وإن لم يخلق الخلق، ويسمى رازقًا وإن لم يرزق الخلق. ألا يُرى أن واحداً منا إذا كان قادراً على الخياطة يسمى خياطاً وإن لم يوجد منه الخياطة، كذلك ههنا الله تعالى لما كان قادراً على التخليق والترزيق يسمى خالقاً ورازقاً، ألا يُرى أن الله تعالى سمّى نفسه مالك يوم الدين وإن لم يخلق يوم الدين، لكن لما كان قادرًا على تخليقه وإيجاده سمَّى نفسه بذلك الاسم، كذا ههنا، إلا أن هذا الجواب ليس بمتين. والجواب الصحيح أن نقول: هذه الصفات قائمة بذات الله تعالى في الأزل، لأنها لو لم تكن قائمة بذات الله تعالى في الأزل لكانت ذات الباري محلاً للحوادث، وهذا ممتنع والله الهادي.
And as for attributes of action (ṣifāt al-fiʿl), such as creating (takhlīq), providing (tarzīq), bestowing (ifḍāl), granting favors (inʿām), beneficence (iḥsān), mercy (raḥma) and forgiveness (maghfira), and guidance (hidāya), all of them are eternal (qadīmāt), beginningless (azaliyyāt), neither He nor other than Him as has passed. And the Ashʿariyya said: All of these attributes are created (makhluqa). And they said: He was not a creator (khāliq) until He created the creation, and He was not a provider (rāziq) until He provided for the creation. But we say: It is permissible that He be called creator even if He has not created the creation, and be called provider even if He has not provided for the creation. Do you not see that if one of us is capable of sewing, he is called a tailor (khayyāṭ) even if sewing has not occurred from him? Similarly here, since God Most High is capable of creating and providing, He is called creator and provider. Do you not see that God Most High named (sammā) Himself Master of the Day of Judgment (mālik yawm al-dīn) even though He has not created the Day of Judgment, but because He is capable of its creation and bringing into existence, He named Himself with that name? Likewise here. However, this answer is not solid (matīn). And the correct answer (al-jawāb al-ṣaḥīḥ) is that we say: These attributes subsist (qāʾima) in the essence (dhāt) of God Most High in pre-eternity (al-azal), because if they did not subsist in the essence of God Most High in pre-eternity, then the essence (dhāt) of the Creator (al-bārī) would be a locus of temporal events (maḥall li-l-ḥawādith), and this is impossible (mumtaniʿ). And God is the Guide (al-hādī).
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage discusses the divine attributes of action (ṣifāt al-fiʿl) such as creating, providing, etc. The author first asserts that these attributes are eternal and beginningless, just like the attributes of essence. He then presents the Ashʿarī position that these attributes are created (makhluqa), arguing that God is not called creator until He actually creates. The author offers a preliminary rebuttal: a person can be called a 'tailor' based on capability alone, even before sewing. Similarly, God called Himself 'Master of the Day of Judgment' before that day exists, based on His power to create it. However, the author immediately deems this answer weak (laysa bi-matīn). He then provides the correct answer: these attributes must subsist in God's essence in pre-eternity. If they were not eternal, God's essence would be a locus for temporal events (maḥall li-l-ḥawādith), which is impossible because God's essence is immutable. Therefore, the attributes of action are eternal and uncreated.

محلاً للحوادث
أشعرية

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Attributes of action are created because they relate to created acts.
Correction: They are eternal because they subsist in God's essence; otherwise His essence would be a locus of temporal events, which is impossible.

The Pre-eternal Subsistence of Divine Attributes
Nasafi states the correct position and provides a proof by impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Islamic theologians affirm that God's attributes subsist in His essence from pre-eternity, and how denying this leads to an impossible consequence.

Core Idea
God's attributes are co-eternal with His essence, not separate entities.

In this passage, Imam Nasafi presents the correct answer to a key theological question: Are God's attributes eternal or created? He argues that they must be subsisting in God's essence from pre-eternity, because otherwise God's essence would become a receptacle for temporal things, which is impossible. This concise proof grounds the orthodox Sunni position on divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's rhythm come through.
2
Identify the key claim
Notice the statement: 'These attributes subsist in the essence of God the Exalted in pre-eternity.' This is the position being defended.
The phrase 'قائمة بذات الله' is central.
3
Analyze the proof
Follow the conditional argument: if the attributes were not pre-eternally subsisting, then God's essence would be a locus for temporally occurring things (hawadith). That is impossible, so the attributes must subsist from eternity.
This is a proof by contradiction (reductio ad absurdum).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب الصحيح أن نقول: هذه الصفات قائمة بذات الله تعالى في الأزل، لأنها لو لم تكن قائمة بذات الله تعالى في الأزل لكانت ذات الباري محلاً للحوادث، وهذا ممتنع. والله الهادي.
The correct answer is to say: These attributes subsist in the essence of God the Exalted in pre-eternity (al-azal), because if they were not subsisting in the essence of God the Exalted in pre-eternity, then the essence of the Creator would be a locus (mahall) for temporally occurring things (al-hawadith), and this is impossible. And God is the Guide.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage is a conclusive answer to an objection concerning the divine attributes. The author states the correct position: the attributes are co-eternal with God's essence and subsist in it from pre-eternity (al-azal). He provides a proof: if the attributes were not pre-eternally subsisting in God's essence, then His essence would become a receptacle for originated things (hawadith), i.e., accidents that come into existence after not existing. But God's essence cannot be a locus for accidents because that would entail change and temporal origination in the divine, which is impossible. Thus the attributes must be eternal. The passage ends with "And God is the Guide" — a common invocatory closing.

Key Terms

قائمة بذات الله
الأزل
محل للحوادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that if God's attributes are eternal, they are separate from God or exist independently.
Correction: But this passage affirms that they subsist in His essence, not separate.

Checkpoint

A) Because the attributes are created.
B) Because otherwise God's essence would be a locus for originated things, which is impossible.
C) Because God is eternal.
D) Because the attributes are identical with His essence.

Key Takeaway
The pre-eternal subsistence of attributes in God's essence is necessary to avoid making His essence a locus for temporal things.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Permissibility of Calling Allah 'Mawjud'
Imam al-Razi's argument for the permissibility of calling Allah 'Mawjud'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the reasoning behind the permissibility of calling Allah 'Mawjud' (the Existent), based on the twofold meaning of existence and the authority of consensus.

Core Idea
Calling Allah 'Mawjud' is valid by consensus and carries praise more than the word 'thing'.

This lesson examines a theological footnote that argues for the permissibility of using the term 'Mawjud' (existent) for Allah. The argument hinges on distinguishing two meanings of existence—perception and self-subsistence—and appeals to both Quranic usage and the consensus of Muslims. An objection is raised and answered, showing that 'Mawjud' indeed conveys praise. Understanding this discussion clarifies how technical terms in kalam are grounded in revelation and communal agreement.

Guided Path
1
Examine the two meanings of 'wujud'
Notice that the passage first establishes that 'wujud' has two distinct senses: finding/perception and actuality/self-subsistence. This distinction is crucial for the argument that follows.
The dual meaning is a key premise in Imam al-Razi's reasoning.
2
Identify the two aspects of applying 'Mawjud' to Allah
The passage then applies this distinction to Allah: He can be called 'Mawjud' either because He is known/perceived or because He is real in Himself. Recognize how each aspect is supported.
The first aspect is supported by a Quranic verse; the second is implied.
3
Understand the role of consensus
The primary proof given is the consensus (ijma') of Muslims that 'Mawjud' is a permissible name for Allah. This overrides any initial hesitation about the term's apparent lack of praise.
Consensus is a powerful source of authority in Islamic theology.
4
Analyze the objection and its response
An imagined objector claims that Allah's names must indicate praise, and 'Mawjud' seems neutral. The response first cites consensus, then argues that 'Mawjud' actually conveys more praise than the word 'thing' because it excludes non-existence and implies universal cognizability.
This comparison with 'thing' is a clever rhetorical move.
5
Reflect on the lesson's significance
Consider how this discussion shows the careful reasoning behind accepted divine names. It also illustrates how kalam balances linguistic analysis, revelation, and communal practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورد بالنسخة (ج) تعليق صورته الأول: أي الذي لا بداية لأوليته، والآخر: أي الباقي بعد فناء خليقته ولا نهاية لآخريته، فمنه الأمر بدأ وإليه يعود، وهو المقصود في مراتب الوجود». «شرح الحصن الحصين لعلي (قاري. قلت: يقول الإمام الرازي في تفسيره» (۱ : ۱٥۸) ما ملخصه: «المسألة الثانية: في بيان أنه هل يجوز إطلاق لفظ الموجود على الله تعالى؟ اعلم أن هذا البحث يجب أن يكون مسبوقاً بمقدمة أن لفظ «الوجود» يقال بالاشتراك على معيين: أحدهما: أن يُراد بالوجود الوجدان والإدراك : وهي والشعور .. والثاني: أن يراد بالوجود الحصول والتحقق في نفسه.... إذا عرفت هذه المقدمة فنقول: إطلاق لفظ الموجود على الله تعالى يكون على وجهين أحدهما كونه معلومًا مشعورًا به، والثاني: كونه في نفسه ثابتًا متحققا، أما بحسب المعنى الأول فقد جاء في القرآن قال الله تعالى: ﴿لَوَجَدُوا الله ﴾ [النساء: ٦٤] ...، ثم نقول: ثبت بإجماع المسلمين إطلاق هذا الاسم فوجب القول به. فإن قالوا: ألستم قلتم : إن أسماء الله تعالى يجب كونها دالة على المدح والثناء؟ ولفظ الموجود لا يفيد ذلك. قلنا: عدلنا عن هذا الدليل بدلالة الإجماع، وأيضا فدلالة لفظ الموجود على المدح أكثر من دلالة لفظ الشيء عليه، وبيانه من وجوه الأول: أنه عند قوم يقع لفظ الشيء على المعدوم كما يقع على الموجود، أما الموجود فإنه لا يقع على المعدوم البتة، فكان إشعار هذا اللفظ بالمدح أولى. الثاني: أن لفظ الموجود بمعنى المعلوم يفيد صفة المدح والثناء، لأنه يفيد أن بسبب كثرة الدلائل على وجوده وإلهيته صار كأنه معلوم لكل أحد موجود عند كل أحد، واجب الإقرار به عند كل عقل، فهذا اللفظ أفاد المدح والثناء من هذا الوجه، فظهر الفرق بينه وبين لفظ الشيء».
It was reported in copy (ج) a comment, its form: al-Awwal: that which has no beginning to its firstness, and al-Akhir: the one remaining after the annihilation of His creation and no end to its lastness, from Him the matter begins and to Him it returns, and He is the purpose in the levels of existence. 'Sharh al-Husn al-Hasin' by Ali al-Qari. He said: al-Imam al-Razi says in his tafsir (1:158) a summary: The second issue: on the statement of whether it is permissible to apply the word 'mawjud' (existing) to Allah ta'ala. Know that this inquiry must be preceded by a premise that the word 'wujud' (existence) is used homonymously for two meanings: one: that by existence is meant finding (wijdan) and perception (idrak): that is, awareness... and the second: that by existence is meant actuality (husul) and self-subsistence (tahaqquq) in itself.... When you have understood this premise, we say: Applying the word 'mawjud' to Allah ta'ala has two aspects: one: His being known and perceived, the second: His being in Himself established and real. As for the first meaning, it has come in the Quran, Allah said: 'they would have found Allah' [4:64]... Then we say: It is established by the consensus of the Muslims to apply this name, so it is obligatory to say it. If they say: Did you not say that the names of Allah must indicate praise and commendation? And the word 'mawjud' does not convey that. We say: We have shifted from this proof by the indication of consensus. Also, the indication of the word 'mawjud' for praise is more than the indication of the word 'thing' (shay') for it, and its explanation is from several aspects: First: that according to some, the word 'thing' falls upon the non-existent as it falls upon the existent, whereas 'mawjud' never falls upon the non-existent, so the connotation of this word for praise is more appropriate. Second: that the word 'mawjud' in the sense of 'known' conveys the attribute of praise and commendation, because it conveys that due to the abundance of proofs of His existence and divinity, He has become as if He is known to everyone, existing for everyone, obligatory to acknowledge by every intellect. So this word conveys praise and commendation from this aspect. Thus the difference between it and the word 'thing' becomes clear.'
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage is a textual note and commentary from a later source (likely an editor's footnote) explaining the permissibility of calling Allah 'Mawjud' (existent). It begins with a gloss on the names al-Awwal and al-Akhir from the previous lesson. Then it quotes Imam al-Razi's argument. The argument starts with a premise: the word 'wujud' has two meanings – (1) finding/perception, and (2) actuality/self-subsistence. Applying 'Mawjud' to Allah can mean either that He is known/perceived or that He is real in Himself. The first meaning is supported by a Qur'anic verse. Then the main proof: consensus (ijma') of Muslims establishes that 'Mawjud' is a permissible name for Allah, so it must be used. An objection is raised: Allah's names should indicate praise, but 'Mawjud' seemingly does not. The answer: first, consensus overrides that principle; second, 'Mawjud' does indicate praise more than the word 'thing' because 'Mawjud' only applies to existents, not non-existents, and because it implies that Allah is known to all due to clear proofs.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that calling Allah 'Mawjud' is impermissible because it seems too generic or does not praise Him.
Correction: The passage argues that consensus establishes its permissibility, and further, 'Mawjud' actually conveys praise more than the word 'thing' because it is exclusive to existents and implies that Allah is universally known.

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, what is the primary proof that it is permissible to call Allah 'mawjud'?
A) The Quranic verses.
B) Consensus of the Muslims.
C) Logical necessity.
D) The fact that it indicates praise.

Key Takeaway
Calling Allah 'Mawjud' is not only permissible but also conveys praise, as established by consensus and by its superiority over the term 'thing'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The objection that 'Mawjud' lacks praise reflects a desire for God's names to feel impactful. In your own supplication, do you ever feel that describing Allah as 'the Existent' is too plain? How does al-Razi's argument challenge you to see even the simplest descriptions as profound praise?
?
The consensus of the Muslims established this name. Are you willing to submit your personal theological hesitations to the collective understanding of the early community, even when a term feels unfamiliar or insufficient? What does that submission cost you?

The Meaning of Divine Oneness
Nasafi defines divine oneness and proves it is non-numerical.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Allah's oneness (tawhid) means absolute simplicity without parts or division, and that it is not numerical oneness.

Core Idea
Allah's Oneness means no parts, not counting to one.

In this passage, Imam Nasafi begins with a familiar claim—Allah is One—but immediately deepens what that means. He cites Quranic verses and then provides a precise definition that guards against a common misunderstanding: that Allah's oneness is like a numerical unit. Instead, it is the oneness of indivisible simplicity. The proof shows why numerical oneness would lead to absurdity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully to grasp the flow from Quranic evidence to definition to proof.
Notice the use of Quranic verses as authority.
2
Identify the key definition
Find the phrase that defines what 'One' means when said about Allah. What does it exclude?
The definition is 'the existent that has no part and no division in its essence'.
3
Understand the proof
Follow the logical argument: if Allah were one in number, He would have parts; parts would each be creators, which is impossible. Therefore, His oneness is not numerical.
This is a proof by contradiction (burhan al-tamthīl).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل [ في معنى الوحدانية ] ويجوز أن يقال بأن الله تعالى واحد وبه ورَدَ النَّصُّ وهو قوله تعالى: ﴿وَإِلهُكُمْ إِلَهٌ وَاحِدٌ﴾ [البقرة: ١٦٣] وقوله: ﴿قُلْ هُوَ اللهُ أَحَدٌ﴾ [الإخلاص : ١]. ومعنى الواحد الموجود الذي لا بعض ولا انقسام لذاته، فإن الله تعالى واحدٌ لا من جهة العدد يدل عليه أنه لو لم يكن واحدًا لا من جهة العدد لكان أبعاضًا، فامتنع من أن يكون إلها واحدًا، لأنه يحصل الإحداث والتخليق والاختراع لكل جزء منه، فيؤدي إلى أن يكون كل جزء منه خالقا قادرًا، وهذا محال.
Chapter [On the meaning of Oneness]. It is permissible to say that Allah Almighty is One, and the text has come with it, which is His saying: 'And your God is One God' (Quran 2:163) and His saying: 'Say, He is Allah, the One' (Quran 112:1). And the meaning of 'the One' is the existent that has no part and no division in its essence. Indeed, Allah Almighty is One not in the sense of number; the proof of that is that if He were not One not in the sense of number, He would have parts, and then He would be prevented from being One God, because innovation, creation, and invention would occur for each part of Him, leading to each part being a creator, capable, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof of divine oneness. It begins by citing Qur'anic evidence for Allah being one. Then it provides a precise definition: 'one' means having no parts or division. The author then argues that Allah's oneness is not numerical oneness, because numerical oneness implies the possibility of parts. The proof is by contradiction: if Allah had parts, each part would have creative power, leading to multiple creators, which is absurd. Therefore Allah is one in the sense of indivisible simplicity.

وحدانية
لا بعض ولا انقسام
لا من جهة العدد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Allah's oneness means He is one in number, like a single object among possible objects.
Correction: The passage clarifies that Allah's oneness is non-numerical; He has no parts or division, so His oneness is absolute simplicity.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the meaning of 'al-wahid' when applied to Allah?
A) One in number
B) One who has no parts or division
C) One who is the first
D) One who is unique

Key Takeaway
Allah's oneness means He has no parts and is not numerical; any division would imply multiple creators.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah has no parts, what does that mean for how you understand His attributes like mercy and power? Are you ever tempted to treat one attribute as separate from another, as if He were composed? How can you correct that in your worship?
?
The proof shows that if Allah had parts, each part would be a creator. How does this absolute singularity of Allah challenge your tendency to rely on created things or people for what only He can give? What is one specific area where you need to trust Allah alone starting today?

Defending the Name 'al-Mawjud' for Allah
Nasafi defends 'al-Mawjud' using consensus and rational arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the name 'al-Mawjud' (the Existent) is valid for Allah, how consensus overrides the criterion of praise, and how it actually conveys more praise than the name 'shay'.

Core Idea
Consensus confirms 'al-Mawjud' as a divine name, and it is more praiseworthy than 'thing'.

Imam Nasafi continues his discussion on divine names by addressing the name 'al-Mawjud'. Critics may argue that this name does not convey praise, but Nasafi shows that consensus establishes its validity, and furthermore, it carries a special praiseworthiness that other names lack. This passage handles both the objection and the positive proof.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two meanings of existence
Read the first part where Nasafi splits 'al-Mawjud' into two senses: known/perceived and real/established. The first sense is Qur'anic.
Notice that the Qur'anic proof only covers the first meaning.
2
See the consensus argument
Nasafi then appeals to ijma' (consensus) to justify the name fully. This overrides any doubt about its praiseworthiness.
Consensus is a strong proof in Islamic theology.
3
Understand the objection
The objector says: 'al-Mawjud' does not seem to be a praise name. Nasafi acknowledges this but uses consensus to go beyond that criterion.
The objection is hypothetical but important.
4
Analyze the two reasons for praiseworthiness
Nasafi gives two reasons why 'al-Mawjud' is actually more praiseworthy than 'shay'. First, 'shay' can apply to non-existents; second, 'al-Mawjud' implies universal recognition.
Focus on how each reason connects to praise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا عرفت هذه المقدمة فنقول: إطلاق لفظ الموجود على الله تعالى يكون على وجهين أحدهما كونه معلومًا مشعورًا به، والثاني: كونه في نفسه ثابتًا متحققا، أما بحسب المعنى الأول فقد جاء في القرآن قال الله تعالى: ﴿لَوَجَدُوا الله﴾ [النساء: ٦٤] ...، ثم نقول: ثبت بإجماع المسلمين إطلاق هذا الاسم فوجب القول به. فإن قالوا: ألستم قلتم : إن أسماء الله تعالى يجب كونها دالة على المدح والثناء؟ ولفظ الموجود لا يفيد ذلك. قلنا: عدلنا عن هذا الدليل بدلالة الإجماع، وأيضا فدلالة لفظ الموجود على المدح أكثر من دلالة لفظ الشيء عليه، وبيانه من وجوه الأول: أنه عند قوم يقع لفظ الشيء على المعدوم كما يقع على الموجود، أما الموجود فإنه لا يقع على المعدوم البتة، فكان إشعار هذا اللفظ بالمدح أولى. الثاني: أن لفظ الموجود بمعنى المعلوم يفيد صفة المدح والثناء، لأنه يفيد أن بسبب كثرة الدلائل على وجوده وإلهيته صار كأنه معلوم لكل أحد موجود عند كل أحد، واجب الإقرار به عند كل عقل، فهذا اللفظ أفاد المدح والثناء من هذا الوجه، فظهر الفرق بينه وبين لفظ الشيء.
When you have understood this premise, we say: Applying the word 'al-mawjud' to Allah Almighty is in two ways: one is that He is known and perceived, and the second is that He is in Himself established and realized. As for the first meaning, it has come in the Qur'an; Allah Almighty said: 'They would have found Allah' (Quran 4:64)... Then we say: It is established by the consensus of the Muslims to apply this name, so it is obligatory to say it. If they say: Did you not say that the names of Allah Almighty must indicate praise and commendation, and the word 'al-mawjud' does not convey that? We say: We have turned away from this proof due to the indication of consensus, and also the indication of the word 'al-mawjud' to praise is greater than the indication of the word 'thing' upon it. Its clarification is in several ways: First, that among some people the word 'thing' applies to the non-existent just as it applies to the existent, whereas 'al-mawjud' does not apply to the non-existent at all, so the implication of this word for praise is more appropriate. Second, that the word 'al-mawjud' in the sense of 'the known' conveys the attribute of praise and commendation, because it conveys that due to the abundance of proofs of His existence and divinity, He becomes as if known to everyone, existent to everyone, and obligatory to affirm by every intellect. So this word conveys praise and commendation from this aspect, so the difference between it and the word 'thing' becomes clear.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage is a defense of using the name 'al-Mawjud' (the Existent) for Allah. It starts by distinguishing two meanings of 'existence': as known/perceived and as real. The first meaning is found in the Qur'an. Then it appeals to consensus (ijma') to justify the name. The anticipated objection is that divine names must be praiseworthy, but 'al-Mawjud' does not seem to praise. The author answers that consensus overrides this criterion, and then provides two reasons why 'al-Mawjud' is actually more praiseworthy than 'thing' (shay'): first, 'thing' can apply to non-existents, while 'al-Mawjud' cannot; second, 'al-Mawjud' in its cognitive sense implies universal recognition due to abundant proofs, which is itself praiseworthy.

?

Have you ever stopped to consider that your very existence is evidence of His existence? How might your awareness of being 'mawjud' by His will change your next action today?
