The Rational Necessity of Calling Allah 'Thing'
Nasafi's rational argument for calling Allah 'thing' and response to Mu'attila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why affirming that Allah is a 'thing' is rationally necessary to avoid complete negation (ta'til).

Core Idea
Denying that Allah is a thing leads to nothingness; affirming thingness saves from ta'til.

This lesson presents the rational argument for why it is permissible and even necessary to refer to Allah as a 'thing' (shay'). The Mu'attila sect forbade it to avoid anthropomorphism, but their fear leads to a worse error: complete denial of Allah's existence and attributes. Nasafi shows that the opposite of 'thing' is 'nothing', so rejecting thingness implies nothingness.

Guided Path
1
Read the rational argument
Read the first part of the passage where Nasafi gives the logical proof: if we do not affirm thingness, we fall into ta'til. Notice the key premise: 'the opposite of a thing is nothing'.
2
Understand the Mu'attila position
The Mu'attila forbid calling Allah 'thing' to avoid tashbih (anthropomorphism). Reflect on why they would think that saying 'thing' implies similarity to created things.
3
Compare the two errors
Nasafi implies that ta'til (denial) is worse than tashbih (likening). The rational necessity of affirming thingness overrides the fear of tashbih. Try to grasp the priority: avoiding complete negation is more important.
This is a foundational principle in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل ويجوز أن يقال بأن الله تعالى شيء، لأنا لو لم نثبت أنه شيء يلزمنا التعطيل، لأن ضد الشَّيء لا شيء، ومن ضرورة نَفْي التعطيل إثباتُ الشيء. وقالت المعطلة : لا يجوز أن يقال بأن الله تعالى شيء فرارًا من التشبيه. فإن قيل: وفي الخبر: «إنَّ الله تعالى تسعة وتسعين اسما فمن أحصاها دخل الجنة» ونحن قد أحصيناها فلم نجد منه «الشيء» والجواب عنه أن نقول: الله سمّى نفسه شيئًا، قال تعالى: ﴿قُلْ أَيُّ شَيْءٍ أَكْبَرُ شَهَدَةً قُلِ اللهُ شَهِيدُ بَيْنِي وَبَيْنَكُمْ﴾ [الأنعام: ۱۹] فثبَتَ أنه يجوز إطلاق اسم الشيء على الله تعالى.
Chapter. And it is permissible to say that Allah Almighty is a thing, because if we do not affirm that He is a thing, we would fall into ta'til (denial of attributes), since the opposite of a thing is nothing, and from the necessity of negating ta'til is affirming thingness. The Mu'attila said: It is not permissible to say that Allah Almighty is a thing, fleeing from tashbih (anthropomorphism). So if it is said: In the hadith: 'Indeed, Allah has ninety-nine names; whoever enumerates them will enter Paradise' and we have enumerated them and did not find among them 'thing'. The answer to that is that we say: Allah named Himself a thing, He said: 'Say: What thing is greatest in testimony? Say: Allah is witness between me and you' (Quran 6:19). So it is established that it is permissible to apply the name 'thing' to Allah Almighty.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage argues for the permissibility of calling Allah 'thing' (shay'). It first gives a rational argument: denying that Allah is a thing leads to ta'til (complete negation of attributes), because the opposite of a thing is nothing, and we must affirm something. Then it addresses the Mu'attila who forbid it to avoid tashbih. Then it answers a scriptural objection: the hadith of 99 names does not include 'shay'', so some argue it is not a name. The answer is that the Qur'an itself calls Allah 'shay'' in Surah Al-An'am (6:19), so it is a valid name. This shows that the 99 names are not exhaustive, and a name can be established by Qur'anic usage.

Key Terms
شيء
تعطيل
تشبيه
معطلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that calling Allah a 'thing' is impermissible because it implies He is like created things.
Correction: The word 'thing' in Arabic (shay') simply refers to something that exists; it does not imply similarity. Denying it leads to complete negation, which is worse.

Checkpoint
According to the rational argument, what is the consequence of not affirming that Allah is a thing?
A) We fall into tashbih (anthropomorphism)
B) We fall into ta'til (complete negation)
C) We fail to enumerate the 99 names
D) We commit shirk

Key Takeaway
Affirming that Allah is a 'thing' is rationally necessary to avoid the complete denial of His existence and attributes (ta'til).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever shy away from affirming something about Allah because you fear it might make Him like creation? How does this lesson challenge you to confront that fear, and what will you do this week to overcome it?
?
When you think about Allah, do you ever reduce Him to an abstract concept or absolute negation? How does affirming that He is a 'thing' protect your belief from falling into emptiness, and how can you remind yourself of this daily?
?
The Mu'attila fled from tashbih but fell into ta'til. Where in your own understanding of Allah might you be overcorrecting, missing the balance between transcendence and affirmation? Commit to one concrete step to restore that balance.

The Qur'anic Proof for Calling Allah 'Thing'
Nasafi's scriptural rebuttal to the objection from the 99 names.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Qur'an itself establishes that 'thing' is a valid name for Allah, and how to answer the objection that it is not among the 99 names.

Core Idea
Allah calls Himself a 'thing' in the Qur'an, so it is a valid name.

In this lesson, we address a scriptural objection to calling Allah a 'thing'. Some argue that since 'thing' is not listed among the 99 names mentioned in a hadith, it cannot be a divine name. Nasafi responds by quoting the Qur'an itself, where Allah refers to Himself as a 'thing' in Surah Al-An'am. This shows that the 99 names are not exhaustive and that divine names can be established by Qur'anic usage.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the part of the passage that presents the objection: the hadith of 99 names does not include 'shay'. Understand why someone might think this makes it impermissible.
The hadith is authentic but does not mention 'shay'
2
Read the answer
Nasafi responds by quoting Q. 6:19 where Allah says 'Ayyu shay'in akbaru shahadah' and then 'Quli Allahu shaheed...'. Notice that Allah describes Himself as a 'thing' in this verse.
3
Reflect on the implication
This shows that the 99 names are not an exhaustive list. Any name or attribute that Allah uses for Himself in the Qur'an or authentic hadith can be used, as long as it is befitting. The argument from silence (the hadith not mentioning 'shay') is invalid because the Qur'an explicitly uses it.
This is a common principle in theology: the Qur'an takes precedence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل ويجوز أن يقال بأن الله تعالى شيء، لأنا لو لم نثبت أنه شيء يلزمنا التعطيل، لأن ضد الشَّيء لا شيء، ومن ضرورة نَفْي التعطيل إثباتُ الشيء. وقالت المعطلة : لا يجوز أن يقال بأن الله تعالى شيء فرارًا من التشبيه. فإن قيل: وفي الخبر: «إنَّ الله تعالى تسعة وتسعين اسما فمن أحصاها دخل الجنة» ونحن قد أحصيناها فلم نجد منه «الشيء» والجواب عنه أن نقول: الله سمّى نفسه شيئًا، قال تعالى: ﴿قُلْ أَيُّ شَيْءٍ أَكْبَرُ شَهَدَةً قُلِ اللهُ شَهِيدُ بَيْنِي وَبَيْنَكُمْ﴾ [الأنعام: ۱۹] فثبَتَ أنه يجوز إطلاق اسم الشيء على الله تعالى.

The answer to that is that we say: Allah named Himself a thing, He said: 'Say: What thing is greatest in testimony? Say: Allah is witness between me and you' (Quran 6:19). So it is established that it is permissible to apply the name 'thing' to Allah Almighty.

This passage argues for the permissibility of calling Allah 'thing' (shay'). It first gives a rational argument: denying that Allah is a thing leads to ta'til (complete negation of attributes), because the opposite of a thing is nothing, and we must affirm something. Then it addresses the Mu'attila who forbid it to avoid tashbih. Then it answers a scriptural objection: the hadith of 99 names does not include 'shay'', so some argue it is not a name. The answer is that the Qur'an itself calls Allah 'shay'' in Surah Al-An'am (6:19), so it is a valid name. This shows that the 99 names are not exhaustive, and a name can be established by Qur'anic usage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that only the 99 names mentioned in the hadith can be used for Allah.
Correction: The 99 names are not an exhaustive list; the Qur'an and authentic hadith contain other names and attributes. The hadith simply enumerates 99 specific names for the purpose of reward.

Checkpoint
What is the main evidence Nasafi uses to prove that 'thing' is a permissible name for Allah?
A) A hadith from the Prophet
B) The consensus of scholars
C) A verse from the Qur'an (Surah Al-An'am)
D) Logical reasoning

Key Takeaway
The Qur'an itself calls Allah a 'thing', so the hadith of 99 names does not preclude using this name.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call upon Allah, do you ever hesitate to use a name from the Qur'an because it seems too ordinary? How does this lesson challenge your assumptions about what is appropriate to say to your Lord?
?
The hadith of 99 names is often recited as a list. Have you ever considered that limiting Allah's names to that list might restrict your intimacy with Him? Will you now seek more names from the Qur'an to deepen your worship?
?
Allah calls Himself a 'thing' — the most basic existent. Does this humble your pride in knowing 'advanced' theology? Will you accept whatever Allah calls Himself, even if it seems simple?

Calling Allah 'Shay': Affirmation or Tashbih?
Nasafi argues for calling Allah 'shay' and refutes those who forbid it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why it is necessary to affirm that Allah is a 'thing' (shay') and how Imam Nasafi refutes the objection that this implies tashbih.

Core Idea
Calling Allah a 'thing' avoids ta'til; it does not imply tashbih.

In this passage, Imam Nasafi addresses a controversial question: can Allah be called a 'thing'? Some groups forbid it for fear of likening Allah to created things. Nasafi argues that refusing to call Allah a 'thing' leads to denying His existence (ta'til), which is far worse. He then responds to the objection that 'thing' is not among the 99 names.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the original Arabic passage once to get a sense of its rhythm, then read the full English translation. Notice the logical flow: permission, proof, objection, rebuttal.
Pay attention to the key terms: شيء, تعطيل, تشبيه.
2
Identify Nasafi's logical argument
Find where Nasafi states that if we do not affirm Allah as a 'thing', ta'til becomes necessary. Understand the logical chain: opposite of thing is nothing → negating ta'til requires affirming thing.
This is a proof by contrapositive.
3
Examine the Mu'attila's objection
The Mu'attila argue that calling Allah a 'thing' leads to tashbih. They also point out that 'thing' is not among the 99 names. Write down both objections.
The Mu'attila are those who strip God of attributes.
4
Analyze Nasafi's Quranic response
Nasafi cites Quran 6:19 where Allah calls Himself 'thing'. How does this refute the objection from the hadith? Reflect on the principle: Allah's own self-naming takes precedence.
The verse shows that 'thing' is used in a non-similarity sense.
5
Synthesize the lesson's takeaway
Summarize in your own words why it is permissible and necessary to call Allah a 'thing', and how this does not imply tashbih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويجوز أن يقال بأن الله تعالى شيء، لأنا لو لم نثبت أنه شيء يلزمنا التعطيل، لأن ضد الشيء لا شيء، ومن ضرورة نفي التعطيل إثبات الشيء. وقالت المعطلة : لا يجوز أن يقال بأن الله تعالى شيء فرارًا من التشبيه. فإن قيل: وفي الخبر: «إن الله تعالى تسعة وتسعين اسما فمن أحصاها دخل الجنة» ونحن قد أحصيناها فلم نجد منه «الشيء». والجواب عنه أن نقول: الله سمى نفسه شيئًا، قال تعالى: ﴿قُلْ أَيُّ شَيْءٍ أَكْبَرُ شَهَادَةً قُلِ اللَّهُ شَهِيدٌ بَيْنِي وَبَيْنَكُمْ﴾ [الأنعام: 19] فثبت أنه يجوز إطلاق اسم الشيء على الله تعالى.
And it is permissible to say that Allah the Exalted is a thing (shay'), because if we do not affirm that He is a thing, then ta'til (denial) would be necessary for us, for the opposite of a thing is nothing (la shay'), and from the necessity of negating ta'til is the affirmation of a thing. And the Mu'attila said: It is not permissible to say that Allah the Exalted is a thing, fleeing from tashbih (anthropomorphism). For if it is said: And in the hadith: 'Indeed Allah the Exalted has ninety-nine names; whoever counts them will enter Paradise.' And we have counted them and did not find among them 'the thing (al-shay')'. The answer to that is that we say: Allah named Himself a thing, He said the Exalted: {Say: 'What thing is greatest in testimony?' Say: 'Allah is witness between me and you.'} [Al-An'am: 19]. So it is established that it is permissible to apply the name 'thing' (al-shay') to Allah the Exalted.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage is a proof and refutation. Imam Nasafi argues for the permissibility of calling Allah 'thing' (shay'). He begins with a logical proof: if we do not affirm that Allah is a thing, then ta'til (denial of His existence or attributes) becomes necessary, because the opposite of a thing is nothing. Since ta'til is invalid (as it negates God's existence), affirming that Allah is a thing is necessary. Then he mentions the objection of the Mu'attila (those who strip God of attributes) who forbid calling Allah a thing out of fear of tashbih (likening God to created things). Next, an objection is raised: the hadith mentions 99 names of Allah, but 'the thing' is not among them, so why use it? Nasafi responds by citing the Qur'an (6:19) where Allah calls Himself 'shay' in the verse 'Say: What thing is greatest in testimony? Say: Allah...' Therefore, it is permissible to use this name because Allah Himself used it. The key point: affirming that Allah is a thing does not imply tashbih; it merely affirms His existence.

Key Terms
شيء
التعطيل
المعطلة
التشبيه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Calling Allah a 'thing' necessarily implies tashbih (likening Him to created things).
Correction: Nasafi shows that calling Allah a 'thing' is necessary to avoid ta'til (denial of existence). The term 'thing' in this context affirms existence, not similarity, as proven by the Quranic verse where Allah calls Himself a 'thing' without any implication of createdness.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason Imam Nasafi gives for the necessity of calling Allah a 'thing'?
A) Because the Mu'attila forbid it.
B) Because otherwise ta'til (denial) would be necessary.
C) Because it is one of the 99 names.
D) Because the word 'thing' implies tashbih.

Key Takeaway
Affirming that Allah is a 'thing' is necessary to avoid ta'til and does not imply tashbih, as affirmed by logical proof and the Quran.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is a thing,' do you feel a tremor of fear that you might be comparing Him to created things? Or do you recognize that your hesitation may actually stem from a subtle denial of His existence? Ask yourself: Am I more afraid of tashbih or of ta'til? Which one does my heart truly guard against? Commit to affirming Allah's existence without reservation, trusting that His own speech guides us.
?
If someone asked you, 'Is Allah a thing?' would your answer reflect the confidence of this lesson, or would you be shy to use that word? Examine your heart: Is there any remnant of embarrassment to call Allah a 'thing' because it seems too ordinary or unworthy? Repent of any subtle denial and embrace the affirmation that He is the Real Thing, the Necessary Existent.
?
The Mu'attila avoided calling Allah a 'thing' out of extreme caution against tashbih. Yet their caution led them to near denial. How often do you, in trying to protect Allah's transcendence, inadvertently diminish or doubt His reality? Reflect on a time you hesitated to attribute something to Allah (like 'He is above the throne') for fear of anthropomorphism. Did that hesitation actually distance you from affirming His existence? Calling Allah 'Nafs': Permissibility and Refutation of Anthropomorphism
Nasafi proves calling Allah 'nafs' is permissible, refuting Mujassimah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ahl al-Sunnah permits calling Allah 'nafs' (essence) and how this refutes the anthropomorphist claim that it implies a body.

Core Idea
Calling Allah 'nafs' means His essence, not a soul or body.

Imam Nasafi addresses a sensitive theological question: can we say Allah is a 'nafs'? Using Qur'anic verses and precise definitions, he shows that 'nafs' simply means essence, not a composite body. The lesson also tackles the objection of the Mujassimah who equate 'nafs' with 'jism'.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the three Qur'anic proofs and the back-and-forth with the Mujassimah.
2
Understand the definition
Nasafi defines 'nafs' as 'dhat' (essence) and 'mawjud' (existent). This is key to the argument.
Avoid the common mistake of thinking 'nafs' means soul here.
3
Analyze the Qur'anic proofs
Identify the three verses and see how each one uses 'nafs' to refer to Allah's essence.
The first two verses have explicit interpretations given by Nasafi.
4
Examine the refutation
Follow Nasafi's response to the Mujassimah: he distinguishes 'nafs' (essence) from 'jism' (composite), showing the objection fails.
Notice the final counter-objection and why the analogy with 'thing not like things' is different.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويجوز أن يقال بأن الله تعالى نفسًا عند أهل السنة والجماعة، لأن النفس تذكر ويراد به الذات والموجود، قال الله تعالى: ﴿وَاصْطَنَعْتُكَ لِنَفْسِي﴾ [طه: 41] أي لذاتي. وقوله تعالى: ﴿وَيُحَذِّرُكُمُ اللَّهُ نَفْسَهُ﴾ [آل عمران: 28] أي ذاته. وقال تعالى: ﴿تَعْلَمُ مَا فِي نَفْسِي وَلَا أَعْلَمُ مَا فِي نَفْسِكَ﴾ [المائدة: 116]. فإن قالت المجسمة: إذا قلتم بالنفس فقد قلنا بالجسم. قلنا: الجسم عبارة عن ذات مركب قابل لصفة العرض، والنفس عبارة عن الذات، ولا يلزم من ضرورة إطلاق اسم النفس عليه إطلاق اسم الجسم عليه. فإن قيل: نحن نقول بأنه جسم لا كالأجسام، كما أنكم تقولون بأنه شيء لا كالأشياء.
And it is permissible to say that Allah the Exalted is a nafs (self/essence) according to Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah, because al-nafs is mentioned and intended by it the essence (al-dhat) and the existent (al-mawjud). Allah the Exalted said: {And I have chosen you for Myself (li-nafsi)} [Taha: 41], meaning for My essence (lidhati). And His saying the Exalted: {And Allah warns you of Himself (nafsahu)} [Al 'Imran: 28], meaning His essence (dhatuhu). And He said the Exalted: {You know what is within my self (nafsi) and I do not know what is within Your self (nafsika)} [Al-Ma'idah: 116]. For if the Mujassimah (anthropomorphists) say: If you affirm the nafs, then you have affirmed the body (jism). We say: The body is an expression of a composite essence (dhat murakkab) capable of receiving the attribute of accident (al-'arad), while the nafs is an expression of the essence (al-dhat). And it does not necessarily follow from the mere application of the name nafs to Him the application of the name body (jism) to Him. For if it is said: We say that He is a body not like bodies, just as you say that He is a thing not like things.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage is a proof and refutation. Imam Nasafi begins by affirming that it is permissible to call Allah 'nafs' (self/essence) according to Ahl al-Sunnah. He explains that 'nafs' here means 'dhat' (essence) or 'mawjud' (existent), not a soul or self in a composite sense. He provides three Qur'anic proofs: (1) 'I have chosen you for Myself' (Taha: 41) – interpreted as 'for My essence'; (2) 'Allah warns you of Himself' (Al 'Imran: 28) – meaning His essence; (3) 'You know what is within my self and I do not know what is within Your Self' (Al-Ma'idah: 116). Then he addresses the objection of the Mujassimah (anthropomorphists) who claim that affirming 'nafs' implies affirming a body (jism). Nasafi refutes this by defining 'jism' as a composite essence capable of receiving accidents, whereas 'nafs' simply means essence without composition. Thus, applying 'nafs' does not necessitate composition or corporeality. Finally, he counters a further objection: the Mujassimah argue that they say 'He is a body not like bodies' just as Ahl al-Sunnah say 'He is a thing not like things'. Nasafi's argument implies that the analogy is invalid because the term 'body' implies composition and accident-receptivity, which are negated from Allah, while 'thing' does not carry such implications.

Key Terms
نفس
الذات
الجسم
المجسمة
العرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Calling Allah 'nafs' means He has a soul or a body.
Correction: In this context, 'nafs' means essence (dhat), not a soul or a composite body.

Checkpoint
How does Imam Nasafi refute the claim that calling Allah 'nafs' implies He is a body?
A) By citing a hadith that Allah is not a body.
B) By defining 'nafs' as essence and 'jism' as composite, showing they are not equivalent.
C) By saying that the Mujassimah are wrong about everything.
D) By stating that 'nafs' is only used metaphorically.

Key Takeaway
Calling Allah 'nafs' is permissible because it refers to His essence, not a body or soul, and this is supported by the Qur'an and sound reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is a thing not like things', do you truly negate any mental image, or does your mind still slip into picturing a form? What specific thought will you correct whenever that image arises?
?
The Mujassimah fell into error by using human analogy. In what areas of your daily life do you subtly compare Allah to His creation—like expecting Him to act as humans do? What is one practical step you will take this week to purify your understanding of Allah's uniqueness?
?
If the Qur'an uses 'nafs' for Allah to mean His essence, how does that affect your recitation of verses like 'And be patient for the judgment of your Lord' and your sense of being in front of Him? Commit to reciting one of these verses daily with the correct understanding.

Why Ahl al-Sunnah Reject Calling Allah 'Nur' (Light)
Nasafi presents the dispute over calling Allah 'Nur' and the Sunni rejection based on tashbih.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ahl al-Sunnah prohibit calling Allah 'Nur' (light) literally, based on the principle of transcendence (tanzih) and the argument that light has a color and implies tashbih.

Core Idea
Light has a color; calling Allah light implies tashbih.

In this passage, Imam Nasafi presents the foundational disagreement between the anthropomorphists (Mushabbiha) and the mainstream Sunni community over the divine name 'Nur'. The anthropomorphists claim Allah is literally a shining light, while the Sunnis reject this absolutely, arguing that light is a created phenomenon with color, and applying it to Allah would compromise His incomparability. Let's examine the Sunni argument and its scriptural basis.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the two contrasting positions introduced: the Mushabbiha and Ahl al-Sunnah.
2
Identify the two positions
The Mushabbiha claim it is permissible to call Allah a shining light. Ahl al-Sunnah say it is not permissible; rather, He is the Creator and Illuminator of light.
3
Analyze the Sunni proof
The Sunni argument is that light has a color. If we say Allah is light, it implies likeness (tashbih) to creation. They cite the verse 'Laysa kamithlihi shay' to affirm transcendence.
Notice that the proof is both rational (color) and scriptural (Quran 42:11).
4
Reflect on the principle of tanzih
Understand that this rejection is rooted in the core Islamic principle that Allah is unlike anything in creation. Calling Allah 'Nur' literally would violate that.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في بيان هل يقال إن الله تعالى نور؟ قالت المشبهة لعنهم الله : يجوز أن يقال بأن الله تعالى نور يتلالاً. وقال أهل السنة والجماعة رضي الله عنهم : لا يجوز ، بل هو خالق النور ومنوّر النور، لأن النور له لون، فلو قلنا بأنه نور يلزمنا التشبيه، والله تعالى منزّه عن التشبيه. قال تعالى: (لَيْسَ كَمِثْلِهِ شَيْءٌ وَهُوَ السَّمِيعُ الْبَصِيرُ ﴾ [الشورى: ١١] وهم احتجوا بقوله تعالى: ﴿اللَّهُ نُورُ السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ ﴾ [النور: ٣٥] سمى نفسه نورا. والجواب عنه أن نقول : قال ابن عباس رضي الله عنهما: يعني منوّر السموات والأرض، وقال بعضهم: يعني هادي أهلِ السَّموات والأرض (۱). (۱) يقول الإمام الصفار في تلخيص الأدلة ص... ص ٤٨٠ ما صورته : ومنها - أي أسماء الله - النور: قال تعالى: ﴿ اللَّهُ نُورُ السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ ﴾ [النور: ٣٥] اعلم بأن النور في العربية - في غير أسماء الله تعالى - شعاع ينافي الظلام، ويُهتدى بالنور في ظلمات البر والبحر، ثم جاء النور اسما لرسول الله كما قال عز وجل: قَدْ جَاءَ كُم مِّنَ اللَّهِ نُورٌ وَكِتَابٌ مُّبِين ﴾ [المائدة: ١٥] فقيل: أراد بالنور محمدًا عليه السلام لأنه يهتدى به من الضلالة. وأما في أسماء الله، فقال بعضهم: لا يجوز إطلاق هذا الاسم على الله لئلا يتوهم متوهم أنه الشعاع الذي ينا في الظلام، ولكن يذكر على سبيل الإضافة كما قال عز وجل: اللَّهُ نُورُ السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ ﴾ وقال بعضهم: يجوز إطلاق هذا الام عليه ومعنى قوله: ﴿اللَّهُ نُورُ السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ ) منور السموات والأرض، وقيل: هادي أهل السماوات والأرض على هذا مذهب أهل الحق .
Section on clarifying whether it is said that Allah is light (nur). The anthropomorphists (al-Mushabbiha), may Allah curse them, said: It is permissible to say that Allah is a shining light. The People of the Sunnah and the Community (Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah), may Allah be pleased with them, said: It is not permissible. Rather, He is the Creator of light and the Illuminator of light, because light has a color. If we say that He is light, then likeness (tashbih) becomes necessary, and Allah is transcendent above likeness. He said: 'There is nothing like unto Him, and He is the All-Hearing, the All-Seeing' (Quran 42:11). And they [the Mushabbiha] argued with His saying: 'Allah is the Light of the heavens and the earth' (Quran 24:35); He named Himself light. The answer to that is to say: Ibn Abbas, may Allah be pleased with them, said: He means the Illuminator of the heavens and the earth. And some said: He means the Guide of the inhabitants of the heavens and the earth. (1) (1) Imam al-Saffar said in Talkhis al-Adillah p. 480: Among them - i.e., among the names of Allah - is al-Nur. He said: 'Allah is the Light of the heavens and the earth' (Quran 24:35). Know that al-nur in Arabic - other than in the names of Allah - is a ray that opposes darkness, and one is guided by light in the darkness of land and sea. Then al-nur came as a name for the Messenger of Allah as He said: 'There has come to you from Allah a light and a clear Book' (Quran 5:15), and it was said: He meant by light Muhammad, peace be upon him, because guidance from misguidance is attained through him. As for the names of Allah, some said: It is not permissible to apply this name to Allah lest some may imagine it is the ray that shines in the darkness, but it is mentioned by way of addition as He said: 'Allah is the Light of the heavens and the earth.' And some said: It is permissible to apply this name to Him, and the meaning of His saying 'Allah is the Light of the heavens and the earth' is the Illuminator of the heavens and the earth, and it was said: the Guide of the inhabitants of the heavens and the earth. This is the position of the People of Truth.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal dispute over the permissibility of calling Allah 'Nur' (light). It begins as a chapter title. Nasafi reports two positions: the Mushabbiha (anthropomorphists) claim that Allah is a shining light, while Ahl al-Sunnah reject this outright. The Sunni argument is that light has a color, so calling Allah light would imply likeness (tashbih), and Allah is beyond likeness. They cite the verse 'Laysa kamithlihi shay' as proof. The Mushabbiha counter with the Quranic verse 'Allahu nur al-samawati wa al-ard' (Allah is the Light of the heavens and the earth), which appears to call Allah light. Nasafi provides the Sunni answer by offering two interpretations from Ibn Abbas and others: the verse means 'Illuminator' or 'Guide.' Then a long footnote from Imam al-Saffar elaborates on the linguistic and theological aspects, noting that nur in non-divine contexts means a physical ray, and that it is used for the Prophet, but for Allah it should only be used in a derivative sense (as 'Illuminator') or not at all unrestrictedly. The footnote also mentions that some scholars permit it with the interpretation of 'Illuminator' or 'Guide,' which is the position of the People of Truth.

Key Terms
النور (al-nur)
التشبيه (al-tashbih)
المشبهة (al-Mushabbiha)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Calling Allah 'Nur' (light) is just a metaphor and does not imply any physical attribute or resemblance to creation.
Correction: According to Nasafi, light has a color and is a created phenomenon. Applying it to Allah without interpretation implies tashbih, which is why Ahl al-Sunnah reject it absolutely unless understood as 'Illuminator' or 'Guide'.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason Ahl al-Sunnah give for rejecting the name 'Nur' for Allah?
A) It is not mentioned in the Quran.
B) Light has a color and would imply tashbih.
C) It is a Christian term.
D) Light is not a perfect attribute.

Key Takeaway
Ahl al-Sunnah reject calling Allah 'Nur' literally because light is a created attribute that would compromise Allah's absolute transcendence (tanzih).

?
Have you ever used a physical description of Allah in your prayers or thoughts without realizing it? What will you do today to purify your conception of Him from any hint of tashbih?
?
The Mushabbiha thought they were honoring Allah by calling Him 'light'. Is there a 'good-sounding' description of Allah that you are attached to, but which might actually limit His transcendence? Will you examine your own supplications tonight and remove any such phrases?
?
The verse 'There is nothing like Him' is a boundary you must not cross. In what area of your life do you act as though Allah is not utterly unique—perhaps comparing Him to human rulers or expecting Him to behave like a person? Refuting the Anthropomorphist Proof from the Quran
Nasafi refutes the anthropomorphist proof from Quran 24:35 with interpretive answers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ahl al-Sunnah interpret the Quranic verse 'Allah is the Light of the heavens and the earth' to avoid anthropomorphism.

Core Idea
The Quranic 'Nur' means 'Illuminator' or 'Guide', not literal light.

The anthropomorphists rely on the famous verse 'Allahu nur al-samawati wa al-ard' to claim that Allah Himself is light. Imam Nasafi provides the orthodox response through the interpretations of Ibn Abbas and others, showing that the verse means Allah is the One who illuminates and guides. This lesson unpacks that rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage: the Mushabbiha cite Quran 24:35, and Nasafi gives the Sunni response with interpretations.
2
Understand Ibn Abbas' interpretation
Ibn Abbas said the verse means 'Munawwir' (Illuminator) of the heavens and earth. This avoids literal light.
3
Learn the alternative interpretation
Some scholars said it means 'Hadi' (Guide) of the inhabitants. Both interpretations steer clear of anthropomorphism.
4
Connect to the principle of tanzih
Understand that the interpretive method (ta'wil) is used to preserve Allah's transcendence when the apparent meaning would imply resemblance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في بيان هل يقال إن الله تعالى نور؟ قالت المشبهة لعنهم الله : يجوز أن يقال بأن الله تعالى نور يتلالاً. وقال أهل السنة والجماعة رضي الله عنهم : لا يجوز ، بل هو خالق النور ومنوّر النور، لأن النور له لون، فلو قلنا بأنه نور يلزمنا التشبيه، والله تعالى منزّه عن التشبيه. قال تعالى: (لَيْسَ كَمِثْلِهِ شَيْءٌ وَهُوَ السَّمِيعُ الْبَصِيرُ ﴾ [الشورى: ١١] وهم احتجوا بقوله تعالى: ﴿اللَّهُ نُورُ السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ ﴾ [النور: ٣٥] سمى نفسه نورا. والجواب عنه أن نقول : قال ابن عباس رضي الله عنهما: يعني منوّر السموات والأرض، وقال بعضهم: يعني هادي أهلِ السَّموات والأرض (۱). (۱) يقول الإمام الصفار في تلخيص الأدلة ص... ص ٤٨٠ ما صورته : ومنها - أي أسماء الله - النور: قال تعالى: ﴿ اللَّهُ نُورُ السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ ﴾ [النور: ٣٥] اعلم بأن النور في العربية - في غير أسماء الله تعالى - شعاع ينافي الظلام، ويُهتدى بالنور في ظلمات البر والبحر، ثم جاء النور اسما لرسول الله كما قال عز وجل: قَدْ جَاءَ كُم مِّنَ اللَّهِ نُورٌ وَكِتَابٌ مُّبِين ﴾ [المائدة: ١٥] فقيل: أراد بالنور محمدًا عليه السلام لأنه يهتدى به من الضلالة. وأما في أسماء الله، فقال بعضهم: لا يجوز إطلاق هذا الاسم على الله لئلا يتوهم متوهم أنه الشعاع الذي ينا في الظلام، ولكن يذكر على سبيل الإضافة كما قال عز وجل: اللَّهُ نُورُ السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ ﴾ وقال بعضهم: يجوز إطلاق هذا الام عليه ومعنى قوله: ﴿اللَّهُ نُورُ السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ ) منور السموات والأرض، وقيل: هادي أهل السماوات والأرض على هذا مذهب أهل الحق .
Section on clarifying whether it is said that Allah is light (nur). The anthropomorphists (al-Mushabbiha), may Allah curse them, said: It is permissible to say that Allah is a shining light. The People of the Sunnah and the Community (Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah), may Allah be pleased with them, said: It is not permissible. Rather, He is the Creator of light and the Illuminator of light, because light has a color. If we say that He is light, then likeness (tashbih) becomes necessary, and Allah is transcendent above likeness. He said: 'There is nothing like unto Him, and He is the All-Hearing, the All-Seeing' (Quran 42:11). And they [the Mushabbiha] argued with His saying: 'Allah is the Light of the heavens and the earth' (Quran 24:35); He named Himself light. The answer to that is to say: Ibn Abbas, may Allah be pleased with them, said: He means the Illuminator of the heavens and the earth. And some said: He means the Guide of the inhabitants of the heavens and the earth. (1) (1) Imam al-Saffar said in Talkhis al-Adillah p. 480: Among them - i.e., among the names of Allah - is al-Nur. He said: 'Allah is the Light of the heavens and the earth' (Quran 24:35). Know that al-nur in Arabic - other than in the names of Allah - is a ray that opposes darkness, and one is guided by light in the darkness of land and sea. Then al-nur came as a name for the Messenger of Allah as He said: 'There has come to you from Allah a light and a clear Book' (Quran 5:15), and it was said: He meant by light Muhammad, peace be upon him, because guidance from misguidance is attained through him. As for the names of Allah, some said: It is not permissible to apply this name to Allah lest some may imagine it is the ray that shines in the darkness, but it is mentioned by way of addition as He said: 'Allah is the Light of the heavens and the earth.' And some said: It is permissible to apply this name to Him, and the meaning of His saying 'Allah is the Light of the heavens and the earth' is the Illuminator of the heavens and the earth, and it was said: the Guide of the inhabitants of the heavens and the earth. This is the position of the People of Truth.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal dispute over the permissibility of calling Allah 'Nur' (light). It begins as a chapter title. Nasafi reports two positions: the Mushabbiha (anthropomorphists) claim that Allah is a shining light, while Ahl al-Sunnah reject this outright. The Sunni argument is that light has a color, so calling Allah light would imply likeness (tashbih), and Allah is beyond likeness. They cite the verse 'Laysa kamithlihi shay' as proof. The Mushabbiha counter with the Quranic verse 'Allahu nur al-samawati wa al-ard' (Allah is the Light of the heavens and the earth), which appears to call Allah light. Nasafi provides the Sunni answer by offering two interpretations from Ibn Abbas and others: the verse means 'Illuminator' or 'Guide.' Then a long footnote from Imam al-Saffar elaborates on the linguistic and theological aspects, noting that nur in non-divine contexts means a physical ray, and that it is used for the Prophet, but for Allah it should only be used in a derivative sense (as 'Illuminator') or not at all unrestrictedly. The footnote also mentions that some scholars permit it with the interpretation of 'Illuminator' or 'Guide,' which is the position of the People of Truth.

Key Terms
النور (al-nur)
منوّر (munawwir)
هادي (hadi)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Quranic verse 'Allah is the Light of the heavens and the earth' literally means Allah is light, so calling Him light is permissible.
Correction: Sunni scholars interpret the verse metaphorically as 'Illuminator' or 'Guide' to avoid anthropomorphism. The literal reading would imply a physical attribute, which contradicts tanzih.

Checkpoint
How did Ibn Abbas interpret the phrase 'Allahu nur al-samawati wa al-ard'?
A) Literally as light
B) As Illuminator (Munawwir)
C) As Guide (Hadi)
D) Both B and C

Key Takeaway
The Quranic verse 'Allah is the Light of the heavens and the earth' is interpreted metaphorically to mean that Allah is the Illuminator and Guide, not literal light.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the verse 'Allah is the Light of the heavens and the earth,' does your heart incline toward a tangible image of light? Are you willing to submit that instinct to the interpretation of the Salaf—that He is the Illuminator and Guide—even if it feels less vivid? What would it cost you to let go of that mental image?
?
The Mushabbiha insisted on a literal reading because it made Allah feel more 'concrete' to them. Have you ever rationalized a misunderstanding about Allah because it made Him feel closer or more relatable? Will you now commit to seeking the interpretation of the righteous predecessors, even when it challenges your emotional comfort?
?
This lesson reveals that even the clearest words of the Quran ('He named Himself light') require careful interpretation to protect Allah's transcendence. What does this teach you about your own tendency to form opinions about Allah without consulting scholars? Will you make a habit of verifying your understanding of His names and attributes with sound knowledge?

The Linguistic Nuance of 'Nur' in Arabic
Imam al-Saffar explains the lexical meaning of 'nur' and the scholarly debate on its usage for Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic background of the word 'nur' in Arabic and the scholarly debate over its permissibility as a divine name.

Core Idea
In Arabic, 'nur' normally means physical light, but it can be used metaphorically for guidance.

Imam al-Saffar's footnote provides a deeper linguistic analysis of the word 'nur'. He explains its original meaning, its application to the Prophet Muhammad, and the scholarly disagreement over whether it can be used as a name for Allah. This lesson unpacks that discussion, showing the Sunni position that it is permissible with interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the footnote from Imam al-Saffar
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. It is a detailed linguistic and theological exposition.
2
Understand the lexical meaning of 'nur'
In non-divine Arabic, 'nur' is a physical ray that opposes darkness and provides guidance.
3
Note the prophetic usage
The Quran also calls the Prophet Muhammad 'nur' (Quran 5:15), meaning guide.
4
Learn the two scholarly opinions on divine name
Some prohibit it absolutely due to risk of misunderstanding; some permit it with the interpretation of 'Munawwir' or 'Hadi'.
5
Conclude with the Sunni position
The position of Ahl al-Haqq (the People of Truth) is that it is permissible with the intended meaning of Illuminator or Guide.

?

?

In what ways have you been treating the Prophet's guidance as merely a nice metaphor rather than the very light that rescues you from darkness? What one step will you take this week to actually follow that guidance in an area where you prefer your own way?
?
Al-Saffar reports two scholarly positions: one prohibits using 'al-Nur' for Allah (except in construct) to avoid misunderstanding, the other permits it with interpretation. Which position feels safer to you? Are you willing to defend the more vulnerable position that permits it because it forces you to constantly interpret and purify your belief? Or do you prefer a prohibition that lets you avoid the hard work of tanzih?

Refuting Literal Interpretation of 'Bi A'yunina'
The author refutes literal interpretation of 'bi a'yunina' citing scholarly opinions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Quranic phrase 'bi a'yunina' cannot be interpreted literally as physical eyes, and how classical scholars interpreted it in a manner befitting Allah's transcendence.

Core Idea
Allah's 'eyes' in the Quran are not literal; they signify His sight and care.

This passage addresses a common anthropomorphic misunderstanding of the Quranic verse 'bi a'yunina'. The author refutes the literal interpretation by pointing out the use of the plural for glorification, not multiplicity, and cites several authoritative salaf interpretations that explain the phrase as referring to Allah's sight, knowledge, command, or protection. Let's explore the reasoning and the various scholarly opinions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main claim
Start by reading the Arabic passage and translation. Notice how the author begins by denying that the verse implies literal eyes.
The author uses a rhetorical question: 'Is it understood that Allah has more than two eyes?'
2
Understand the rhetorical point about the plural form
The author argues that the plural 'a'yun' is used for glorification, not multiplicity. Compare with other Quranic plurals like 'قادرون'.
This is a key linguistic principle in kalam.
3
Examine the salaf interpretations cited
Look at the quotes from al-Qurtubi, al-Rabi' ibn Anas, and Ibn Abbas. Each interprets 'eyes' as sight, preservation, or guarding.
All these interpretations avoid anthropomorphism.
4
Review the alternative interpretations
The passage lists other views: angels, knowledge, command, revelation, assistance. Note how each is a form of divine care.
The variety shows the breadth of orthodox interpretation.
5
Reflect on defending divine transcendence
Consider why all these interpretations are necessary: to protect Allah from having physical limbs or senses.
The passage ends by affirming that Allah is transcendent above senses and modality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في منطوق الآية ولا مفهومها تثنية العين ولا حقيقتها، فمنطوق الآية يتحدث عن العين بصيغة الجمع وليس التثنية، فهل يُفهم من ذلك أن الله أكثر من عينين اثنتين؟ تعالى الله عما يصفون، ثم أنقل هنا بعضًا من كلام السلف المعتبرين لتقف على حقيقة المسألة. يقول الإمام القرطبي رحمه الله في الجامع لأحكام القرءان» (۹: ۳۰) في تفسير هذه الآية ما صورته: أي اعمل السفينة لتركبها أنت ومن آمن معك بأعيننا أي بمرأى منا وحيث نراك ، و ، وقال الا ، الربيع ابن أنس: بحفظنا إياك حفظ من يراك، وقال ابن عباس رضي الله عنهما: بحراستنا، والمعنى واحد، فعبر عن الرؤية بالأعين؛ لأن الرؤية يكون بها، ويكون جمع العين للعظمة لا للتكثير كما قال تعالى: فَنِعْمَ الْقَدِرُونَ ﴾ فَنِعْمَ الْمَهِدُونَ ﴾ ﴿وَإِنَّا لَمُوسِعُونَ ﴾ وقد يرجع معنى الأعين في هذه الآية وغيرها إلى معنى عين كما قال: ﴿وَلِتُصْنَعَ عَلَى عَيْنِي ) وذلك كله عبارةً عن الإدراك والإحاطة، وهو سبحانه منزه عن الحواس والتشبيه والتكييف لا رب غيره ، وقيل : المعنى بِأَعْيُنِنَا أي بأعين ملائكتنا الذين جعلناهم عيونا على حفظك ومعونتك، فيكون الجمع على هذا للتكثير على بابه :وقيل: بِأَعْيُنِنَا أي بعِلْمِنا قاله مقاتل ، وقال الضحاك وسفيان: بأَعْيُنِنَا بأمرنا، وقيل: بوحينا، وقيل: بمعونتنا لك على صنعها. . هذا فيما يتعلق بالآية التي استدل بها الشيخ.
In the explicit wording of the verse nor its implied meaning, there is neither dual form of 'eye' nor its literal reality. The explicit wording of the verse speaks of 'eye' in the plural form, not the dual. Is it understood from that that Allah has more than two eyes? Exalted is Allah above what they describe. Then I quote here some of the statements of the reliable Salaf so that you may know the reality of the matter. Imam al-Qurtubi, may Allah have mercy on him, says in 'al-Jami' li Ahkam al-Qur'an' (9:30) in explanation of this verse, its form: That is, build the ship so that you and those who believe with you may ride in it, 'with Our eyes', meaning under Our sight and where We see you. And al-…, al-Rabi' ibn Anas said: 'With Our preservation of you, the preservation of One Who sees you.' And Ibn Abbas, may Allah be pleased with them both, said: 'With Our guarding.' The meaning is one. So He expressed seeing with 'eyes' because seeing is by them. And the plural of 'eye' is for glorification, not for multiplicity, as Allah said: 'How excellent are the capable!' and 'How excellent are the spreaders!' and 'And indeed We are the expanders.' And the meaning of 'eyes' in this verse and others may refer back to the meaning of 'eye' as He said: 'And that you be made under My eye.' All of that is an expression for perception and encompassment. And He, glorified and exalted, is transcendent above senses, anthropomorphism, and modality; there is no lord besides Him. And it was said: The meaning is 'with Our eyes' meaning with the eyes of Our angels whom We made as watchers over your preservation and assistance. So on this, the plural is for multiplicity in its literal sense. And it was said: 'with Our eyes' meaning with Our knowledge – said by Muqatil. And al-Dahhak and Sufyan said: 'with Our eyes' meaning with Our command. And it was said: with Our revelation. And it was said: with Our assistance to you in its construction. This is concerning the verse which the Shaykh used as evidence.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a literal anthropomorphic interpretation of the Quranic phrase 'bi a'yunina' (with Our eyes). The author begins by stating that neither the explicit wording nor the implied meaning of the verse supports a dual or literal sense of physical eyes. The verse uses the plural 'a'yun', not the dual, and asks rhetorically whether this implies more than two eyes for Allah, which is impossible. He then cites several authoritative Salaf interpretations to clarify the true meaning. Imam al-Qurtubi is quoted explaining that 'bi a'yunina' means under Allah's sight and observation, not literal eyes. Al-Rabi' ibn Anas interprets it as preservation, and Ibn Abbas as guarding. The author notes that the plural form is used for glorification (li al-'azama) rather than multiplicity, similar to other Quranic expressions like 'how excellent are the capable ones'. He further mentions alternative interpretations: the 'eyes' refer to angels, or to knowledge, command, revelation, or assistance. All these interpretations avoid attributing physical limbs to Allah. The passage concludes by affirming that this discussion concerns the verse the Shaykh used as evidence, thus setting the stage for refuting his anthropomorphic claims.

أعين
عين
الجمع للعظمة
التشبيه
التكييف
الرؤية
الإدراك
الإحاطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the plural 'a'yun' in 'bi a'yunina' proves Allah has multiple physical eyes.
Correction: The plural is for glorification, not multiplicity, and classical scholars interpret it as sight, preservation, or knowledge, never as literal eyes.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary reason the Quran uses the plural 'a'yun' in 'bi a'yunina'?
A) To indicate that Allah has more than two physical eyes.
B) To indicate multiplicity in the literal sense of many eyes.
C) For glorification (li al-'azama), not multiplicity.
D) To refer to the eyes of angels.

Key Takeaway
The Quranic phrase 'bi a'yunina' must be understood metaphorically as Allah's sight and care, not as literal eyes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read 'bi a'yunina' in the Quran, do you instinctively imagine a physical eye? What does this lesson reveal about the subtle danger of letting your mind reduce Allah to a limited form? Pause and ask yourself: have you ever corrected that thought, or do you still allow that image to linger without repentance?
?
The Salaf understood the verse as Allah's watchful care and preservation. If you truly believed that Allah's 'eyes' are His constant sight over you, how would it change the way you live tonight? What sin would you find harder to commit knowing His gaze is upon you—not as a threat, but as mercy? Commit to one action tonight that reflects this awareness.
?
The passage mentions that the plural is for glorification, not multiplicity. Do you read Allah's attributes with a sense of His majesty, or do you unconsciously strip them of their grandeur by imagining finite human qualities? Confess to yourself one moment today where you treated His attribute casually, and decide on a specific change in your worship to restore reverence for His names.

Refutation of Anthropomorphism Using the Dajjal Hadith
The author critiques the use of the Dajjal hadith to prove Allah has two eyes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the hadith of the Dajjal cannot be used as evidence for Allah having physical eyes, and the correct interpretation of the hadith.

Core Idea
The Dajjal hadith rebuts false divinity, it does not describe Allah.

This passage addresses a serious misunderstanding in theology—using a hadith about the Dajjal to prove that Allah has two real eyes. The author shows that the hadith's purpose is to expose the Dajjal's deficiency, not to affirm attributes of Allah. Let's examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the full translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the author's astonishment at the misuse of this hadith.
2
Understand the reasoning
The author rejects the claim by stating the hadith's wording does not support it. He quotes Ibn Hajar to show that the hadith is about Dajjal's deficiency, not about Allah's attributes. Identify the key points in Ibn Hajar's quote.
3
Reflect on the core principle
The hadith 'Allah is not one-eyed' negates the false divinity of Dajjal, not affirms physical eyes for Allah. This is a crucial distinction in Islamic theology: negating a false claim does not confirm the opposite physical attribute.
Apply this principle to avoid anthropomorphist interpretations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك منطوق الحديث السابق ليس فيه ما يدل مطلقا على ما قاله، والأغرب والأعجب من ذلك کله استدلاله بحديث الدّجّال على أن الله عينين حقيقيتين، مدعيًا أن الإجماع على أن هذه عقيدة السَّلف رضوان الله عليهم، ولم أجد أحدًا - ممن يعتمد قوله من السَّلف أو الخلف - استدل بمثل هذا الاستدلال الغريب، وأنقل لك كلام الحافظ ابن حجر في فتح الباري تعليقا على هذا حديث الدجال، قال ما صورته : إنما اقتصر على ذلك مع أن أدلة الحدوث في الدجال ظاهرة لكون العور أثر محسوس يدركه العالم والعامي ومن لا يهتدي إلى الأدلة العقلية، فإذا ادعى الربوبية وهو ناقص الخلقة والإله يتعالى عن النقص علم أنه كاذب» انتهى. فالنبي يصف الدجال حتى يعرفه الناس، ومن صفته أنه أعور العين اليمنى، وليس فيه دلالة على أن الله عينين، فأين وجه الدلالة في هذا الحديث؟ وأما قول النبي : إن الله ليس بأعور» ففيه نفي للألوهية المكذوبة والمزعومة التي يدعيها الدجّال لعنه الله ومن شايعه، وفيها كذلك نفي للنقص والعجر والعيب عن الله ، وليس إثباتًا للعينين اللتين هما جارحتان، تعالى الله عن ذلك.
Similarly, the explicit wording of the previous hadith does not contain anything that absolutely indicates what he said. And the strangest and most astonishing of all that is his use of the hadith of the Dajjal as evidence that Allah has two real eyes, claiming that there is consensus that this is the creed of the Salaf, may Allah be pleased with them. And I have not found anyone – among those whose words are relied upon from the Salaf or the Khalaf – who used such a strange evidence. And I quote for you the words of al-Hafiz Ibn Hajar in Fath al-Bari commenting on this hadith of the Dajjal. He said, its form: 'He only confined himself to that, even though the proofs of the Dajjal's temporality are apparent, because the one-eyedness is a perceptible effect that the scholar and the layman and those who cannot reach intellectual proofs can perceive. So if he claims divinity while he is deficient in creation, and the deity is exalted above deficiency, it is known that he is a liar.' End quote. So the Prophet describes the Dajjal so that people may know him, and among his attributes is that he is one-eyed in his right eye. There is no indication in that that Allah has two eyes. So where is the indication in this hadith? As for the Prophet's statement: 'Indeed Allah is not one-eyed' – in it there is a negation of the false and claimed divinity that the Dajjal (may Allah curse him and his followers) claims, and in it also a negation of deficiency, incapacity, and defect from Allah. It is not an affirmation of two eyes which are limbs. Exalted is Allah above that.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an anthropomorphic claim that uses the hadith of the Dajjal to prove that Allah has two real eyes. The author begins by stating that the explicit wording of the hadith does not support the claimant's assertion at all. He then expresses astonishment at the use of the Dajjal hadith for this purpose, noting that he has found no reliable Salaf or Khalaf scholar who used such a strange evidence. The author then quotes Ibn Hajar's commentary from Fath al-Bari, which explains that the mention of Dajjal's one-eye is to provide a perceptible proof of his temporality and false claim to divinity, since a deficient creation cannot be God. The author explains that the Prophet's description of Dajjal is to make him recognizable, and the statement 'Allah is not one-eyed' negates the false divinity claimed by Dajjal, not positively affirms physical eyes for Allah. The hadith is about refuting Dajjal's false claim, not about describing Allah's attributes. Thus, using it to prove Allah has two eyes is a distortion.

الحديث
الدجال
أعور
نفي النقص
إثبات العينين
الإجماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith 'Allah is not one-eyed' implies that Allah has two eyes.
Correction: The hadith is a polemical response to Dajjal's claim to divinity; it negates deficiency and false divinity, not affirms physical attributes for Allah.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject using the Dajjal hadith as evidence for Allah having two eyes?
A) Because the hadith is not authentic.
B) Because the hadith only describes Dajjal's deficiency, not Allah's attributes.
C) Because the hadith says Allah has one eye.
D) Because the Salaf never used this hadith for that purpose, as per Ibn Hajar.

Key Takeaway
The Dajjal hadith refutes the false claim of divinity by highlighting the Dajjal's deficiency, and cannot be twisted to affirm physical attributes for Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
When you hear a claim about Allah's attributes, do you reflexively accept it if it seems to magnify Allah, or do you check the evidence? Where might you be holding onto an incorrect belief about Allah?
?
This passage shows that even a famous hadith can be twisted. Are there beliefs you hold about Allah that you are afraid to re-examine because they seem 'obvious'? Are you willing to submit to the evidence, even if it challenges your comfort?

The Eternal Attribute of Hand (Yad)
Affirmation of yad as eternal attribute and interpretation as will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the attribute of hand (yad) is eternal, affirmed without modality or comparison, and that its intended meaning is Allah's will.

Core Idea
The hand of Allah is an eternal attribute, affirmed without how or likeness.

In this passage, the author establishes the hand (yad) as one of the eternal attributes, similar to hearing and sight. He clarifies that it should be affirmed without asking how or comparing to creation, and that the term 'hand' symbolically refers to Allah's will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Let the author's rhythm come through.
Notice the list of attributes.
2
Identify the eternal attributes
The author lists attributes like hearing, sight, knowledge, etc., and includes hand. Note that each is affirmed 'without limb'.
3
Understand the interpretation
The author explains that 'hand' here means Allah's will. This is a key point: the attribute is real but not literal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واليد من الصفات الأزلية بلا كيف ولا تشبيه، كالسمع والبصر، والعلم، والقدرة، والحياة، والإرادة، والكلام. فإن الله تعالى سميع بلا ،جارحة، بصير بلا عين عالم بلا آلة مريد بلا قلب متكلم بلا لسان وشفتين، وكذلك اليد من صفاته الأزلية بلا كيف وتشبيه وجارحة، فعبر باليد والمراد به إرادة الله تعالى. * وما قطع به الشيخ من تثنية العينين كيف يستقيم مع مثل قوله تعالى: ﴿وَلِتُصْنَعَ عَلَى عَيْنِي فقد وردت الآية بصيغة المفرد، فكيف يستقيم نص الآية مع فهم الشيخ ابن عثيمين؟ وهذا ينطبق على ما ورد في شأن اليد كذلك، فتارة يعبر القرءان عن اليد بصيغة المفرد كقوله: ﴿إِنَّ الَّذِينَ يُبَايِعُونَكَ إِنَّمَا يُبَايِعُونَ اللَّهَ يَدُ اللَّهِ فَوقَ أَيْدِيهِم وتارة بصيغة التثنية كقوله: ﴿بَلْ يَدَاهُ مَبْسُوطَتَانِ يُنفِقُ كَيْفَ يَشَاءُ وتارةً بصيغة الجمع كقوله: ﴿ وَالسَّمَاءَ بَنَيْنَهَا بِأَيْيْدِ وَإِنَّا لَمُوسِعُونَ ﴾ وقوله: ﴿أَوَلَمْ يَرَوْا أَنَّا خَلَقْنَا لَهُم مِّمَّا عَمِلَتْ أَيْدِينَا أَنْعَمَّا فَهُمْ لَهَا مَلِكُونَ ﴾ فعلى ما يقول به ابن عثيمين ومن ينحو هذا المنحى سيقع التضارب في صفات الله تعالى، والله تعالى منزّه عن الصور والأشكال والحوادث والأعراض، تعالى الله عما يقول الظالمون علوا كبيرًا. كذلك قول أحدهم إن الله يدا أو رجلاً ليست كأيدينا، معتقدا أنه بذلك قد نزّه المولى جل في علاه عن التجسم فهذا أيضًا غير صحيح، لأن نفي التماثل لا يعني بالضرورة نفي الجسمية، وهو بذلك لم يغلق باب التصورات العقلية الوهمية لذات الله تعالى، بل فتح بابًا لتسلّط الشيطان على عقله وقلبه، فقد نفى تماثل الكيفيات ولم ينف الكيفية أصلاً، والله تعالى منزّه عن الكيف، فقد يأتيه الشيطان ليقول له: إن لم تكن يد الله ليست كيدك، فكيف هي إذن؟ كما في الحديث المتفق عليه من حديث أبي هريرة رضي الله عنه عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم: «يأتي الشيطانُ أحدكم فيقول : من خلق كذا؟ من خلق كذا ؟ حتى يقول : من خلق الله ؟ فإذا بلغه فليقل: آمنت بالله ورسله فمثل هذه الأقوال الملبسة لا تغلق هذا الباب، بل تفتح باب التصورات العقلية الباطلة لذات الله تعالى، والواجب الانتهاء عن ذلك والوقوف مع النصوص التي وردت عن الله تعالى وعن رسوله ، دون تورط في أقوال تفضي إلى التجسيم والتشبيه، والله أعلى وأعلم.
And the hand (yad) is among the eternal attributes (sifat al-azaliyya) without modality (kayf) and without comparison (tashbih), like hearing (sam'), sight (basar), knowledge ('ilm), power (qudra), life (haya), will (irada), and speech (kalam). For indeed Allah, exalted, is Hearing without a limb (jarhah), Seeing without an eye, Knowing without an instrument, Willing without a heart, Speaking without a tongue and lips. Likewise, the hand (yad) is among His eternal attributes without modality, comparison, and limb. So the expression 'hand' is used, and what is meant by it is the will (irada) of Allah, exalted. * And how can what the Shaykh firmly asserted regarding the duality of the two eyes be consistent with such words of Allah, exalted: 'And that you be made under My eye' (20:39), while the verse came in the singular form? So how can the text of the verse be consistent with the understanding of Shaykh Ibn 'Uthaymin? And the same applies to what has come concerning the hand. Sometimes the Quran expresses the hand in the singular form, as in His saying: 'Indeed those who pledge allegiance to you are only pledging allegiance to Allah; the hand of Allah is above their hands' (48:10); sometimes in the dual form, as in His saying: 'Nay, both His hands are outstretched; He spends how He wills' (5:64); and sometimes in the plural form, as in His saying: 'And the heaven We built it with hands (ayd)', (51:47) and His saying: 'Do they not see that We have created for them from what Our hands (aydina) have made, cattle, so they are their owners?' (36:71). So according to what Ibn 'Uthaymin and those who follow this path say, contradiction will occur in the attributes of Allah, exalted. And Allah, exalted, is transcendent (munazzah) above images, forms, temporal events (hawadith), and accidents (a'rad). Exalted is Allah above what the wrongdoers say, a great exaltation. Similarly, the statement of one of them that 'Allah has a hand or a foot, but not like our hands and feet,' thinking that by this he has declared the Lord, glorified and exalted, transcendent above corporealism (tajassum) – this is also incorrect, because the negation of similarity (tamathul) does not necessarily imply the negation of corporeality (jismiyya). Thus he has not closed the door of mental and imaginary conceptions of the essence of Allah, exalted, but rather opened a door for the domination of Satan over his mind and heart. For he negated the similarity of modalities (kayfiyyat) but did not negate modality (kayfiyya) itself originally, while Allah, exalted, is transcendent above modality. So Satan may come to him and say: 'If the hand of Allah is not like your hand, then how is it?' As in the agreed-upon hadith from Abu Hurayra, may Allah be pleased with him, from the Prophet, peace be upon him: 'Satan comes to one of you and says: Who created such-and-such? Who created such-and-such? Until he says: Who created Allah? When it reaches that, let him say: I believe in Allah and His messengers.' Such confusing statements do not close this door, but rather open the door of false mental conceptions of the essence of Allah, exalted. And what is obligatory is to refrain from that and to adhere to the texts that have come from Allah, exalted, and from His Messenger, without becoming entangled in statements that lead to corporealism (tajsim) and anthropomorphism (tashbih). And Allah is higher and more knowing.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage is a theological exposition on the divine attribute of 'yad' (hand) and a refutation of anthropomorphic interpretations. The author begins by affirming that the hand is an eternal attribute (sifa azaliyya) like hearing, sight, knowledge, etc., to be affirmed without modality (kayf) or comparison (tashbih). He emphasizes that Allah is Hearing without a limb, Seeing without an eye, etc., and likewise the hand is an attribute without limb or modality, interpreting it as referring to Allah's will (irada). Then he criticizes the inconsistency of Shaykh Ibn 'Uthaymin's literalist approach: if one insists that 'eye' is dual in some verses, what about the singular 'ayni' in 'ala 'ayni'? The same issue arises with 'hand' appearing in singular, dual, and plural forms in the Quran. Following a literalistic method would lead to contradictions in the divine attributes. The author then refutes a common apologetic: saying 'Allah has a hand but not like ours' does not adequately safeguard transcendence. Negating similarity (tamathul) does not negate corporeality (jismiyya); it still allows the mind to conceive of a body, just with different properties. This opens the door for satanic whisperings asking 'how is it?' He invokes the hadith about Satan questioning creation, advising to simply say 'I believe in Allah and His messengers' rather than engage in speculative theology that leads to false images. The correct approach is to stop at the texts without delving into anthropomorphic interpretations.

Key Terms

الصفات الأزلية
الكيف
التشبيه
الإرادة

Checkpoint

D) Allah's knowledge

Key Takeaway
The hand of Allah is an eternal attribute affirmed without modality, symbolizing His will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Quranic Usage of Hand: Singular, Dual, Plural
Critique of literalist interpretation using Quranic examples of hand in singular/dual/plural.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a literalist interpretation of anthropomorphic expressions leads to contradiction, as seen in the Quran's use of singular, dual, and plural forms.

Core Idea
The Quran's varied expressions for hand show that a literal reading causes contradictions.

The author highlights the inconsistency of a literalist approach by pointing out that the Quran uses the word 'hand' in singular, dual, and plural forms. If taken literally, this would imply multiple hands or contradictions, which is impossible for Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage showing the different forms
Read the Arabic passage that cites Quranic verses with singular, dual, and plural forms of 'hand'. Note the eye example as well.
2
Identify the contradiction
The author argues that a literalist reading would lead to contradictions because the same attribute appears in different numeric forms.
The challenge is to Ibn 'Uthaymin's position.
3
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that Allah is transcendent above forms and accidents, so the expressions must be interpreted metaphorically.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واليد من الصفات الأزلية بلا كيف ولا تشبيه، كالسمع والبصر، والعلم، والقدرة، والحياة، والإرادة، والكلام. فإن الله تعالى سميع بلا ،جارحة، بصير بلا عين عالم بلا آلة مريد بلا قلب متكلم بلا لسان وشفتين، وكذلك اليد من صفاته الأزلية بلا كيف وتشبيه وجارحة، فعبر باليد والمراد به إرادة الله تعالى. * وما قطع به الشيخ من تثنية العينين كيف يستقيم مع مثل قوله تعالى: ﴿وَلِتُصْنَعَ عَلَى عَيْنِي فقد وردت الآية بصيغة المفرد، فكيف يستقيم نص الآية مع فهم الشيخ ابن عثيمين؟ وهذا ينطبق على ما ورد في شأن اليد كذلك، فتارة يعبر القرءان عن اليد بصيغة المفرد كقوله: ﴿إِنَّ الَّذِينَ يُبَايِعُونَكَ إِنَّمَا يُبَايِعُونَ اللَّهَ يَدُ اللَّهِ فَوقَ أَيْدِيهِم وتارة بصيغة التثنية كقوله: ﴿بَلْ يَدَاهُ مَبْسُوطَتَانِ يُنفِقُ كَيْفَ يَشَاءُ وتارةً بصيغة الجمع كقوله: ﴿ وَالسَّمَاءَ بَنَيْنَهَا بِأَيْيْدِ وَإِنَّا لَمُوسِعُونَ ﴾ وقوله: ﴿أَوَلَمْ يَرَوْا أَنَّا خَلَقْنَا لَهُم مِّمَّا عَمِلَتْ أَيْدِينَا أَنْعَمَّا فَهُمْ لَهَا مَلِكُونَ ﴾ فعلى ما يقول به ابن عثيمين ومن ينحو هذا المنحى سيقع التضارب في صفات الله تعالى، والله تعالى منزّه عن الصور والأشكال والحوادث والأعراض، تعالى الله عما يقول الظالمون علوا كبيرًا. كذلك قول أحدهم إن الله يدا أو رجلاً ليست كأيدينا، معتقدا أنه بذلك قد نزّه المولى جل في علاه عن التجسم فهذا أيضًا غير صحيح، لأن نفي التماثل لا يعني بالضرورة نفي الجسمية، وهو بذلك لم يغلق باب التصورات العقلية الوهمية لذات الله تعالى، بل فتح بابًا لتسلّط الشيطان على عقله وقلبه، فقد نفى تماثل الكيفيات ولم ينف الكيفية أصلاً، والله تعالى منزّه عن الكيف، فقد يأتيه الشيطان ليقول له: إن لم تكن يد الله ليست كيدك، فكيف هي إذن؟ كما في الحديث المتفق عليه من حديث أبي هريرة رضي الله عنه عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم: «يأتي الشيطانُ أحدكم فيقول : من خلق كذا؟ من خلق كذا ؟ حتى يقول : من خلق الله ؟ فإذا بلغه فليقل: آمنت بالله ورسله فمثل هذه الأقوال الملبسة لا تغلق هذا الباب، بل تفتح باب التصورات العقلية الباطلة لذات الله تعالى، والواجب الانتهاء عن ذلك والوقوف مع النصوص التي وردت عن الله تعالى وعن رسوله ، دون تورط في أقوال تفضي إلى التجسيم والتشبيه، والله أعلى وأعلم.
And the hand (yad) is among the eternal attributes (sifat al-azaliyya) without modality (kayf) and without comparison (tashbih), like hearing (sam'), sight (basar), knowledge ('ilm), power (qudra), life (haya), will (irada), and speech (kalam). For indeed Allah, exalted, is Hearing without a limb (jarhah), Seeing without an eye, Knowing without an instrument, Willing without a heart, Speaking without a tongue and lips. Likewise, the hand (yad) is among His eternal attributes without modality, comparison, and limb. So the expression 'hand' is used, and what is meant by it is the will (irada) of Allah, exalted. * And how can what the Shaykh firmly asserted regarding the duality of the two eyes be consistent with such words of Allah, exalted: 'And that you be made under My eye' (20:39), while the verse came in the singular form? So how can the text of the verse be consistent with the understanding of Shaykh Ibn 'Uthaymin? And the same applies to what has come concerning the hand. Sometimes the Quran expresses the hand in the singular form, as in His saying: 'Indeed those who pledge allegiance to you are only pledging allegiance to Allah; the hand of Allah is above their hands' (48:10); sometimes in the dual form, as in His saying: 'Nay, both His hands are outstretched; He spends how He wills' (5:64); and sometimes in the plural form, as in His saying: 'And the heaven We built it with hands (ayd)', (51:47) and His saying: 'Do they not see that We have created for them from what Our hands (aydina) have made, cattle, so they are their owners?' (36:71). So according to what Ibn 'Uthaymin and those who follow this path say, contradiction will occur in the attributes of Allah, exalted. And Allah, exalted, is transcendent (munazzah) above images, forms, temporal events (hawadith), and accidents (a'rad). Exalted is Allah above what the wrongdoers say, a great exaltation. Similarly, the statement of one of them that 'Allah has a hand or a foot, but not like our hands and feet,' thinking that by this he has declared the Lord, glorified and exalted, transcendent above corporealism (tajassum) – this is also incorrect, because the negation of similarity (tamathul) does not necessarily imply the negation of corporeality (jismiyya). Thus he has not closed the door of mental and imaginary conceptions of the essence of Allah, exalted, but rather opened a door for the domination of Satan over his mind and heart. For he negated the similarity of modalities (kayfiyyat) but did not negate modality (kayfiyya) itself originally, while Allah, exalted, is transcendent above modality. So Satan may come to him and say: 'If the hand of Allah is not like your hand, then how is it?' As in the agreed-upon hadith from Abu Hurayra, may Allah be pleased with him, from the Prophet, peace be upon him: 'Satan comes to one of you and says: Who created such-and-such? Who created such-and-such? Until he says: Who created Allah? When it reaches that, let him say: I believe in Allah and His messengers.' Such confusing statements do not close this door, but rather open the door of false mental conceptions of the essence of Allah, exalted. And what is obligatory is to refrain from that and to adhere to the texts that have come from Allah, exalted, and from His Messenger, without becoming entangled in statements that lead to corporealism (tajsim) and anthropomorphism (tashbih). And Allah is higher and more knowing.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage is a theological exposition on the divine attribute of 'yad' (hand) and a refutation of anthropomorphic interpretations. The author begins by affirming that the hand is an eternal attribute (sifa azaliyya) like hearing, sight, knowledge, etc., to be affirmed without modality (kayf) or comparison (tashbih). He emphasizes that Allah is Hearing without a limb, Seeing without an eye, etc., and likewise the hand is an attribute without limb or modality, interpreting it as referring to Allah's will (irada). Then he criticizes the inconsistency of Shaykh Ibn 'Uthaymin's literalist approach: if one insists that 'eye' is dual in some verses, what about the singular 'ayni' in 'ala 'ayni'? The same issue arises with 'hand' appearing in singular, dual, and plural forms in the Quran. Following a literalistic method would lead to contradictions in the divine attributes. The author then refutes a common apologetic: saying 'Allah has a hand but not like ours' does not adequately safeguard transcendence. Negating similarity (tamathul) does not negate corporeality (jismiyya); it still allows the mind to conceive of a body, just with different properties. This opens the door for satanic whisperings asking 'how is it?' He invokes the hadith about Satan questioning creation, advising to simply say 'I believe in Allah and His messengers' rather than engage in speculative theology that leads to false images. The correct approach is to stop at the texts without delving into anthropomorphic interpretations.

The Insufficiency of 'Not Like Ours' in Avoiding Anthropomorphism
Refutation of the idea that 'hand but not like ours' suffices, and emphasis on refraining from speculation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why saying 'Allah's hand is not like our hands' is insufficient to negate corporealism, and why the proper approach is to stop at the texts without speculation.

Core Idea
Negating similarity does not eliminate the mental conception of a physical body.

The author refutes a common argument that by saying 'Allah has a hand but not like ours,' one has safeguarded transcendence. He explains that this still allows the mind to imagine a physical hand, just with different properties, and thus opens the door to satanic whisperings. The correct response is to refrain from such speculations and adhere to the texts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage refuting the 'not like ours' statement
Read the Arabic passage that directly addresses this apologetic.
2
Understand the distinction
The author distinguishes between negating similarity (tamathul) and negating corporeality (jismiyya).
Negating tamathul does not negate jismiyya.
3
Connect to the hadith
The author invokes the hadith about Satan's whisperings to show why such statements are dangerous.
4
Learn the proper approach
The correct response is to say 'I believe in Allah and His messengers' and refrain from speculative theology.

واليد من الصفات الأزلية بلا كيف ولا تشبيه، كالسمع والبصر، والعلم، والقدرة، والحياة، والإرادة، والكلام. فإن الله تعالى سميع بلا ،جارحة، بصير بلا عين عالم بلا آلة مريد بلا قلب متكلم بلا لسان وشفتين، وكذلك اليد من صفاته الأزلية بلا كيف وتشبيه وجارحة، فعبر باليد والمراد به إرادة الله تعالى. * وما قطع به الشيخ من تثنية العينين كيف يستقيم مع مثل قوله تعالى: ﴿وَلِتُصْنَعَ عَلَى عَيْنِي فقد وردت الآية بصيغة المفرد، فكيف يستقيم نص الآية مع فهم الشيخ ابن عثيمين؟ وهذا ينطبق على ما ورد في شأن اليد كذلك، فتارة يعبر القرءان عن اليد بصيغة المفرد كقوله: ﴿إِنَّ الَّذِينَ يُبَايِعُونَكَ إِنَّمَا يُبَايِعُونَ اللَّهَ يَدُ اللَّهِ فَوقَ أَيْدِيهِم وتارة بصيغة التثنية كقوله: ﴿بَلْ يَدَاهُ مَبْسُوطَتَانِ يُنفِقُ كَيْفَ يَشَاءُ وتارةً بصيغة الجمع كقوله: ﴿ وَالسَّمَاءَ بَنَيْنَهَا بِأَيْيْدِ وَإِنَّا لَمُوسِعُونَ ﴾ وقوله: ﴿أَوَلَمْ يَرَوْا أَنَّا خَلَقْنَا لَهُم مِّمَّا عَمِلَتْ أَيْدِينَا أَنْعَمَّا فَهُمْ لَهَا مَلِكُونَ ﴾ فعلى ما يقول به ابن عثيمين ومن ينحو هذا المنحى سيقع التضارب في صفات الله تعالى، والله تعالى منزّه عن الصور والأشكال والحوادث والأعراض، تعالى الله عما يقول الظالمون علوا كبيرًا. كذلك قول أحدهم إن الله يدا أو رجلاً ليست كأيدينا، معتقدا أنه بذلك قد نزّه المولى جل في علاه عن التجسم فهذا أيضًا غير صحيح، لأن نفي التماثل لا يعني بالضرورة نفي الجسمية، وهو بذلك لم يغلق باب التصورات العقلية الوهمية لذات الله تعالى، بل فتح بابًا لتسلّط الشيطان على عقله وقلبه، فقد نفى تماثل الكيفيات ولم ينف الكيفية أصلاً، والله تعالى منزّه عن الكيف، فقد يأتيه الشيطان ليقول له: إن لم تكن يد الله ليست كيدك، فكيف هي إذن؟ كما في الحديث المتفق عليه من حديث أبي هريرة رضي الله عنه عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم: «يأتي الشيطانُ أحدكم فيقول : من خلق كذا؟ من خلق كذا ؟ حتى يقول : من خلق الله ؟ فإذا بلغه فليقل: آمنت بالله ورسله فمثل هذه الأقوال الملبسة لا تغلق هذا الباب، بل تفتح باب التصورات العقلية الباطلة لذات الله تعالى، والواجب الانتهاء عن ذلك والوقوف مع النصوص التي وردت عن الله تعالى وعن رسوله ، دون تورط في أقوال تفضي إلى التجسيم والتشبيه، والله أعلى وأعلم.
And the hand (yad) is among the eternal attributes (sifat al-azaliyya) without modality (kayf) and without comparison (tashbih), like hearing (sam'), sight (basar), knowledge ('ilm), power (qudra), life (haya), will (irada), and speech (kalam). For indeed Allah, exalted, is Hearing without a limb (jarhah), Seeing without an eye, Knowing without an instrument, Willing without a heart, Speaking without a tongue and lips. Likewise, the hand (yad) is among His eternal attributes without modality, comparison, and limb. So the expression 'hand' is used, and what is meant by it is the will (irada) of Allah, exalted. * And how can what the Shaykh firmly asserted regarding the duality of the two eyes be consistent with such words of Allah, exalted: 'And that you be made under My eye' (20:39), while the verse came in the singular form? So how can the text of the verse be consistent with the understanding of Shaykh Ibn 'Uthaymin? And the same applies to what has come concerning the hand. Sometimes the Quran expresses the hand in the singular form, as in His saying: 'Indeed those who pledge allegiance to you are only pledging allegiance to Allah; the hand of Allah is above their hands' (48:10); sometimes in the dual form, as in His saying: 'Nay, both His hands are outstretched; He spends how He wills' (5:64); and sometimes in the plural form, as in His saying: 'And the heaven We built it with hands (ayd)', (51:47) and His saying: 'Do they not see that We have created for them from what Our hands (aydina) have made, cattle, so they are their owners?'
