Quranic Proof for Belief in All Books and Messengers
Nasafi cites Quranic proofs that belief in all books and messengers is obligatory

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Quran requires believers to affirm all divine books and all prophets as a condition of faith.

Core Idea
Belief in all books and messengers is not optional—it is a condition.

This passage presents two Quranic proofs that establish the obligation to believe in all of Allah's books and all of His messengers. Nasafi uses direct verses to show that faith is not selective; it requires comprehensive affirmation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two conditions
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, noting that it states two separate conditions: belief in all books and belief in all messengers.
The passage is short but powerful.
2
Examine the first verse (2:136)
Look at the verse: 'Say: We believe in Allah and what has been sent down to us.' This commands belief in all revelations, not just some.
The phrase 'what has been sent down to us' implies all divine books.
3
Examine the second verse (2:177)
The verse lists belief in the Book and the prophets as components of righteousness, alongside belief in Allah and the Last Day.
This shows that believing in all prophets is part of true faith.
4
Reflect on the necessity of comprehensive belief
Ask yourself: why does faith require believing in all books and messengers? Consider that rejecting one is like rejecting all.
The Quran emphasizes unity of the message.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والدليل على أن الإيمان بجميع الكتب شرط قال الله تعالى: ﴿ قُولُوا ءَامَنَا بِاللَّهِ وَمَا أُنزِلَ إِلَيْنَا ﴾ [البقرة: ١٣٦] الآية والإيمان بجميع الرسل شرط، قال الله تعالى: ﴿وَلَكِنَّ البِرَّ مَنْ ءَامَنَ بِاللَّهِ وَالْيَوْمِ الْآخِرِ وَالْمَلَائِكَةِ وَالْكِتَابِ وَالنَّبِيِّينَ ﴾ [البقرة: ١٧٧].

And belief in all messengers is a condition: Allah said: 'But righteousness is [he] who believes in Allah, the Last Day, the angels, the Book, and the prophets' [2:177].

Commentary
This passage presents Quranic evidence that faith requires believing in all of Allah's books and messengers. The author cites two verses: the first (2:136) commands believers to affirm belief in what was revealed to them, which implicitly includes all divine books; the second (2:177) lists belief in the angels, the Book, and the prophets alongside Allah and the Last Day as a component of true righteousness. Thus the obligatory nature of believing in all books and all messengers is established. The structure is straightforward: proof followed by the condition (shart).

Wine Forbidden by Revelation and Response to Objection
Imam al-Nasafi argues for wine prohibition and answers an objection about timing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scriptural evidence for the prohibition of wine and how a potential objection about the timing of the revelation is answered.

Core Idea
Wine is forbidden by explicit prophetic report and Quranic identification of sin as wine.

This passage from Imam al-Nasafi presents two key issues: the scriptural basis for the prohibition of wine and a response to an objection concerning a verse that seems to excuse past consumption. Additionally, a brief ruling on beating the drum in weddings is given. We'll explore how the author uses hadith, Quran, and poetry to establish the ruling, and then addresses the historical context of the revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get a sense of the flow. Notice the sequence: a Quranic verse, then the claim that wine is forbidden, followed by evidence, an objection, and a brief ruling on drums.
The passage starts with a verse about creation, which sets the context of accountability.
2
Identify the evidence for prohibition
Find the hadith that prohibits wine (including its little and much) and the Quranic verse that lists 'sin' (ithm) as forbidden. Note how the author equates ithm with wine using a line of poetry.
The evidence is both from the Prophet's statement and the Quran.
3
Understand the objection and its answer
Some argued that the prohibition verse (Al-A'raf 33) was revealed after people had drunk wine, implying it was not yet forbidden. The author explains that verse was a comfort for those who drank unknowingly after the prohibition was revealed but before they learned of it.
This shows that rulings apply only after knowledge reaches the people.
4
Note the ruling on drum beating
The author mentions that beating the drum is permitted by Imam al-Shafi'i in weddings for announcement, not for entertainment. This is a separate point from the main argument.
This illustrates how permissibility is context-dependent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعالى: (أَفَحَسِبْتُمْ أَنَّمَا خَلَقْنَاكُمْ عَبَثًا وَأَنَّكُمْ إِلَيْنَا لَا تُرْجَعُونَ) [المؤمنون: 115]. وأما قولنا: الخمر حرام؛ لأنه ورد بها الخبر، وهو قوله : الحرمت عليكم الخمر قليلها وكثيرها والسكر من كل شراب. وقال الله تعالى: ﴿ قُلْ إِنَّمَا حَرَّمَ رَبِّيَ الْفَوَاحِشَ مَا ظَهَرَ مِنْهَا وَمَا بَطَنَ وَالإِثْمَ وَالْبَغْيَ بِغَيْرِ الْحَقِّ وَأَن تُشْرِكُوا بِاللَّهِ مَا لَمْ يُنزِلْ بِهِ سُلْطَانًا وَأَن تَقُولُوا عَلَى اللَّهِ مَا لَا تَعْلَمُونَ ﴾ [الأعراف: 33] والإثم هو الخمر، يدل عليه قول القائل: شربت الخمر حتى ضل عقلي كذاك الإثم تذهب بالعقول. والجواب عن احتجاجهم بالآية، قلنا: الآية نزلت في قوم شربوا الخمر بعد نزول آية التحريم قبل بلوغ الخبر إليهم، فاغتموا بذلك فأنزل الله تعالى هذه الآية. وأما ضرب الدف، قلنا: أباحه الإمام الشافعي رضي الله عنه في التزويج للإعلام لا للعب.
Exalted is He: 'Did you think that We created you in play and that you would not be returned to Us?' [Al-Mu'minun: 115]. As for our statement: Wine is forbidden because the report came concerning it, and it is his saying: 'Wine is forbidden to you, its little and its much, and the intoxicant from every drink.' And Allah the Exalted said: 'Say: My Lord has only forbidden immoralities – what is apparent of them and what is concealed – and sin, and oppression without right, and that you associate with Allah that for which He has not sent down authority, and that you say about Allah that which you do not know' [Al-A'raf: 33]. And 'sin' (al-ithm) is wine, as indicated by the saying of the poet: 'I drank wine until my mind strayed; thus sin (al-ithm) takes away intellects.' And the answer to their argument by the verse: We say: The verse was revealed concerning a people who drank wine after the revelation of the verse of prohibition before the news reached them, and they were distressed by that, so Allah the Exalted revealed this verse. And as for beating the drum, we say: Imam al-Shafi'i, may Allah be pleased with him, permitted it in marriage for announcement, not for play.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument defending the prohibition of wine by revelation and responding to an objection about the timing of a Quranic verse. The author begins by citing a Quranic verse (Al-Mu'minun 115) to establish the purpose of creation, then states that wine is forbidden because a prophetic report (hadith) has come regarding it, quoting the Prophet's prohibition of wine and intoxicants. He then cites another verse (Al-A'raf 33) where Allah prohibits sins, and identifies 'sin' (ithm) as wine, supporting this with a line of poetry. The author then addresses an objection: some argued that the prohibition verse was revealed after people had already drunk wine, implying that it was not yet forbidden. He answers that the verse Abu 'l-Laith al-Samarqandi? actually pertains to those who drank after the prohibition was revealed but before they learned of it, and that the verse was sent down to relieve their distress. Finally, he discusses the ruling on beating the drum (duff), stating that Imam al-Shafi'i permitted it in weddings for announcement, not for amusement. The passage demonstrates how Islamic law relies on scripture and the principle that rulings apply only after knowledge of them reaches the people.

Key Terms
الخمر
الإثم
التحريم
الخبر
ضرب الدف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the verse (Al-A'raf 33) was revealed to abrogate the permissibility of wine, indicating that the prohibition of wine came later and thus those who drank earlier were not sinful.
Correction: The author clarifies that the verse was a comfort to those who drank after the prohibition was revealed but before they heard about it, so the ruling was already in place; ignorance of the prohibition excuses sin.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary evidence that wine is forbidden?
A) The poet's saying
B) The prophetic report (hadith) and the Quranic verse
C) The consensus of scholars
D) The analogy with drums

Key Takeaway
The prohibition of wine is established by clear revelation, and rulings apply only after they are known; ignorance of the ruling can excuse past actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have heard the prohibition of wine and intoxicants. But what about the 'small amounts' mentioned in the hadith? Are you quick to rationalize or compromise on what you know is forbidden, even in matters less public than alcohol? How does this affect your soul's accountability?
?
The verse was a comfort for those who acted before they knew. Is there an area of your life where you are relying on ignorance or lack of clear knowledge to continue a sin? What would you do if you received a clear text about it tomorrow?
?
Wine is equated with 'ithm' (sin). Reflect on any action in your life that you know leads to loss of reason or moral clarity. Are you treating it as a minor matter, while Allah calls it sin? What must you change today?

Divine Attributes
intermediate
6 min
Defending Abrogation Against the Badā' Objection
Imam al-Nasafi refutes the claim that abrogation implies badā' (change of mind) in Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Islamic scholars refute the claim that abrogation implies a change of mind (badā') in Allah, using the concept of temporary rulings and the analogy of resurrection.

Core Idea
Abrogation is not a change of mind but the end of a temporary ruling.

This lesson addresses a critical theological objection: if Allah can abrogate a previously permitted ruling, does that mean He changes His mind? The objection seems logical, but the answer reveals a deeper understanding of divine decree. Imam al-Nasafi shows that abrogation simply means a preordained temporary ruling has reached its term, just as the resurrection ends the state of death.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the objector's premise: abrogation would mean Allah 'returns' from His first command, implying ignorance.
2
Identify the key claim in the objection
The objector says badā' and return are only from one who is ignorant of outcomes.
3
Read the answer: abrogation as expiration
The author rejects that abrogation involves badā'; rather, it is the expiration of a temporary ruling that was always limited in time.
The key term is 'munqadā' – expiration.
4
Follow the analogy of resurrection
Allah will resurrect the dead; this is not called badā' but the end of death and start of new life. Similarly for abrogation.
5
Reflect on why this matters
This clarifies that Allah's knowledge and will are unchanged; He decrees temporary rulings from eternity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: إباحة الخمر والمتعة كانت حلالاً في الابتداء، فلو قلنا بجواز النسخ يكون ذلك رجوعًا عن الأول، ويصير كأن الله تعالى أمر بأمر، ثم بدا له الرجوع عن ذلك، والبداء والرجوع من الله تعالى لا يصح؛ لأن البداء والرجوع إنما يكون ممن كان جاهلاً ولا يعرف عواقب الأمور. والجواب عنه أن نقول: لا نسلم بأن في النسخ بداءً ورجوعًا، بل هو انقضاء حكم أول وانتهاؤه، واستئناف حكم آخر؛ لأنه قد ظهر لنا أن الحكم الأول [قد انتهى] لم يكن مؤبدًا، لكنه مؤقت إلى ذلك الوقت، إلا أنا لا نعرف ذلك، فظهر لنا أن الحكم الأول قد انتهى وانقضى. يدل عليه أن الله تعالى يحشر الموتى يوم القيامة، ولا يقال بأن فيه بداءً ورجوعًا، بل فيه انتهاء حكم الموتى، ويستأنف حكمًا آخر، كذلك ههنا ولا يقال بأن في النسخ بداءً ورجوعاً، بل فيه انتهاء حكم المنسوخ في الموتى، واستئناف حكم الناسخ.
For if it is said: The permissibility of wine and temporary marriage (mut'ah) were lawful in the beginning, and if we say that abrogation (naskh) is permissible, that would be a return from the first, and it would become as if Allah the Exalted commanded a command, then He changed His mind about that, and badā' (change of mind) and return from Allah the Exalted are not valid because badā' and return are only from one who is ignorant and does not know the outcomes of affairs. The answer to that is that we say: We do not concede that in abrogation there is badā' and return; rather, it is the expiration of a first ruling and its termination, and the initiation of another ruling. Because it became apparent to us that the first ruling [has ended] was not eternal, but was temporary until that time, except that we did not know that. So it became apparent to us that the first ruling has ended and terminated. Evidence for that is that Allah the Exalted will resurrect the dead on the Day of Resurrection, and it is not said that in that there is badā' and return. Rather, there is the termination of the ruling of the dead, and He initiates another ruling. Similarly here: It is not said that in abrogation there is badā' and return; rather, there is the termination of the abrogated ruling in the dead, and the initiation of the abrogating ruling.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer concerning the Islamic doctrine of abrogation (naskh). The objector claims that if we accept that Allah can abrogate a previously permitted ruling (like wine and temporary marriage), this would imply that Allah changes His mind (badā'), which is impossible because change of mind occurs only from ignorance of future outcomes. The author responds by rejecting that abrogation involves badā'. He explains that abrogation is simply the expiration of a temporary ruling that was always intended to end at a specific time, even though humans were not aware of its time limit. When the appointed time arrives, the ruling ends and a new one begins. To illustrate, he gives the analogy of the resurrection: Allah will resurrect the dead on the Day of Judgment; this is not considered a change of mind but the end of the state of death and the beginning of new life. Likewise, abrogation is the end of a temporary ruling and the start of another. The author emphasizes that no one accuses Allah of badā' in resurrection, so the same applies to abrogation.

Key Terms
النسخ
البداء
الرجوع
المؤقت
الحكم الأول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Abrogation implies that Allah changed His mind (badā'), which would be a flaw.
Correction: Abrogation is simply the end of a pre-ordained temporary ruling; Allah always knew the time limit and intended the new ruling from eternity.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does abrogation represent instead of badā'?
A) A contradiction in divine commands
B) The expiration of a temporary ruling and initiation of a new one
C) An error in the original ruling
D) A concession to human weakness

Key Takeaway
Abrogation does not imply a change in Allah's will; it is simply the expiration of a temporary ruling and the start of a new one, as with the resurrection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation where a religious ruling seems to have changed, do you trust that Allah's wisdom is perfect, or do you secretly doubt His consistency? How does this lesson on abrogation as expiration of a temporary ruling challenge that doubt?
?
Have you ever felt that Allah 'changed His mind' about something in your life—like a prayer answered differently than expected? How does the distinction between badā' and the expiration of a temporary decree correct that thought?
?
If you truly believed that every apparent change in your life is not a sign of Allah's uncertainty but the end of a preordained temporary phase, how would that transform your patience and hope in trials?
